CREDIT AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:            Republic of The Gambia
Beneficiaries:       The President's Office (Women's Bureau) and Ministries
of Agriculture; Local Government and Lands; Education,
Youth, Sports and Ctlture; Health, Environment, Labor
and Social Welfare; and Information and Tourism
Credit Amount:       SDR 5.4 million (US$7.0 million equivalent)
Terms:               Standard, with 40 years' maturity
Project              The project aims to: (a) improve women's productivity
Obiectives:          and income-earning potential; (b) improve uomen's
welfare and status; (c) strengthen government
institutions to enable them to better deal with
women's issues; and (d) contribute to bringing about a
change in Gambian society's perception of the role of
women.
Project              The project comprises the following six components:
Description:
(a) Increasing women's agricultural productivity and
disposable income and improving the nutritional level
of women and children through: the strengthening of
agricultural extension services to better serve women;
private sector input distribution; support for women's
cooperative groups; strengthening of food storage,
preservation and nutrition extension; improved
management of small-scale post-harvest equipment; and
a savings mobilization campaign directed at women.
(b)  Improving the skills of rural women end promoting
viable and marketable skills, organizing and
strengthening women's groups in rural areas for small
business and income- generating activities, and
promoting basic literacy and numeracy skills through
training activities related to women's social and
economic needs.
(c)  Promoting safe motherhood and strengthening family
planning services through: training of village-level
health workers and traditional birth attendants in
early identification and management of high-risk
pregnancies; development of an *alarm" and transport



- ii -
system at the community level to handle medical
emergencies in time; a nutrition education and micro-
nutrient supplementation program; and expansion of the
community-based contraceptive distribution system.
(d)  Developing an information, education and communication
(IEC) strategy, with emphasis on specific needs and
concerns of women, and reinforcing ongoing educational
and motivational campaigns on agriculture, health,
family planning, and nutrition.
(e)  Strengthening the capacity of the Women's Bureau for
policy and program formulation, advice, awareness
creation, and monitoring and evaluation of the impact
of women's development programs through technical
assistance, training, and support for studies.
(f)  Establishing a Fund to be used by NGOs, on a matching
fund basis, for small projects and programs designed
to help women and children.
ProJect        Women all over The Gambia, particularly those living in
Benefits:      rural areas, would benefit from the institutional
strengthening of organizations dealing directly with
women's issues (i.e., the Women's Bureau, the Ministries of
Agriculture, Education, Health, and Local Government). The
project would help improve these institutions'
responsiveness to women's needs and create a greater
awareness about women's development issues among policy-
makers; this, In turn, would contribute to better sectoral
project design in the long term. The project coordination
efforts would improve inter-agency cooperation and
harmonization of existing women's programs. A number of
project activities would be targeted to women in 200
selected villages, with a population of direct
beneficiaries estimated at 50,000. Rural women nationwide
are expected to benefit from the improved extension
services and the private farm input distribution network.
Equally, the family planning program would benefit both
rural and urban women. The entire community, in particular
children, would benefit from women's improved health and
education. Over the long term, women's enhanced role in
the country's development effort would contribute to better
overall economic performance and to poverty reduction.
Proiect        The risks involve primarily (a) the difficulties in
Risks:         changing society's perception of the role of women and the
attitudes of civil servants dealing with women's issues;
(b) IDA's and the Government's lack of experience in



- iii -
preparing and implementing a project of this nature; and
(c) the difficulty of implementing a project with a multi-
sectoral approach. Nevertheless, the Government's strong
commitment to implementing a WID project, the large-scale
involvement of local professionals in project preparation,
and the planned full-scale IEC campaign included In the
project design are expected to provide a favorable climate
for successful implementation. By involving experienced
NGOs, together with technical assistance, the risk of
unsatisfactory implementation would be reduced.

REPUBLIC OF THE GAMBIA
WOMEN IN DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.31     The Government of The Gambia has requested IDA's assistance in
financing a project desig,ned to improve women's productivity, income-
earning potential, welfare, and status. The proposed project also aims to
bring about a change in society's perception of the role of women and to
strengthen government institutions to enable them to better deal with
women's issues.
1.02     Total project costs are estimat.,d at US$15.1 million equivalent
(net of taxes and duties), with a foreig.n exchange component of
US$9.2 million. The Government would contribute US$0.8 million. The
African Development Fund, Norway, UNDP, UNIFEM and UNFPA together would
provide US$7.3 million; the remaining US$7.0 million would be financed by
IDA.
1.03     The proposed project would be the Bank Group's first free-
standing women in development (WID) project. The 1988 social indicator
data sbeet is at Annex 1-1.
II. BACKGROUND
A. Socio-Economic Setting
2.01     The Gambia, with a population estimated at 800,000 and a per
capita income of US$220, is one of the poorest countries in Sub-Saharan
Africa. Situated on the edge of the Sahel, it suffers from recurrent
droughts and untimely distribution of rainfall. About two-thirds of the
population live in rural areas, deriving their livelihood from subsistence
farming, livestock, and the cultivation of groundnuts for export. The
country is highly dependent on trade, importing about one-third of its food
requirements, all of its fuel and capital goods, and most other
manufactured goods. Re-export trade to neighboring countries, encouraged by
an open trade policy, provides over 50Z of foreign exchange earnings.
Groundnuts account for almost 90? of domestic exports and represent 38Z of
export value. The Gambia's low level of agricultural diversification makes
it vulnerable to fluctuations in groundnut revenues, due to such factors as
adverse weather conditions or declining world market prices. Hence the
country's lonig-term development potential will rely upon diversification of
the productive base within agriculture -- mainly into horticultural and
livestock products -- as well as divercification into resource-based
manufacturing, fisheries, and tourism.
2.02     A severe economic and financial crisis in the latter part of the
1970s, resulting from a dramatic expansion of public expenditures,
inappropriate exchange rate and other pricing policies, and adverse
external factors, prompted the Government in 1985 to introduce a
comprehensive economic reform program, which received the support of the
Bank and other multilateral and bilateral donors. The program stresses the
role of the private sector in the economy, focusirng the public sector more
narrowly on activities ir support of private sector development. Positive



effects of this program are becoming evident in the country's improved
macroeconomic performance. The Government has also begun the task of civil
service reform, a selective divestiture of parastatals, institutional
strengthening in critical areas, and reduction of some public services. The
improved policy and institutional environment has led to a -rge in private
sector activities and has helped to lay the foundation for sustained
medium-term growth and diversification of the economy.
2.03     A major constraint to developing the long-term productive
potential of the economy and improving the welfare of the Gambian
population is the country's low level cf human resources development,
reflected in literacy rates of 152 for women and 362 for men and an
estimated 57? primary school enrollment rate for school-age children. This
low level of human resources development W..ontributes to very high infant.
child and maternal mortality rates and to a high incidence of infectious
diseases. The Government recognizes this and despite its recent budgetary
constraints has made an effort to protect basic social services; with donor
assistance, it is expanding and improving the quality of education and
hetlth services in both urban and rural areas. In addition, the Government
has taken other steps to mitigate the effects of adjustment on the most
vulnerable groups, and provides job counselling, retraining and credit for
the retrenched workers. However, a lack of data and analytical
capabilities makes it difficult to monitor the social impact of the
Economic Reform Program. To overcome this deficiency, the Government has
requested African Development Bank/UNDP/IDA assistance in establishing a
nationwide living standard survey in the context of a regional Social
Dimension of Adjustment (SDA) program.
2.04     In focusing its strategy on developing the country's human
resources, the Government is becoming increasingly aware of women's
development issues as it recognizes the significant contribution that women
make to the economy. A growing concern about the pressures that rapid
population growth places on scarce national resources has also prompted the
Government to give greater attention to the relationship between birth
rates and the education of women and to women's participation in the market
economy.
B.     The Role of Women in Gambian Society: Issues and Constraints
2.05     Women in The Gambia contribute substantially to the economy and to
the well-being of their families, yet they face many constraints that
reduce the efficiency and productivity of their work. The major
constraints are outlined below and discussed in det&il in Annex 2-1.
(a) Limited access to productive resources, particularly good land,
credit and other agricultural inputs such as fertilizer and farm
equipment;
(b) Relatively lower levels of educational attainment and more limited
training opportunities than their male counterparts;
(c) The heavy burden of high fertility. exacerbated by poor maternal
health and nutritional status;



- 3 -
(d) Prevailing attitudes of society concerning women's abilities and
socio-economic role, which reduce their status and mobility; and
(e) Women's perception of themselves, combined with a lack of
knowledge concerning both their rights and the resources
potentially available to them.
1.  Agriculture
2.06     Women play a prominent role in the Gambian farming system,
providing between 30X and 50? of the total agricultural labor, depending on
village location. The farming system is characterized by a diverse
division of responsibilities and resources within the extended, polygamous
households (often referred to as compounds), which average 15 family
members. Gender-specific responsibilities vary according to village
location, ethnicity, and the level of farm mechanization. Farming
activities are generally more separated in lowland villages, where mutual
assistance between men and women is less common and where women spend more
time on farming than do those in upland villages. In upland farming there
is a considerable amount of labor exchange, with men often helping women
with the heavier tasks such as land clearing and when animal traction is
used for ploughing, planting and weeding. Women, in turn, provide most of
the labor for harvesting and are responsible for post-harvest processing of
cereals, one of the most time-consuming and arduous tasks of rural women.
Both men and women produce food for household consumption (maruo crops),
which is usually done collectively; both also grow crops for their personal
use (kamanyango crops), a large proportion of which is sold, with the
proceeds used for a wide range of purposes (Annex 2-1, para 5). More than
half of the women of working age cultivate their own kamanyango fields.
Women are practically the sole producers of swamp and tidal rice,
horticultural crops, and sesame, and they also own and manage a large
proportion of the country's sheep, goat and poultry stock.
2.07     Access to Productive resources.  Despite the large role they play
in agricultural production, Gambian women farmers have only limited access
to the resources they need to increase their productivity and improve their
income-earning capabilities. Seldom can they count on the timely
availability of fertilizer, improved seed or 'arm implements. Men usually
have priority access to these inputs through tne cooperative system, which
is the major distributor of agricultural inputs and the only source of
institutionalized farm credit in rural Gambia. Of the 110,000 members of
the Gambia Cooperative Union (GCU), less than 102 are women. GCU also
provides loans for agricultural implements. The limited access of women to
these loans greatly restricts their capacity to benefit from farm
mechanization, which is a key factor in improving agricultural productivity
in The Gambia. Since they are virtually excluded from the formal credit
system, most rural women borrow and save through the informal sector; for
example, they may do this through a rotating fund (osusu) in which members
of local women's groups pool their money and distribute it according to
need. With limited resources and normal'y used by one member at a time,
this fund cannot meet all of its members' needs at the same time, such as
for buying farm inputs at the start of the planting season.
2.08     Retaining good land for cultivating their crops is yet another
problem that women face. Although their right to land is recognized, they



- 4 -
often are kept from exercising this right because traditional law favors
compound and village ward rights to land over individual rights. As the
assignment of land to individuals is usually the prerogative of the male
compound head -- ihe village elders, women may be left with poor quality
land or even be driven off their traditionally held fields. A further
constraint faced by women farmers is limited access to information on new
and improved production technologies; this is due to the extension services
and agricultural research orienting their work mainly towards predominantly
male-produced cash and export crops. Through the Training and Visit (T&V)
system which is being adapted to the extension program under the Second
Agricaltural Development Project (Cr. 1476-GM), women could have better
access to information that would help them improve their farming methods.
However, of the 4,100 'contact farmersl already in the T&V system, so far
only 4-52 are women, because the criteria for selecting these farmers
include literacy and only a minimal number of rural women are literate.
2.09     Recent studies in The Gambia have shown that women's agricultural
productivity is generally 35Z less than that of men.  While the heavy
domestic workload they carry, as well as frequent pregnancies, are
contributing factors, there should be little doubt that the main reason for
this productivity gap is that t1 y do not have equal access to the inputs
needed to match their productivity with that of men: new and improved
production technologies, good land, farm implements, credit, seed and
fertilizer. That they can do so when provided with these inputs has been
demonstrated in the IFAD-assisted Jahally-Pacharr Rice Project (Annex 2-1,
para 10). But that project has also shown that when an agricultural
improvement program is designed to help women, it should take into account
intra-household relationships. Despite the planners' efforts to give women
access to new rice technologies and thus improve their disposable income,
most ended up with the lower yielding technologies and the highest
yielding, irrigated rice technology was to a large extent taken over by
men.
2.   Education and Training
2.10     Women continue to have limited access to education and training
opportunities in The Gambia, despite the expansion of the education system
and increase in primary school enrollments over the past two decades.
Disparities in access to formal education are evident in the primary school
enrollment rates: in 1988 the national enrollment rate was about 68? for
boys and only 46? for girls. Boys outnumber girls nearly 3 to 1 in the
secondary high schools and 2 to 1 in the secondary technical schools (The
Gambia has no university). There are also regional disparities in
schooling, with urban areas having a larger female participation than rural
areas; in the Banjul area, for example, only 168 girls per thousand are out
of school, whereas in the Upper River Division as many as 860 per thousand
are out of school. Gender disparities in educational opportunities have
increased over the past few years in the face of severe budgetary
constraints that have led to a shortage of teachers and school places
available for an ever-increasing number of school-age children. The
limited number of places, along with negative teacher attitudes concerning
female scholastic ability and potential, works against the enrollment of
girls and in favor of boys. Even when girls are enrolled, several factors
operate against successful completion and achievement; these include
competing demands on their time, early marriage and pregnancy, a lack of
positive role models, negative parental and community attitudes toward the



- 5 -
Western-type of education for girls, and the poor quality and lack of
relevance of education to the expected vocation of women. An additional
factor in low female educational attainment in The Gambia is the late age
of school entry (age 8), which gives girls only a few years of schooling
and little chance to complete it before they reach puberty.
2.11     As part of the overall effort to improve the status of women and
promote their full participation in the economy, a study was made by an
inter-ministerial task force to identify the underlying causes of female
under-representation in the educational system and recommend remedial
actions to be implemented over the long term. The proposed The Gambia
Education Sector Project, being processed in parallel with this operation,
would include some of the measures recommended in the study, such as
lowering the primary school entry age to 7 and later on to age 6; revising
curricula and textbooks to promote a more positive image of women's role in
society; giving priority to the training of female teachers; actively
encouragiag g'rls to enroll in more traditionally male-dominated classes,
such as s-ience and mathematics; and integrating family life education
programs into school curricula.
2.12     Nonformal education.  As an alternative to formal schooling, which
the large majority of Gambian women either have not attended or were unable
to complete, nonformal education programs could help them to acquire basic
skills in areas of vital impcrtance in improving their lives: literacy,
preventive health caxre, sanitation, and family planning. Training in
skills to improve w.amen's work productivity, such as in agriculture and
marketing, could also be offered. Governmental and non-governmental
agencies already providing training in numeracy/literacy and other skills
(e.g., vegetable gardening, seed multiplication, tie-and-dye, soap-making,
accounting and record keeping) include the Department of Nonformal
Education (NFE) of the Ministry of Education, which introduced a functional
literacy/numeracy program in 1982; the Department of Community Development
(DCD) of the Ministry of Local Government and Lands, which has the largest
number of skill training programs offered by government; and Action Aid,
the most active NGO in the nonformal education sector and accounting for
nearly half of the programs offered. Though not specifically designed for
women, the training programs place special emphasis on upgrading women's
skills. The number of participants is rather small (about 23,000,
according to a recent survey, representing about 102 of the adult rural
population), and women far outnumber men, thus demonstrating their desire
and need for training. Women attending the programs are in the 20-30 age
group, and competing household and child care responsibilities make their
attendance less regular than that of men.
3.   Female Entrepreneurship
2.13     Women are very active in the informal sector, comprising the
majority of the self-employed workers. In the urban areas, they are
engaged in such enterprises as retail marketing of fresh produce, small
restaurant services, textile dying, tailoring for both the domestic and
tourist market, tile manufacturing, and road transport. In the rural areas
their activities are limited to crafts production and the marketing of
surplus vegetables and fruit. Informal surveys have shown that there is
considerable scope for increasing income from these enterprises by
improving the product or service, exploiting economies of scale, and
introducing better business management practices. There is also a



- 6 -
potentia. for women entrepreneurs to branch out into new enterprises, such
as fish processing, large-scale horticultural production and marketing, and
food processing and preservation.
2.14     While all Gambians engaged in small-scale enterprises (SSEs) have
to cope with many problems in conducting their activities -- the main ones
involve finances, markets, and availability of raw materials -- for womerL
these common problems often become actual barriers because of society's
negative attitude concerning women's abilities, greater difficulty in
obtaining credit, and lack of training and business experience. Most of the
institutional lending in The Gambia is for financing wholesale and retail
trade business and groundnut marketing, in which few women are involved.
Though some women entrepreneurs have received commercial loans and they
have a better repayment record than mer. very few women participate in the
formal financial system, either as borrowers or savers. Small credit
schemes for urban enterprises operated by women have been provided by the
Cooperative Thrift and Credit Society and the Indigenous Business Advisory
Service (IBAS), set up under the Ministry of Planning and Industrial
Development. Both of these institutions, however, face severe financial
problems, due mainly to poor management, and therefore cannot be considered
as sources of lending under the proposed WID project.
4.   Health and Nutrition Status
2.15     The health status of women in The Gambia is among the poorest in
the world. The maternal mortality rate of 10 per 1,000 live births is more
than twice the average rate (4.5) for developing countries, and in some of
the country's rural areas maternal mortality is as high as 20-22 per 1,000
live births. There is also a high incidence of malnutrition among women
and children. The poor health of women has profound implications for the
welfare of the country's children: the infant and under five mortality
rates (143 and 242, respectively, per 1,000 births) are among the highest
in Sub-Saharan Africa. Post-partum hemorrhage and infections are the
leading causes of maternal mortality.  Infant and child mortality is cause4
mainly by malaria, diarrhea, and respiratory tract infections.
Malnutrition has a compounding effect on mortality and morbidity,
especially among children and pregnant women. The main factors influencing
the high maternal mortality rate are the lack of an effective early warning
system for high-risk pregnancies, inadequate access to antenatal care,
unavailability of transport in an emergency, and a first-referral level not
adequately equipped to deal with high-risk cases.
2.16     Improvement in the health care system is expected under the
National Health Development Program, launched in 1987 with the support of
IDA and other international donors, including the Italian, British and
Dutch Governments. The program focuses on (a) expanding the referral
levels, from health centers to hospitals; (b) training community health
nurses (CHNs) and state-enrolled nurses (SENs); (c) improving
communications; (d) setting up an efficient logistical system; and (e)
introducing important reforms to strengthen health sector management and
financing. The program is helping to extend primary health care (PHC) to
the Greater Banjul area, and is introducing cost recovery for drugs and
health services to promote more efficient use of health facilities and
shift some of the cost of improved services to the beneficiaries.



-7-
2.17     Malnutrition is widespread, the most severely affected groups
being pregnant and lactating mothers and children between the ages of 9 and
36 months. There are both regional and seasonal variations in the
incidence of malnutrition, with rural areas more affected than urban areas.
In rural communities during the rainy season, referred to as the 'hungry
season" because less food is available, the caloric intake is only 1,660
calories per person per day, compared with 2,140 during the post-harvest
season. The effect of this caloric shortfall is exacerbated by the heavy
workload borne by all active members of the community, including pregnant
and lactating women.
2.18     A high fertility rate (6.5 live births per woman) is yet another
factor that weakens maternal health and a woman's ability to provide for
her children. Reflecting its concern about the rapid growth of the
population, the Government has made public pronouncements on the need to
reduce the growth rate and is fully supportive of the family planning
programs that have been underway in The Gambia for several years
(Annex 3-3, para 30). While these programs have met with some success,
they face a number of constraints that impede the effort to gain wider
acceptance of the services they provide and better understanding of the
health benefits of birth spacing. These constraints include the generally
low literacy level of the population, particularly women; a bias toward
having large families; and many misconceptions about family planning shared
by men and women alike. The Government's efforts to increase awareness of
the importance of family planning in relation to development and to
improving the condition of women, especially those in rural communities,
needs to be followed up by a well-coordinated action program that includes
quantitative targets for the FP programs.
C. Institutions
2.19     The President's Office has overall responsibility for women's
issues. In 1980 the President established the Women's Council and the
Women's Bureau to advise on women's issues and to promote programs that
benefit women. Women's programs are being implemented by a number of
ministries and Governmental agencies. NGOs also address specific women-
related issues, but their activities are scattered and uncoordinated.
2.20     National Women's Council and Women's Bureau.  The National Women's
Council Act of 1980 established the National Women's Council as an advisory
body to the Government. The Council consists of 27 members, including the
Chairwoman and the Executive Secretary of the Women's Bureau (an ex-
officio member), appointed by the Minister for Women's Affairs (the
President) and representing the country's five Divisions, Greater Banjul
and the Kombos. The Act also established the Women's Bureau, under the
Office of the President, to administer the Council's affairs. The main
function of the Council and the Bureau is to advise the Government on all
matters affecting the development and welfare of women, with particular
reference to: (a) their education and training; (b) appropriate measures
that should be taken to mobilize and integrate women into the development
process as equal partners; (c) areas where women's participation should be
encouraged and strengthened; (d) steps that should be taken to correct the
adverse effects of traditional beliefs, customs and practices on the
advancement of women; (e) dissemination of information necessary to help
promote the advancement and well-being of women; and (f) establishment of



programs to permit continuous review and evaluation of the economic and
social progress achieved by women.
2.21     In addition to advising the government on issues of concern to
women and to promoting women's interests and their integration into
national, regional and sectoral planning, the Women's Bureau has
implemented and supervised a number of projects, funded mainly by external
donors, designed to enhance the general welfare of women. In those
projects and in the workshops and seminars conducted by the Bureau,
emphasis has been on easing women's workload through the provision of
labor-saving devices; improving health conditions; increasing educational
opportunities and helping women acquire income-generating skills; and
providing them with small credit facilities. The Bureau has also conducted
research on the needs and problems of Gambian women; the topics include
training needs, women engaged in small-scale enterprises, the effects of
female circumcision, the land tenure system and how it affects women, and
the participation of women in the fishing industry. Effective
administration of these activities has been constrained by a skeletal and
inexperienced staff, despite the role played by the five specialized
committees set up by the Council and the Bureau to look into specific areas
related to women's development needs (agriculture and small-scale
industries, health, education and training, and legal status). The lack of
staff has also hampered the Bureau's ability to mediate and coordinate the
interests of women as expressed in the activities of other ministries.
Since 1988, with the assistance of the United Nations Fund for Women
(UNIFEM) the Women's Bureau has been able to expand its staff and
strengthen its operations. Strengthening of its data collection
capabilities is also planned under the Social Dimension of Adjustment (SDA)
project. The Bureau's staff presently consists of an executive secretary,
a deputy executive secretary, a public relations officer, two planners, a
researcher, an editor, 11 field officers, and a United Nations volunteer.
2.2k     Ministry of Agriculture.   So far, most of this Ministry's
programs have not taken into consideration in their design the
contributions of women in the various agricultural activities. Until
recently, extension messages were not reaching the majority of women
farmers, because they were geared mainly to the promotion of male-
dominated crops. Nor have the minuscule number of female extension workers
(less than 57) been able to effectively reach women farmers. This situation
has improved with the Ministry's involvement in the preparation of the
proposed WID project; a deliberate effort is being made to address women's
development issues in the training program for extension workers, currently
being implemented under the Second Agricultural Development Project
(Cr. 1476-GM). Cultural factors do not appear to prevent male extension
agents from working with female farmers. The embryonic Food and Nutrition
Unit (FNU), established in 1986 in the Ministry of Agriculture, is the only
part of the Ministry that has a recognized brief to work specifically with
women. Of FNU's six staff members, only one has had degree-level training.
The Unit's tasks include: improving traditional storage of foodgrains;
testing and advising on new small-scale technologies for post-harvest
processing, vegetable processing and storage; and developing recipes and
ways to improve the variety and nutritional quality of food prepared and
consumed by households. FNU is short of operating funds that would enable
it to operate in the field, and has no facilities with which to prepare
demonstrations or to test improved practices. Also, cooperation between



- 9 -
FRU and the Nutrition Unit in the Ministry of Health is currently very
limited and needs to be improved.
2.23     Ministry of Health, Environment, Labor and Social Welfare.  This
Ministry provides maternal and child health care (MCH) through a primary
health care system that includes 16 health centers and 316 village health
posts staffed with a Village Health Worker (VHW) and a Traditional Birth
Attendant (TBA). In adO'tion, mobile trekking teams link the health centers
with more than 100 vilL.,_es. Most rural health facilities are in poor
condition, and the transport system to take emergency cases to either the
health centers or the country's two hospitals is inadequate. The ongoing
Health Development Project (Cr. 1760-GM) will go a long way toward
improving this situation. Under that project, the existing referral system
is being improved through the rehabilitation and upgrading of the bulk of
the health centers and existing buildings at the two national hospitals.
2.24     Ministry of Education, Youth, Sports and Culture.   This Ministry
is responsible for formal education at the primary, secondary and post-
secondary levels. Post-secondary education is offered principally at the
Gambia College and the Gambia Technical Training Institute (GTTI). The
Gambia College has four schools: teacher training, agriculture, nursing and
mid-wifery, and public health. Formal vocational training is the
responsibility of the National Vocational Training Board (NVTB), which is
also under the Ministry of Education. GTTI offers technician and crafts
courses leading to a commercial and industrial certificate. In addition,
the Institute develops tailor made training programs as required by private
industry and governmental agencies. Also under NHTB is the National Rural
Vocational Training Center at Mansakongo, with a mandate to train
instructors for the training and upgrading of rural craftsmen in basic
trades. Thus far, only a small number of women have participated in the
center's training programs.
2.25     Ministry of Local Government and Lands.  This Ministry's
Department of Community Development (DCD) offers training programs for
village women, including literacy and numeracy training. The DCD has eight
specialized units,including a Research, Planning and Monitoring Unit, a
Training Unit, and a Women's Programs Unit. DCD's various activities are
being implemented by 100 rural-based Community Development Assistants
(CDAs), whose role is to act as catalysts of development and facilitate the
establishment of village self-help organizations. The women's Programs
Unit has 17 Homecraft Assistants, who provide training in home management
and crafts skills to village women. DCD has extensive operational
experience and plays a key role in coordinating the work of different
agencies and in mobilizing participants in development programs.
2.26     Ministry of Economic Planning and Industrial Development. The
Indigenous Business Advisory Service (IBAS), established under this
Ministry in 1983, offers training courses for small rural and urban
entrepreneurs on business concepts (e.g., record-keeping, planning and
marketing). IBAS also administers a loan scheme which so far has not faired
well due to low repayment rates and poor supervision on the part of its
staff. Women constitute about 252 of IBAS' clients; they are mostly small
urban entrepreneurs engaged in activities such as petty trading, tie-and-
dye. and the operation of small retail shops. The organization's limited
staffing and rural outreach (IBAS has only four branches outside Banjul)
hamper its efforts to integrate more women into its programs.



- 10 -
2.27     Financial institutions.  In addition to the Central Bank, The
Gambia's financial sector comprises a housing finance company (Social
Security and Housing Finance Company -- SSHFC) and three commercial banks:
Standard Bank, a subsidiary of Standard Chartered of London, with minority
(252) local ownership; Banque Internationale pour le Commerce et
l'Industrie, a branch of Senegal's BICIS; and the Gambia Commercial and
Development Bank, which is fully Government owned. All the commercial
banks provide credit to practically all sectors (agriculture, tourism,
manufacturing, construction), and there are no legal barriers to women's
access to credit. However, the commercial banks have traditionally
advanced less than 5X of their credit to women, and despite recent efforts,
including an attempt by the Gambia Women's Finance Company (GWFC) to
provide partial guarantees for women borrowers, access of women to
institutional credit will be limited for some time. This is due to a
variety of reasons. First, the banks have, for reasons of prudency,
concentrated on short-term commercial credit to medium and large private
businesses. Second, the oligopolistic nature of Gambian banking assures
the banks of significant earnings from Government Treasury bills, thus
reducing their need to compete for a client base. Third, the geographic
concentration of the banks, with most branches in the Banjul area, makes it
difficult for rural women to gain access to them.
2.28     Public information sector institutions.  Responsibility for the
public information sector lies with the Ministry of Information and
Tourism. The sector is very small, with no daily newspapers or television
broadcasting stations. There are two radio stations: one is private,
broadcasting mainly music, headline news and advertisements; the other,
Radio Gambia, is state owned. Radio Gambia transmits in local languages
magazine programs with a development content, particularly in programs for
rural areas, on tcpics relating to self-reliance, health, family planning,
agriculture, forestry, and fisheries. The programs are broadcast in the
country's four main languages. A recent media survey revealed that radio
is the most accessible source of information in The Gambia for both men and
women, and is also considered to be the most reliabl. A number of
ministries (Agriculture, Health, Education, and Information) have their own
communications units and undertake information, education and communication
(IEC) activities.
2.29     Non-governmental organizations.  Many NGOs are active in The
Gambia. Their exact lumber is not known because not all are registered
with the Ministry of Justice, as required by law. A recent survey of NGOs
lists over 100 of varying size, both indigenous and international.
Registered NGOs have formed an inter-NGO entity, The Association of Non-
Governmental Organizations (TANGO). Coordination of governmental and NGO
activities is the responsibility of the Advisory Committee for the
Coordination of Non-Governmental Organizations (ACCNO). NGOs, particularly
those that have been operating in the country for some time, have made
significant contributions to The Gambia's development efforts not only by
complementing the government services, but also by playing an innovative
and demonstration role; NGOs such as Catholic Relief Services (CRS), Action
Aid, and Save the Children Fund (SCF) have introduced new farming crops and
provided wells and infrastructure. In the health sector, NGOs work at the
village level to expand the capacity of health programs (particularly MCI
care) to reach the rural poor. NGOs are involving villagers as volunteer
representatives in their programs. For example The Gambia Family Planning



- 11 -
Association (GFPA), an offshoot of the International Planned Parenthood
Association (IPPA), uses village volunteers, usually (TBAs), to expand its
capacity to deliver contraceptives and disseminate family planning
information. SCF (US) has developed village health teams to work with SCF
health recorders. CRS has formed MCH management committees to act as
liaison between CRS and the community. In education, NGOs also play an
important role. Action Aid, Caritas, the Christian Children's Fund, and
SCF (US) have participated in the construction of primary schools and
provided teaching equipment and supplies. Action Aid has also been
successfully experimenting with village group lending.
2.30     At a time when the Government is attempting to make further
reductions in the civil service, the role of the NG0s becomes particularly
important in providing much-needed services, especially in sectors where
the Government's outreach is limited. NGOs have been particularly
successful in reaching women and in developing programs specifically
designed for them (e.g., cultivation of village gardens, bee-keeping,
smoking and drying of fish, and weaving), and ensuring that their staff are
aware of the need to include women in their programs. As the contributions
that NGOs are making in all of the country's development efforts are
becoming increasingly important, their programs need to be better
coordinated to ensure maximum effectiveness. UNDP is helping to strengthen
TANGO and ACCNO to enable them to perform their coordinating functions
effectively.
2.31     Kafos.  These are village-level women's groups-, generally
organized on the basis of age. Membership is voluntary, and contributions
are made regularly on a weekly or monthly basis. The number of members
ranges from 15 to over 300, with most kafos having about 100. Most of them
have a "father' -- a trusted man in the community to whom they frequently
entrust their savings. Traditionally, kafos were primarily concerned with
providing mutual support: members sharing labor on each other's fields,
making loans to those in need, and combining resources for major events.
Lately, kafos have widened their scope of activities and become active in
income-generating activities, such as horticulture, tie-and-dye, sewing,
and soap-making. Many provide skill training for their members. Some kafos
have even opened bank or post office savings accounts, but having ready
access to these accounts generally poses problems because of the expensive
travel involved. Kafos also now tend to be more structured, with the
leaders assigned officer-type roles and held accountable by the members.
2.32     In the process of evolving, kafos have come up against a variety
of constraints. While some are simply extensions of common problems faced
by rural women (e.g. insecurity of land tenure and a lack of labor-saving
devices, farm equipment, and transport to carry their produce to market),
other constraints result from the important role of the kafos not being
recognized by outside interventions. A recent survey undertaken in a number
of villages revealed that most development agencies, including many NGOs,
often are not aware of the organizational potential represented in a kafo
and establish new structures to involve women in their development efforts.
This not only wastes available resources often in short supply, but also
weakens the indigenous kafos' ability tc help themselves. Village-level
women's associations should be made use of and, to the extent practicable
and useful, be encouraged to register under appropriate existing
legislation.



- 12 -
2.33     Village Development Committees (VDCs).  These committees are
village institutions responsible for the "grassroot' implementation of
development programs. Their membership is composed of village secular and
religious leaders, representatives of male and female groups, and agents of
government departments (i.e., Community Development Assistants,
Agricultural Extension Workers, and Village Health Workers). VDCs have
been established in a large number of villages, but their degree of
involvement in community decision-making varies considerably. In some
areas, particularly where development programs are sponsored by NGOs, VDCs
are very active, while in other areas they are dormant. The Government
intends to strengthen and expand VDCs nationwide as the focal point for
integrated development at the village level and for mobilizing and
generating resources. A proposal for strengthening VDCs, with emphasis on
improving their capacity to provide supervision and support for the
national PHC program, was recently prepared by Project Concern
International, a US-based institution, and is currently being considered by
USAID for funding.
D.  The Government's Strategy
2.34     The Gambia's main development objectives, as stated in the 1985-
88 Economic Recovery Program (para 2.02) are to (a) bring the public sector
into balance with the productive base of the economy; (b) create favorable
incentives for private sector productive activities in agriculture,
resource-based industries, fishing, and tourism; and (c) develop The
Gambia's human resources capital by giving priority to long-term
investments in education and basic health services. The Government is well
aware that improving the condition of women is an essential part of the
overall effort to achieve these objectives. It has formally acknowledged
the need to improve women's status and increase their access to productive
resources and basic social services. Various initiatives are already
underway to increase women's participation in socio-economic development.
To assist in these efforts, many donors and NGOs are funding programs
designed to alleviate some of the constraints faced by women (para 2.05);
however, both the Government and the donors agree that much more needs to
be done to improve the welfare and status of women. Hence, with the Bank's
assistance, the Government has developed a national strategy for
integrating women into the development process.
2.35     This national strategy, covering all identified aspects of women's
concerns and needs, has two principle objectives: (a) provide
opportunities for women to improve their economic status by increasing
their access to productive resources; and (b) increase women's access to
basic social services (education, health, nutrition, and family planning).
The Government's commitment to these objectives is manifested in the direct
and personal involvement of the President, who also serves as the Minister
for Women's Affairs, and in the creation of the National Women's Council
and the Women's Bureau in 1980 to advise the Government on women's issues.
Moreover, recognizing the importance of NGOs in the overall effort to
improve the condition of women, the Government has also created an Advisory
Committee for the Coordination of NGOs (ACCNO), to monitor their activities
and advise on community needs and opportunities.
2.36     Within the framework of the national strategy, the Government
intends to address a number of policy and program concerns. With respect
to the productive sector, the Government intends to  (a) improve women's



- 13 -
access to productive resources and labor-saving technology (e.g.,
fertilizer, chemicals, improved farm implements, milling machines, and
transport facilities); (b) improve the extension workers' knowledge of
"female crops"; (c) strengther. support for women's horticultural
activities; (d) increase women's access to credit; and (e) strengthen
women's entrepreneurial capacity. To increase women's access to basic
social services, the Government proposes to (a) increase female educational
enrollment (it has already taken a major step in this regard by lowering
the primary school entry age from 8 to 7); (b) increase female literacy and
their skill training opportunities; and (c) expand and strengthen maternal
and child health, family planning. and nutrition programs.
2.37     While the Government's strategy covers the most essential programs
and reforms, there also is need for legislative and related actions to help
overcome discrimination in both the public and private sectors. In line
with its mandate under the National Women's Council Act of i980, 1/ the
Women's bureau commissioned a highly important study on the legal status of
women. It deals with such questions as the civil. and political rights of
women, marriage, divorce, inheritance and succession, and land ownersaip
rights, from the viewpoint of the statutory legislation, the sharia
(Islamic law), the traditional laws and customs, and the role of the
courts. Seminars have been organized to discuss the stud) s conclusions
and recommendations, some of which have also been taken up by the Law
Reform Commission, whose mandate is to prepare the relevant bills to be
sent to the Minister of Justice and thence to Parliament. As part of these
ongoing activities, the study should be widely distributed, possioly in an
abridged form. As stated in the study, improving the status of wvomen calls
for the utmost caution, sensitivity and determination. The Women's Bureau
should be commended for its invaluable work and be given the means to
undertake more research and awareness campaigns, particularly through the
proposed WID project, to build on what has already been accomplished.
Further government initiatives are now needed to bring about a change in
society's perception of the role of women and to build commitment and
understanding of women's issues at the community level. This should
include a full-scale and well-orchestrated information, education and
communication (IEC) campaign, covering the full range of issues.
E. The Bank Group's Role
2.38     Agriculture.  IDA has financed three projects in the agricultural
sector of The Gambia, two of which are completed: The Gambia Agricultural
Development Project (Cr. 333-GM for US$1.3 million, 1972) and the Rural
Development Project (Cr. 644-GM for US$4.1 million, 1976). The Second
Agricultural Development Project (Cr. 1476-GM for US$9.4 million, 1984) is
ongoin6. All three projects have failed to establish a viable system for
supplying farm inputs because of low credit recovery, due mainly to overly
liberal credit policies and exclusive reliance on parastatals. In
addition, GCU, the primary implementing agency, has had long-standing
management problems. Both project performance audit reports on the
1/ The Act empowers the Women's Bureau 'to examine and study the economic,
rocial and cultural structure of the society... and to study and advise
government on how traditiorm! beliefs and practices have adversely affected
the advancement of Women and what steps should be taken to correct the
situation.*



- 14 -
completed projects (PPAR No. 1724 for The Gambia Agricultural Development
Project, September 1977, and PPAR No. 4558 for the Rural Development
Project. June 1983) mention the failure of these projects to recognize and
take into account the crucial role that women play in the Gambian farming
system and cite the failure to do so as one cause of their poor
performance. While the first project completely ignored women, the Rural
Development Project addressed women's needs only marginally; under that
project, women leaders with some schooling were selected in 3 few farming
communities to provide extension services to other women. This experiment
proved to be successful, but its impact was negligible because of its
modest size. The ongoing Second Agricultural Development Project has only
recently addressed women farmers' needs by integrating them into the
training program for extension workers.
2.39     IDA's experience in the Gambian agricultural sector  provides
valuable lessons that are taken into account in the design of the proposed
WID project. These includes (a) recognizing the complex division of tasks
and responsibilities that characterizes the Gambian household economy; (b)
placing more emphasis on savings than on credit to provide capital for
increased agricultural productivity; (c) encouraging increased community
participation and responsibilities, which is consistent with the approach
promoted under the Economic Reform Program (para 2.02), to reduce public
sector involvement in meeting community needs; and (d) establishing an
effective monitoring and evaluation system for regularly measuring project
progress.
2.40     Enterprise development.  The Enterprise Development Project (Cr.
1975-GM for US$10.0 million, 1989) provides logistical and technical
support to the Gambia Women's Finance Company (GWFC), an affiliate of
Women's World Banking. GWFC was established in 1987 to provide technical
and managerial training and access to institutional credit for small and
medium enterprises headed by women. With contributions from local business
establishments, its own fund-raising activities, and matching funds from
Women's World Ban-king, GWFC has set up a fund to partially guarantee
commercial loans to women. US$1.0 million of the project's line of credit
has been earmarked to finance small and medium sub-projects of women
entrepreneurs.
2.41     Health.  The National Health Development Project (Cr. 1760-GM for
US$5.6 million, 1987) is in its third year of implementation and
considerable progress has been made in the areas of (a) cost recovery (the
current recovery rate is 65Z and the utilization rate of health services
has increased considerably since project inception); (b) primary health
care, which has been expanded to cover the Western Region of the country;
and (c) in-service training in MCH care and family planning.
Implementation of the civil works program (mainly reconstruction and
rehabilitation of health centers) has, however, experienced substantial
delays and cost overruns; also, the sectoral reform program, particularly
the decentralization of financial management of the two national hospitals
and the three Regional health offices is progressing slowly. Technical
assistance will be provided to the Ministry of Health to accelerate the
decentralization program, and a revised work program with detailed revised
costing will be devised for the civil works program to accelerate its
completion and determine the magnitude of the financing gap.



- 15 -
2.42     Urban services.  The ongoing Urban Management and Development
Project (Cr. 1443-GM for US$11.5 million, 1984), directly benefits women's
small urban enterprises through the expansion and rehabilitation of three
major markets in the Greater Banjul area. At least 502 of the traders in
these markets are women. The markets are being provided with new roofing
and concrete floors, as well as water and sanitation facilities, which will
considerably improve the traders' working conditions.
2.43     Rationale for IDA's involvement.  The Government's WID strategy
addresses women's concerns and issues adequately. Its dual purpose of
increasing women's productive capacity and at the same time increasing
their access to basic social services, thereby contributing to general
economic growth, is in line with IDA's country objectives. The Gove'.ment
looks to IDA for assistance to further develop and implement this strategy.
In its sectoral lending programs, IDA is helping to address critical
women's development issues through the Enterprise Development, Health, and
Urban Management Development Projects. An education sector project, being
processed in parallel with this operation, addresses the issue of female
enrollment and achievement in the formal education system. There is need,
however, to address women's development issues within a more comprehensive
framework. Through the proposed WID project, IDA can play a critical role
in helping the Government to do so and to design and test new approaches to
women's issues and better coordinate donor assistance in this field. Once
these initiatives have been developed and prove successful, they could
become part of the country's sectoral programs. An IDA free-standing WID
project makes sense in The Gambia at this time, because of the small
project pipeline and consequent limited opportunity to introduce WID
components in a number of sectors in the short-term, and also because the
component approach has not been fully successful in some previous projects
in The Gambia.
2.44     The proposed VID project would be the Bank Group's first WID
project. The Gambia is particularly suited for such a novel project.
because (a) the Government has made a strong commitment to address women's
issues; and (b) the encouraging results of small-scale innovative
approaches, particularly by NGOs, merit replication.



- 16 .
III. THE PROJECT
A.   Project Origin
3.01      In May 1987, with the assistance of the World Bank and other
major donors, the Government of The Gambia organized a Women in Development
Workshop, which drafted the Government's women in development (WED)
strategy. Thereafter, the Government requested IDA's assistance in
addressing the women's issues raised during the course of the worksh:p; the
IDA mission that participated in the workshop also identified areas for
possible IDA intervention.
3.02      Preparation of the prcposed project began in 1988.  Efforts have
been made to identify specific needs and concerns of Gambian women, and the
project components have been selected and designed to address those
considered to be the most important and urgent. To ensure local
participation in defining and addressing the needs of Gaibian women,
project preparation was entrusted to local task forces composed of
government officials and representatives of the NGOs most active in women's
projects. The task forces were assisted by consultants, with financing
provided under the Project Preparation Facility (PPF), and by four IDA
missions during 1988 and 1989. Various pilot activities, studies and
surveys were undertaken during the preparation stage to identify
strategies for replication under the proposed project. More recently, line
ministries have assumed direct responsibility for the remainder of project
preparation to enable them to incorporate relevant project components into
their normal operational tasks. The project was appraised in
October/November 1989.
B.   Project Obiectives
3.03      The WID project aims to (a) improve women's productivity and
income-earning potential; (b) improve women's welfare and status; (c)
strengthen government institutions to enable thetm to better deal with
women's issues; and (d) contribute to bringing about a change in society's
perception of the role of women.
C.   Summary of Project Composition
3.04      The project comprises six components, summarized below.
(a) Increasing women's agricultural productivity and disposable
income and improving the nutritional level of women and children
throughs (i) the strengthening of agricultural extension
services to better serve women; (ii) private sector input
distribution; (iii) support for women's cooperative groups; (iv)
strengthening of food storage, preservation and nutrition
extension; (v) improved management of small-scale post-harvest



- 17 -
equipment; and (vi) a savings mobilization campaign directed at
women.
(b)  Improving the skills of rural women and promoting viable and
marketable skills; organizing and strengthening women's groups
in rural areas for small business and income-generating
activities; and promoting basic literacy and numeracy skills
through training activities related to women's social and
economic needs.
(c) Promoting safe motherhood and strengthening family planning
services through: (i) training of village-level health workers
and traditional birth attendants in early identification and
management of high-risk pregnancies; (ii) development of an
*alarm' and transport system at the community level to handle
medical emergencies in time; (iii) a nutrition education and
micro-nutrient supplementation program; and (iv) expansion of
the community-based contraceptive distribution system and
involvement of pharmacists and drug dispensers in the family
planning program.
(d)  Developing an information, education and communication (IEC)
strategy, with emphasis on specific needs and concerns of women,
and reinforcing ongoing educational and motivational campaigns
on agriculture, health, family planning, and nutrition.
(e)  Strengthening the capacity of the Women's Bureau for policy and
program formulation, advice, awareness creation, and monitoring
and evaluation of the impact of women's development programs
through technical assistance, training, and support for studies.
(f) Establishing a fund to be used by NGOs, on a matching fund
basis, or small projects and programs designed to help women
and children.
3.05      The project would be implemented over a five-year period
(October 1990 - September 1995). Although a few of the proposed activities
would be nationwide, 200 villages (out of a total of about 1,800 in The
Gambia), with a population representing about 25Z of the cotntry's total
population, are targeted for project interventions. Most of these 200
villages would benefit from the full range of planned activities; other
villages, where there is no sptzific program need or where intervention is
not feasible, would receive only parts of the package. For example, while
villages in the Upper River Division would be targeted for intervention
under the safe motherhood and family planning component, the agricultural
scope of work will be limited in that Division because it is the
beneficiary of a large EEC agricultural project. Also, the skills
development component would be implemented in only 125 villages. In
selecting the 200 villages, criteria specific to each component were
identified and used to prepare preliminary lists. These lists have been
reconciled on the basis of an appropriate geographical distribution and a
mixture of criteria, including women's needs and vulnerability, village
accessibility, and the existence of a minimum of facilities and activities.



- 18 -
The selection process followed by the Government is appropriate. The list
of the 200 project villages was finalized during negotiations.
D.   Project Description
1. Agriculture
3.06      The objectives of the agricultural component are to increase
women's agricultural productivity, provide women in rural areas wPh
opportunities to increase their disposable income, and improve the
nutritional status of women and children. The implementation strategy
developed for this component aims to integrate government services, private
initiatives, and local NGOs according to their comparative advantages by:
(a) Strengthening government extension services so that they will
better serve women farmers engaged in producing field crops and
in horticultural and livestock production;
(b) Increasing women's access to improved production technologies
through the establishment of a private sector network of women
retailers for agricultural inputs, and through institutional
support to GCU for increasing the provision of inputs to women's
farming groups;
(c) Reinforcing the long-term ability of the Ministry of
Agriculture's Food and Nutrition Unit (FNU) to work with women
on post-harvest storage, preservation and food processing by
providing technical assistance, training, and operational funds;
(d) Establishing village management committees and operator training
facilities for existing coarse-grain mills to alleviate
maintenance and operational problems; and
(g) Mobilizing savings by facilitating access of rural women to
commercial banks and other financial institutions.
The sub-components comprising the agricultural component are described in
Annex 3-1 and summarized below.
3.07      Strengthening extension services for women's field crops.  The
Government has agreed to develop and implement a program to strengthen
extension services for women's agricultural activities. During
negotiations the Government gave assurances that among the "contact
farmers, selected by the extension service, it will increase the percentage
who are women to 10, 15Z, 202, 252 and 302 by June 30, 1991, 1992, 1993,
1994, and 1995 respectively; and among the farmers selected to participate
in block demonstrations, it will increase the percentage of women to 152,
202, 252, 302 and 352, respectively, by the same dates. The Government
also gave assurances that it will prepare and transmit to IDA by December
31, 1990, for discussion a national strategy paper, including strategy at
the divisional level, aimed at increasing the impact of its agricultural
extension program on women and ensuring achievement of the above-described



- 19 -
percentage increases in the number of women contact farmers and women block
demonstration participants. The following measures under the WID project
will provide guidance to field staff in identifying and overcoming the
constraints faced by women farmerss
-    A campaign to sensitize field staff and women farmers would be
conducted at the beginning of the project; this would include
workshops attended by extension staff and leaders of women's
groups for mutual exchange of information.
-    Semiannual program planning workshops within the Department of
Agricultural Services and an annual workshop with non-
governmental extension workers would be held so exchange
extension experience and improve strategies for reaching women.
-    Extension manuals, with special reference to the constraints
faced by women farmers and with emphasis on wvmen's traditional
crops, would be produced for use by village extension workers.
Block demonstrations, specifically designed for women farmers, on the use
of improved technologies are expected to play a key role in increasing
demand for such technologies among women. These demonstrations will be
carefully orchestrated with the efforts to improve input availability
through private retailers and Cooperative women's groups.
3.08      Strengthening extension services forwomen's horticulture.
Horticulture is almost exclusively a women's activity in The Gambia.
A Horticultural Extension Program (HEP) already exists, but the principal
responsibility for horticultural extension rests with the polyvalent
Village Extension Workers (VEWs), most of whom have no specific training
for gardening. This sub-component would upgrade the HEP by providing
logistical support, local training for extension personnel, and training
abroad for senior staff. Support would also be given for the horticultuzal
demonstration programs of the FAO Fertilizer Project.
3.09      Strengthening extension services for women's livestock
production. Improving small ruminant and poultry production has especially
important implications for the economic status of women. Women own 402 of
the sheep, 50X of the goats and most of the poultry in The Gambia. This
sub-component would build on experience gained in pilot programs presently
being undertaken by the Department of Livestock Services. It would
include: the training of specialized livestock extension staff and the
development of delivery systems for health care packages and low-cost
feeding supplements; logistical support for field staff; a revolving fund
for a variety of preventive and curative drugs; and laboratory equipment.
The drugs would be sold by extension staff for cash, with the revenues used
to replenish the revolving fund at the Department of Livestock Services.
3.10      Extending the private sector input retailer network.  Limited
access to inputs has been identified as a major constraint to agricultural
production. ThJs sub-component would enable 18 women retailers to become
suppliers of agricultural inputs, and would be implemented through the FAO
Fertilizer Project, attached to the Department of Agricultural Services,



- 20 -
which has already gained experience in identifying and assisting
entrepreneurs who wish to enter this field. Candidates would be selected
on the basis of their previous experience, track record, and geographical
location. Business training would be offered, and credit for farm inputs
would be supplied to the retailers. The retailers, in turn, would pass
credit on to customers at their discretion. For each woman retailer, the
project would provide a Technical Sales Agent for two years to assist in
book-keeping, input distribution, and liaison with farmers. A study on the
proposed private retailer network would be undertaken as soon as possible
to determine the kind and amount of financial support that may be needed to
enhance the prospects for women retailers.
3.11      Support for women's Cooperative groups.  The Gambia Cooperative
Union (GCU), the only source of institutional credit for small farmers in
The Gambia (para 2.07), recently embarked upon a program to make
agricultural loans (both seasonal and medium term inputs) available to
target groups, particularly women farmers, that are traditionally under-
represented in access to GCU credit. GCU presently uses the village branch
lending system for credit disbursement; thio system transfers decision-
making and responsibility for loan recovery to the village level and
strongly relies on the traditional authority and inherent social pressures
in rural society. In successive years, credit would be provided to
villages only if the overall rate of repayment exceeds 95Z. During the
1989 season, GCU provided credit to five women's groups on an experimental
basis. The WID project would encourage this activity by providing
institutional support financing for up to seven specialized women's credit
officers; they would identify and liaise with the women farmer groups
deemed suitable for GCU credit.
3.12      Strengthening food storage, preservation and nutrition
extension. The Food and Nutrition Unit (FNU) of the Department of
Agricultural Services has a mandate to work with women on post-harvest
storage, preservation, and processing of food. FNU is characterized by
limited staff capability, insufficient recurrent funding, and inadequate
equipment. Under this sub-component, an institutional linkage would be
established with ar. appropriate technology organization, with technical
assistance provided during the start-up phase of the project. The project
would finance the development of extension materials, provide further
training for FNU staff, and provide logistical support as well as basic
office, laboratory and kitchen equipment. FNU would collaborate with the
Nutrition Unit of the Ministry of Health in an outreach program to avoid
seasonal malnutrition, with particular emphasis on maternal malnutrition
and also with the Agricultural Engineering Unit on developing and adapting
improved food-processing equipment.
3.13      Improving management of small-scale post-harvest equipment.
Post-harvest processing of cereals is among the most arduous and time-
consuming duties of rural women (para 2.06). Several hundred coarse-grain
mills, coarse-grain dehullers and rice dehullers have been distributed to
villages under various donor projects. Most of this equipment appears to
be non-functional because of maintenance and management problems. The
Catholic Relief Services (CRS) has been involved since 1983 in testing
post-harvest equipment and training village mill management committees.



- 21 -
CRS is now withdrawing from direct involvement in such development
activities. To ensure the continuation of those activities and that
maximum development impact is obtained from the existing stock of mills and
dehullers, the Project Coordinating Committee, in conjunction with TANGO,
will identify a suitable NGO or a private sector organization to build on
the work undertaken by CRS by setting up village management committees and
training local operators and mechanics.
3.14      Mobilizing women's savings.  Little emphasis has been given thus
far to savings mobiMzation, although individual savings could achieve many
of the objectives of seasonal credit. There is evidence of considerable
unmet demand for secure institutional savings mechanisms in The Gambia.
One advantage of providing improved savings facilities is that they would
be a safe means of helping women to keep control of the cash they earn and
to use it for productive purposes -- frequently difficult to do in a social
setting where there are strong pressures on women to 'lend' their earnings
to husbands or other extended household members. The Gambia Women's
Finance Company (GWFC), a local NGO, would, under a two-year renewable
technical assistance contract, help organize and educate existing women
groups for purposes of promoting and improving savings practices,
familiarizing these groups with available savings options and facilitating
their access to the regular banking channels. GWFC would not have any
fiduciary responsibility under this sub-component. GWFC would prepare a
detailed work program for IDA's review.
3.15      Agricultural Component Coordinator.  An Agricultural Component
Coordinating Unit, with a senior full-time Coordinator, would be
established within the Program Division of the Ministry of Agriculture.
The project would include provisions for establishment of the Unit as well
as its operating costs.
3.16      The project would finance: (a) office and laboratory furniture
and equipment, including cold chain and kitchen equipment for the Food and
Nutrition Unit (FNU), the Department of Livestock Services (DLS), the
Department of Agricultural Services (DAS), and the Agricultural Component
Coordinating Unit; (b) 72 motorcycles for extension workers, women
retailers, and saving mobilization agents; 11 vehicles (4-WD pick-up
trucks, etc.) for FNU, DLS, DAS, NGOs and the Agricultural Component
Coordinator, and vehicle operating and maintenance costs; (c) crop
extension manuals, training and library materials, and livestock drugs; (d)
training programs and workshops, regional study tours for extension
workers, field trips for women's groups, training abroad for two
horticultural supervisors, three Jiploma courses in the area of livestock
laboratory, and three university courses toward a Bachelor's degree in food
science and nutrition; (e) travel allowances for extension workers; (f)
stationery and communication materials; and (g) international technical
assistance to (i) assess and evaluate the private sector input distribution
programs, (ii) assist FNU in planning and launching its activities (iii)
help implement a program of institutional strengthening of village cereal
processing, and (iv) evaluate the savings mobilization campaign.



- 22 -
2. Skills Development
3.17      This component focuses on improving the skills of rural women
and promoting viable and marketable skills; organizing and strengthening
women's groups in rural areas to undertake small business and income-
generating activities; and promoting basic literacy and numeracy skills
through training activities related to women's social and economic needs.
To attain these objectives, the skills development component comprises
three sub-components, summarized below and described in detail in
Annex 3-2.
3.18      Strengthening Government Departments and Participating Agencies.
The Department of Community Development (DCD) in the Ministry of Local
Government and Lands would be the main implementing agency for the skills
development component. The main participating agancy would be the
Department of Nonformal Education (NFE) in the Ministry of Education,
Youth, Sports and Culture. To achieve the objectives of the skills
development component within the context of the WID project, the technical
and managerial capabilities of the professional staff need to be
strengthened and the physical infrastructure improved. Hence the main
activities of this sub-component would be: the training of national staff
of DCD and NFE (Skills Component Coordinator, CDOs and NFE Divisional
staff) in project implementation and management techniques; study tours in
neighboring countries for national level staff (selected personnel of DCD,
NFE and Divisional CDOs), followed by debriefing workshops in Banjul, vhich
would include field-level CDAs; Division-level orientation workshops for
Division Commissioners and members of Village Development Committees
(VDCs); in-service and refresher training for 50 CDAs and retraining of
home crafts assistants (HCAs).
3.19      Skills Development Program.  Under this sub-component, as the
main operational activity of the skills development component, a skills
development program would be undertaken to improve the economic
productivity of women, increase their income, and enhance their social
status. The program would target about 10,000 women in 125 villages
nationwide, to be selected from among the larger group of 200 project
villages before the implementation phase begins in Gambia's five
administrative Divisions. Existing women's kafo groups would serve as
entry points for the skills development component. Trained CDAs would
facilitate the formation of other groups interested in initiating new
business activities based on identified market opportunities. The CDAs
would also use the kafo groups as entry points to identify and select women
tutors or other appropriate persons from the villages to promote basic
numeracy and literacy. A total of 125 tutors would be trained for three
months over a period of six to seven months. The skills development
program would improve and upgrade existing skills as well as introduce new
ones. It would offer training in technical skills, business management,
improved production methods and technologies, and the capacity to use
credit. Skill training for income generation and for functional literacy
would commence simultaneously, so that both of these activities reinforce
each other and have maximum impact. Sustainability would be maintained
through the use of existing facilities and of local personnel in the



- 23 -
identification of training needs, training activities, management, etc., so
that the program may continue uninterrupted in following years.
3.20      Improved Access to Credit.  This sub-component for improving
access to credit for rural women is a crucial addition to the skills
development component. Credit would be extended to women participants in
the skills development program who have acquired specific skills. Women's
groups as welU as individuals would be considered. A small-scale credit
scheme, not exceeding a total of US$600,000, would be funded under the
skills development component.
3.21      Monitoring and evaluation would be a continuous activity carrled
out throughout the life of the skills development component and its
interrelated credit scheme. The three technical assistants for the
