component, together with their Gambian counterparts, would develop
performance indicators. To ensure that the monitoring and evaluation
system is understood and properly implemented by all concerned, it would
form part of the curriculum for the training of national staff, Division-
level personnel, and field workers in workshops.
3.22      The project would finance: (a) equipment and furniture for DCD,
village classrooms, and an office for the credit specialist; (b) post-
literazy materials for 10,000 women and production of booklets for the
skills development program; (c) S0 motorcycles, 10 vehicles for DCD, and
ope.ating and maintenance costs of these vehicles; (d) in-service and
refresher training for 50 CDAs and retraining of HCAs; (e) 10 person-
months of study tours in Africa; (f) the services of the following
specialistss credit (9 person-months), training (12 person-months) and
small business (36 person-months); (g) training for 125 tutors, and their
salaries for the life of the project; (h) a US$600,000 credit scheme; and
i) stationery for offices and workshops.
3. Safe Motherhood and Family Planning
3.23      The objectives of this component are to reduce the current high
maternal mortality rate and the incidence of malnutrition in the target
villages, and improve the use of modern contraceptives nationwide. The
strategy proposed for achieving these objectives includes: the training of
TBAs, CHNs and outreach health personnel to improve identification and
management of at-risk mothers well before delivery; establishment of an
'alarm evacuation system to transfer high-risk pregnancies and emergencies
to facilities where more sophisticated care can be provided; a nutrition
education and micro-nutrient supplementation program; and the expansion of
family planning (FP) services. The activities of this component would
expand and strengthen the village based health care system; they would
complement the activities undertaken in the National Health Development
Program (para 2.16) and improve the link between village communities and
the public health services, both with respect to the patient referral
system and to staff supervision and support. Some of the proposed measures
are innovative and would therefore be introduced on a pilot basis. The MCH
Unit in the Ministry of Health's Medical and Health Department would have
major responsibility for implementation of this component.



- 24 -
3.24      The safe motherhood and family planning comprises four sub-
components: (a) risk identification and management; (b) local evacuation
and telecommunications; (c) women's nutrition; and (d) family planning.
These are described in Annex 3-3 and summarized below.
3.25      Identification, Management and Referral of High-Risk
Pregnancies. In-service training would be provided for 200 TBAs, 40-50
CHNs, and 45 trekking team members to provide better antenatal, perinatal
and post-natal care for all pregnant women in the target villages and to
improve risk identification and referral. In addition, 400 untrained TBAs
would be given training in sterile delivery techniques and risk
identification/management, they would be integrated into the national PHC
system. An evaluation of ongoing TBA programs is expected to be conducted
before the end of 1990. Lessons learned from this evaluation would be
incorporated into the in-service training program. Approaches currently
used by NG0s -- use of traditional women's groups (Kabilos) based on family
ties to identify and refer high-risk pregnancies and simple pictoral cards
to assist the TBAs in the identification and referral of high-risk
pregnancies -- would be tested in the context of the public health services
before the Government integrates them into the national PHC system. Under
this sub-component, 35 sub-dispensaries would be refurbished and/or
reconstructed. Fifteen trekking teams would also receive new equipment.
In addition, health posts would be built in 10 isolated villages.
3.26      Alarm and Evacuation System.  Animal-drawn carts would be
provided for some 105 remote villages for the emergency evacuation of
pregnant women to a point where motorized transport is available. The
carts would be designed to improve comfort and safety (i.e. built with
cushioned pads and side barriers). They would belong to the Village
Development Committees (VDCs), which would appoint a caretaker for the
animals and carts; some use of the animal (e.g., for farming activities)
would be granted to the caretaker as an incentive for caring for the
animal. The VDCs would closely monitor the use and the maintenance of both
animal and cart. The system would be first tried out in 21 villages and
only if it proves successful would the system be extended to the remaining
villages. The existing telecommunications facilities would be expanded to
provide communication between the health centers and key villages in the
Upper River Division, one of the country's most isolated and unserviced
areas.
3.27      Women's Nutrition.  This sub-component comprises: (a) a
nutrition educational program to promote changes in behavior that will
improve the nutrition and health of high-risk groups, with particular
emphasis on the nutritional needs of pregnant and lactating women; and (b)
a micro-nutrient supplementation program for these women. Recipes for
weaning food and maternal supplements using local ingredients would be
developed under the project with the assistance of FNU. Qualitative
research on food practices and beliefs, a study on vitamin A deficiency and
market and household shelf studies would be undertaken to guide the design
and development of the nutrition educational program. The project
activities would complement and support the ongoing food supplementation
program of the Catholic Relief Services (CRS). A multi-media approach
would be used for the nutrition educational strategy, but special emphasis



- 25 -
would be given to participatory communication techniques (e.g., use of
local musicians, problem dramas, role playing) and inter-personal
communication. Financing would be provided for workshops, meetings and
seminars at various administrative levels to support the educational
process. All community outreach workers (health, agriculture, CDAs and
NGOs) would be included in the program.
3.28      Family Planning.  This sub-component complements activities
envisaged under a UNFPA-financed project to strengthen family planning
services. It comprises the following activities: (a) expanding the modern
contraceptive distribution network 'n both rural and urban areas; (b)
providing community health education, including family life education; (c)
enlisting the private sector in the safe and effective distribution of
contraceptives; (d) undertaking a contraceptive prevalence survey jointly
with UNFPA; and (e) financing a study on infertility. Educational
activities would, in particular, address the issue of male attitudes toward
family planning and the impact of religious beliefs on FP practices. Sixty
new community based distribution (CBD) sites would be established under
this sub-component -- 40 by the Medical and Health Department in rural non-
PHC areas, using TBAs as CBD agents, and 20 by the Gambia Family Planning
Association (GFPA) in the Greater Banjul area. Financing would cover the
training and supervision of the GFPA agents and the TBAs.
3.29      The project would finance: (a) the upgrading of 35 existing
dispensaries used as trekking stations by the MCH outreach clinics and the
construction of 10 new health posts; building operating and maintenance
costs; (b) 105 animals and carts; one 4-WD vehicle, three double cabin
pick-ups, eight motorcycles and their operating and maintenance costs for
the nutrition education and family planning programs; (c) equipment and
furniture for the outreach stations and new health posts; radio equipment,
solar-power modules for four base stations and 10 outstations in the Upper
River Division; td) training (TBAs, CHNs, trekking teams, kabilos, and
extension workers); (e) foreign and local specialist qervices (for studies
and surveys as well as a health nutrition education specialist); (f)
nutrition and planning workshops and studies; and (g) training and
educational materials and micro-nutrients.
4. Information, Education and Communication (IEC'
3.30      This component would provide IEC support to the other project
components -- agriculture, safe motherhood and family planning, and skills
development -- as well as generally promote the Ftatus of Gambian women.
This support would include the training of front-line workers in
communication techniques, the production and dissemination of IEC
materials, research on communication issues related to implementation of
the WID project, and infrastructure development to ensure sustainability.
3.31      The IEC component has two principal objectives: promote
awareness among men as well as women about the needs and concerns of
Gambian women and their role in the country's socio-economic development;
and identify, mobilize and utilize communication resources accessible to
women in promoting the goals of the WID project. Specifically, the IEC
component aims to: (a) create awareness and initiate dialogue among policy-



- 26 -
makers and 'gatekeepers concerning the needs and concerns of women; (b)
provide communication support for the field activities of the line
ministries; (c) provide infrastructure support for the production and
broadcast agencies to make them more responsive to development needs; (d)
establish a communication model in 30 villages to enhance learning and
accelerate improvement in women's status in general; (e) provide research
support for all communication activities to ensure quality production and
efficient dissemination and to enhance learning; (f) provide a feedback
mechanism to enable popular participation in the formulation, design and
implementation of communication activities through traditional as well as
modern media and to serve as a source of information for policy-makers; and
(g) train a cadre of professionals in all the concerned ministries to
familiarize them with communication processes and thus enable them to plan,
implexent and evaluate IEC activities.
3.32      To achieve its objectives, the IEC component would rely on four
public sector institutions, whose responsibilities and activities in
implementing the component comprise its four sub-components, described in
detail in Annex 3-4 and summarized below. The Women's Bureau would
coordinate all IEC activities, both centrally and in the field. The units
responsible for IEC within each sector would be the Agricultural
Communication Unit (ACU) of the Ministry of Agriculture for the
agricultural component; the Health Education Unit (lEU) of the Ministry of
Health for the safe motherhood and FP component; and the Nonformal
Education Department of the Ministry of Education for the skills
development component.
3.33      The Women's Bureau and Central Coordination.  Central
coordination responsibility for the IEC component would be assigned to the
Bureau's IEC Unit, which would coordinate the scheduling of campaigns and
media production, assist in the formulation and delivery of messages, and
supervise overall technical progress of the IEC component. The IEC Unit
would also assist the PMU in ordering and installing equipment for
implementing the component. As part of IEC infrastructure development, two
technical specialists would be recruited. One would serve as the
Coordinator of the IEC component for a period of three years; he would be
assisted by and train a Gambian counterpart, and would be recruited prior
to project start-up to help prepare a detailed IEC plan and assist in
procurement of electronic equipment. The other specialist would be
recruited to oversee maintenance and repair of the electronic equipment and
to train Gambian counterparts; he wculd be recruited in the second year of
the project and serve two years.
3.34      The Women's Bureau would initiate an innovative program in which
solar-powered VCRs and monitors would be set up in video halls in 30 of the
project villages. Educational video showings would be prepared by each
project component's Film Unit and screened at the video halls on a regular
weekly basis. Field personnel from the concerned ministries would coincide
their visits with showings of their material so that they may respond to
questions or stimulate discussion of women's issues. The video halls would
be under the care of organized women's groups in each village. Commercial
videos could also be shown, for a charge, by the women's associations, with
the income used for maintenance, rental of commercial video tapes, or other



- 27 -
minimal recurrent expenditures. It is anticipated that 10 video halls vill
be established in each of project years two, three and four (Annex 3-4,
para 8). The Women's Bureau would also coordinate the activities of 200
radio-listening clubs, print production (posters, pamphlets, etc.) for all
four sub-components, and all IEC-related research. It would be responsible
for a visual literacy survey, to be undertaken prior to project start-up,
and for other qualitative surveys at a later date. These surveys would
serve as a baseline for assessing attitudes and behavior concerning women;
this in turn would aid in message development and in monitoring progress in
improving women's status in general. The project would include IEC
activities aired at eradicating harmful traditional practices, such as
female circmIcision.  The Women's Bureau would organize workshops. training
of TBAs and radio listening groups. The Bureau would assist the production
agencies in pre-testing their materials prior to mass producticn and
distribution. It would also undertake a longitudinal survey to compare the
30 villages with video halls with control groups to determine what impact,
if any, easy access to media may have on educational, social, economic and
anthropological variables. The Bureau would organize four IEC workshops.
and host seminars and conferences for sharing information and exchanging
views on the role of women. A workshop would be held during each of the
first four years of the project. Technical assistance for the IEC
component, including short-term TA (total of 10 person-months), would be
managed by the Bureau. Production of materials, except those for the
Agricultural Communication Unit, would be delegated to local production
agencies (Annex 3-4, para 12).
3.35      Radio Gambia would provide radio support for all key project
activities. This would include preparation of radio programs, production
of single-issue series (e.g., women's health), radio spots, duplication of
tapes for use by the radio-listening clubs, and other serv4.ces as needed.
Radio Gambia would be provided with technical training to enable it to
undertake audience surveys to improve program quality.  The PMU would
assist Radio Gambia in purchasing and installing a transmitter during the
first year of the project in order to extend the radio signal to the whole
country.
3.36      The Agricultural Communication Unit (ACU).  With an educational
system already in operation (including studio and video tape and print
production facilities), ACU has an important role in the IEC component. It
would produce video programs in support of its field activities,
coordinating them with the Women's Bureau for viewing in the 30 villages to
be provided with video halls. ACU would support the field activities of
extension workers, training them in the communication process and supplying
them with IEC materials.
3.37      Two workshops are scheduled, one to develop prototype materials
for field workers and the other to test these materials prior to
duplication. A flip-chart would be produced for each of the 200 project
villages, along with pamphlets and posters on a variety of agricultural
topics. ACU would provide IEC support for the agricultural component's
savings mobilization scheme (para 3.14), as part of an annual social
mobilization campaign to recruit new savers and later campaigns to
encourage savers to remain in the scheme.



- 28 -
3.38      The Health Education Unit (HEU).  HEU would provide IEC support
for the safe motherhood and FP component.  Since many of t'e issues
involved in disseminating safe motherhood con:epts are addressed by UNFPA,
USAID and other agencies, HEU would focus on activities not covered in
other programs. The main activity would be nutrition education, for which
allocations have been made for staff training, preparation of print and
electronic materials, and field activities. The topics for the video tapes
related to nutrition would include nutritional deficiency diseases, weaning
practices and infant nutrition, oral rehydration therapy, prenatal
nutrition, and other topics (Annex 3-4, para 27). A materials development
workshop would be held in project year two. The Food and Nutrition Unit
(para 3.12) would be provided with audio-cassette equipment to enable the
staff to make field recordings; these would serve not only as a feedback
mechanism but also as a resource for production of tapes by Radio Gambia.
3.39      In addition to r trition education, HEU would provide materials
to be used in the training of traditional birth attendants (TBAs), covering
topics such as pregnancy complications and safe delivery. In all of its
health education activities, HEU is expected to make maximum use of the
video halls and radio-listening clubs (Annex 3-4, paras 8 and 9).
3.40      The Nonformal Education (NFE) DePartment.  This Department would
manage IEC r_ztivities in support of the skills development component, most
of which would comprise the preparation of print materials in support of
literacy and numeracy programs. Three workshops would be held to train
literacy teachers in use of the IEC materials. Technical assistance would
be provided to help prepare and pre-test prototypes and train artists to
create illustrations to be used in the literacy/numeracy programs. The
Department would purchase 60 audio-cassette recorders for use during
literacy sessions, and would have access to the 200 radio-listening clubs
and their equipment. One member of the staff would accompany the four-
person team that during the project start-up phase would visit Niger to
observe rural solar-videos in operation; the other members would be the IEC
Coordinator, an ACU staff member, and an HEU staff member.
3.41      Items to be financed under the IEC component include: (a) three
4-WD vehicles for supervision, field recording and equipment maintenance
and their operating and maintenance costs; (b) audio and production
equipment and operating and maintenance costs; (c) IEC materials (audio-
cassette recorders, tape recorders, etc); (d) in-country training
(seminars, workshops) and training abroad; (e) the services of
international specialists (IEC coordinator, maintenance/repair engineer,
short-term specialists) and local specialists; (f) studies and research;
(g) office supplies; (h) production of IEC materials for village
consumption; and (i) travel allowances for personnel involved in IEC
delivery.
5.   Strengthening of the Women's Bureau
3.42      In addition to the technical and logistical support to be
provided to the implementing agencies under the various project components,
special emphasis would be given to strengthening the capacity of the
Women's Bureau for policy and program formulation, advice, awareness



- 29 -
creation, and monitoring and evaluation of the impact of women's
development programs. The assistance to the Bureau currently provided by
UNIFEM would be continued and expanded.
3.43      This component comprises two sub-components6  general
strengthening of the Women's Bureau, and monitoring and evaluation. These
are described in detail in Annex 3-5 and summarized below.
3.44      General strengthening of the Women's Bureau.  This involves its
organizational structure and staffing as well as the training of staff, and
includes:
(a)  Reinforcement of the Research Unit with a research program
focusing on small-scale and special surveys to supplement
national data programs;
(b) Some decentralization of the Bureau's activities through the
work of field officers, training of women leaders, and
organization of meetings, workshops and seminars in the Regions
to expand the Bureau's outreach, gain better understanding of
women's needs, and facilitate research and data collection;
(c) Mounting of nationwide educational and awareness campaigns on
women's issues;
(d) Strengthening of the Bureau's capability to collaborate and
negotiate with other agencies to ensure that women-oriented
activities are included in their programs;
(e) Improvement of the Bureau's documentation and reference service;
(f) Enhancement of the Bureau's communications program; and
(g) A new headquarters building for the Bureau, together with
furniture and equipment.
3.45      A Monitoring and Evaluation Unit would be established within the
Women's Bureau and staffed by a full-time Monitoring and Evaluation Officer
and two assistants. Before start-up of the WID project, the Women's Bureau
would conduct a baseline survey of women residents in the 200 project
villages and a nationwide knowledge, attitudes and practices (KAP) survey
of both men and women. Tha baseline and RAP surveys would be repeated in
the final year of the project and the baseline survey would also be
repeated at post-project stages. The RAP survey has particular relevance
to the project's IEC activities. Additionally, throughout the project
period a series of small-scale project services monitoring surveys would be
conducted in target areas and communities to assess the effectiveness of
specific project interventions. These surveys would be carried out by the
M&E Unit in collaboration with the implementing agencies, and would be
particularly useful to the WID Project Management Unit for project
monitoring. The entire evaluation program is designed to be compatible
with and make use of other data-collection activities that would proceed in
parallel in The Gambia, particularly the permanent household survey (to be



- 30
conducted under the Social Dimension of Adjustment Project) and the
national agricultural sample survey. The impact evaluation program would
be an integrated program, using both primary and secondary sources of data
and phased over time, to provide the basis for assessing the project's
effects and impact. A Monitoring and Evaluation Committee, chaired by the
WID Project Manager and with the Bureau's M&E Officer serving as secretary,
would coordinate the design and activities of the various monitoring and
evaluation systems and the dissemination of information.
3.46      The project would finance:  (a) construction of a headquarters
building for the Women's Bureau, together with furniture and equipment; (b)
two 4-WD vehicles, including one for the M&E Unit, and 10 motorbikes for
the Bureau's field officers; (c) publications and documentation; (d) six
person-months of internationally recruited consultants for research and
monitoring/evaluation, and 18 person-months of local consultants for
research; (e) training of the Bureau's staff and women leaders, and costs
of seminars and workshops; (f) salaties of a researcher, two planners. an
editor, a documentalist, a social scientist. 10 field officers, a
monitoringlevaluation officer, and two monitoringlevaluation assistants;
5g) costs of the baseline, KAP and other surveys; and (h) vehicle operating
and maintenance costs.
6. The Fund
3.47      While the project's sectoral components are designed to address
women's most important and urgent needs and concerns, they cannot take care
of all the concerns and needs of Gambian women. To remedy that
shortcoming, the project would include a Fund, with an amount set at
US$500,000, to be used by NGOs, on a matching fund basis, for a variety of
village-level activities, small projects and programs designed to help
women and children. Possible areas of intervention include digging wells,
providing credit or inputs to women in rural areas, and enabling NGOs to
expand ongoing successful activities beyond the areas in which they
normally operate.
3.48      The Fund would be administered by the Project Management Unit
(PHU) in accordance with procedures and eligibility criteria satisfactory
to IDA. The Project Manager would review the relevant proposals and make
recommendations to the Project Coordinating Committee (PCC), which would
approve the individual grants. During negotiations the Government gave
assurances that (a) it will make available to the participating NGOs
portions of the amount allocated to the Fund, under agreements to be
eLtered into between the Government and the NGOs on terms and conditions
satisfactory to IDA; and (b) IDA will have the right to review and approve
(i) the first three proposals of NGOs for any particular activity (credit,
well-digging, etc.) regardless of the amount involved; and (ii) any
proposal in excess of US$40,000 equivalent. An outline of the functioning
of the Fund, including a list of eligibility criteria that could be used,
is provided in Annex 3-6. During negotiations, substantial agreement was
reached with the Government on that outline, but the Government wishes to
obtain the views of the NGOs before finalizing the details. A condition of
credit effectiveness would be the submission to IDA of details,



- 31 -
satisfactory to IDA, on the management and administration of the Fund,
including eligibility criteria.
E.   Project Management Unit (PMU)
3.49      Although each executing ministry or agency would be responsible
for implementing its component, overall management and coordination of the
project would be the responsibility of a Project Management Unit (PMU)
established in the Office of Lie President and under the overall guidance
of a Project Coordinating Committee (PCC) (para 4.05). The PMU staff would
comprise a Project Manager, a Deputy Project Manager, a Project Accountant,
an accounts clerk, a secretary and other support staff.
3.50      The project would finance (a) two vehicles and their operating
and maintenance costs; (b) technical assistance for procurement; (c) audits
of the project accounts; (d) costs of attendance by the ?MU staff at
conferences and seminars; and (e) salaries of the Project Manager, Deputy
Project Manager, and Project Accountant.
F. Project Costs and Financing
3.51      The project cost, at current prices, over the five-year project
period is estimated at US$15.1 million, of which US$9.2 million, or 61?, is
estimated to represent the foreign exchange component. The base cost would
be US$12.5 million. Physical and price contingencies are estimated to
amount to US$2.6 million; the physical contingencies include 10? for
physical investments and 5 for training, technical assistance and
operating costs. Price contingencies assume (a) a domestic inflation of 8?
p.a. for the first five years and 6.5? for the sixth 7ear, and (b) an
international inflation of 3.9? p.a. in the first five years and 3.7? p.a.
in the sixth year.  The table below shows project costs by component;
detailed project costs are shown in Annexes 3-7 and 3-8.
Component                                 Cost (US$ million)
Agriculture                                    3.2
Skills Development                             2.8
Safe Motherhood and Family Planning            1.2
Information, Education and Communication       2.1
Strengthening of the Women's Bureau            1.2
The Fund                                       0.5
Project Management Unit                        0.3
Refinancing of PPF                             1.2
Total Baseline Costs                          12.5
Physical and price contingencies               2.6
Total Estimated Project Costs                 15.1



- 32 -
3.52      IDA has allocated US$7.0 million toward the financing of this
project. The Government is expected to contribute US$3.8 million
equivalent, or 52 of project cost. The remaining US$7.3 million would come
from the African Development Fund, Norway, UNDP, UNIFEM and UNFPA.
IV. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. Status of Prolect Preparation and Readiness
4.01      Following the Women in Development Workshop (para 3.01)
organized by the Government to define a WID strategy, preparation of the
project started in 1988 and was entrusted to local task forces assisted by
consultants. Under the Project Preparation Facility (PPF) an amount of
US$530,000 was approved in June 1988; a PPF Supplemental Advance of
US$640,000 was approved in February 1990.
4.02      The project preparation by the task forces is generally of high
quality. The scope and design of the project components, as well as
implementation and management and coordination issues, were discussed at a
Consolidation Workshop held in Banjul, August 28-September 1, 1989. The
recommendations of the workshop were published in a report 1/, which
outlines action plans, responsibilities and implementation schedules, and
as such have been considered as part of the appraisal. During appraisal,
IDA reached agreement with the Government on the project objectives and
scope, including on some modification of the strategy and design of the
skills development component in order to reach the intended women
beneficiaries; the scheduling of that component has therefore been phased
with the start-up phase of the project.
4.03      Timetables of project activities are shown in Annex 4-1.  They
will be reviewed and revised as necessary by the various component
coordinators or managers in consultation with the PMU (para 3.49) once
their staff are in place.
4.04      The funds provided under the PPF are for:
(a) Establishment of the PMU, including the employment of a Project
Manager, a Deputy Project Manager and a Project Accountant, and
the services of a specialist for the procurement of goods (para
3.49); and offices for the Agricultural and IEC Coordinators
(paras 3.15 and 3.33);
(b) Preparation of a detailed proposal for making funds available
for use by NGOs under the Fund component (para 3.48);
lllmplementation Coordination for the WID Project -- Results and
Recommendations of the Consolidation Workshop, 29 August-l September
1989, Banjul, The Gambia (September 1989).



- 33 -
(c) Firming up of the Women's Bureau research program, the
architectural design and preparation of tender documents for the
Bureau's headquarters building (para 3.44); and the undertaking
of baseline and UAP surveys by the Women's Bureau (para 3.45)
and a visual literacy survey by its IEC Unit (para 3.34);
(d) Completion of pilot programs under the agricultural component
(para 3.09), a study on the private sector retailer network
(para 3.10), and preparation of a work plan proposal by GWFC for
savings mobilization (para 3.14);
(e) An evaluation of TBA/MCH services (para 3.25) and a
contraceptive prevalence survey (para 3.28) under the safe
motherhood and FP component; and
(f) Technical assistance in the areas of income-generating
activities, establishment of a credit scheme, and skill training
design, curriculum development and delivery methods under the
skills development component (para 4.18).
B.   Project Coordination and Management, Monitoring and Evaluation
4.05      The project would be implemented under the overall guidance and
supervision of the Project Coordinating Committee (PCC), established in
July 1989. The Committee is composed of the Permanent Secretary, Office of
the President, as Chairperson; the Permanent Secretary of MEPID as Vice-
Chairperson; the Permanent Secretaries of the Miinistries of Finance and
Trade, Agriculture, Education, Local Government and Lands, Information and
Tourism, Health, Labor,the Environment and Social Welfare; the Chairperson
of TANGO; the Executive Secretary of the Women's Bureau; and the Project
Manager, who serves as Secretary of the Committee. The PCC would have an
important role in the fields of policy formulation and review, the
expediting of policy decisions, especially of an inter-ministerial nature,
and the resolution of any major implementation problems that may arise.
Specifically, the Committee would approve work programs and budget for each
component, review semiannual and annual progress reports and initiate any
corrective action that may be necessary, appoint and supervise senior
project personnel, and approve individual grants under the Fund component.
The PCC would meet LL least four times a year, but during the first year of
the project it is expected to meet monthly to facilitate project
implementation.
4.06      While implementation responsibility for each project component
rests with the executing ministry or agency, overall management and
coordination of project activities is the responsibility of the Project
Management Unit (PMU), established in the Office of the President (para
3.49). The Permanent Secretary, Office of the President, would oversee the
PMU's activities, which would be oriented toward monitoring, coordination,
and support to the executing ministries and agencies (including interface
between them, the PCC and the cofinancing institutions). The PMU would
consolidate the annual work plans and budgets tor all project components
for submission to the PCC for approval; supervise implementation of the



- 34 -
approved work plans; and have authority, through the PCC, to trigger
corrective action. In addition to its general advisory role, the PMU would
have an important role in ensuring that expenditures correspond to project
activities and that resources acquired for the project are effectively
used. Specifically, the PMU would be responsible for: (a) procurement and
disbursement; (b) maintaining the project accounts and making arrangements
for audits; (c) monitoring semiannual, annual, and other reports that may
be required by the PCC and cofinanciers (the Project Manager would chair
the Monitoring and Evaluation Coordinating Committee (para 3.45); and (d)
administering the proposed Fund (para 3.48). Joint IDA/Goverrment reviews
of project implementation would be carried out on an annual basis. Such
reviews are designed to examine the progress of implementation to-date,
discuss the results of the previous year's work program and agree on the
following year's work program and budget. Donors supporting the project
would be invited to participate in the annual reviews. During negotiations
the Government gave assurances that the joint annual review of project
implementation will be held not later than April 30 of each year, on the
basis of appropriate documentation to be prepared by the PMJ.
4.07      The Project Manager would be working full time on the project
and would be assisted by a Deputy Project Manager; the PMU staff would also
include an accountant, an accounts clerk, a secretary and other support
staff (para 3.49). Following advertising for the position, the Government
has appointed as Project Manager the former Secretary of the Women's
Bureau, a Gambian who has demonstrated a strong commitment to the promotion
of the welfare and status of women. During negotiations, agreement was
reached on the final terms of reference for the PCC, the Project Manager
and the Deputy Project Manager. The Government also provided assurances
that the PMU positions (including that of a full-time Project Manager) and
those of the Coordinators for the agricultural, skills development, safe
motherhood and FP, and IEC components and of the M&E officer will be filled
at all times with personnel whose qualifications and experience are
satisfactory to IDA. The appointment of the Deputy Project Manager, the
four component Coordinators and the M&E officer would be a condition of
credit effectiveness.
4.08      Because most of the project components have sub-components, they
require some form of coordination. For each component, coordination would
also be required at the village and Division level. At the village level,
the critical coordination issue is to avoid that various project
interventions create competing demands on women's limited time. The
starting point for project coordination is therefore the establishment of
annual priorities by the Village Development Committees (VDCs).   At the
Divisional level, coordination of actions in support of women's priorities
is the principal issue and the structure to be used under the project would
be the Divisional Coordinating Committee (DCC), which already exists.
Composed of tecbnical representatives of the relevant line ministries, the
DCC would review the village's priorities and assist them in organizing and
scheduling their activities and in preparing annual budgets.
4.09      The management and coordination arrangements for each project
component would be as follows: (a) The Fund component would be managed by
the PMU under the supervision of the PCC; (b) the component for



- 35 -
strengthening of the Women's Bureau would be managed by the Bureau's
Executive Secretary, with the assistance of the M&E officer and the M&E
Coordinating Committee chaired by the Project Manager; (c) for the
agricultural component there would be a Coordinator and an Agricultural
Component Advisory Committee (ACAC); (d) for the skills development
component there would be a Coordinator and a Management Unit, with the
Department of Community Development (DCD) in the Ministry of Local
Government and Lands as the main implementing agency, and the Department of
Nonformal Education (NFE) in the Ministry of Education, Youth, Sports and
Culture as the main participating agency; (e) for the IEC component there
would be ar. internationally recruited IEC Coordinator with a Gambian
counterpart, reporting to the Executive Secretary of the Women's Bureau;
(f) for the safe motherhood component the Head of the MCH Unit in the
Health and Medical Department, Ministry of Health, would be the
coordinator, and for the family planning sub-component GFPA would be the
main participating agency. A Safe Motherhood Component Advisory Committee
(SMAC) would also be established, and would include the NGOs involved in
implementation of the component
4.10      Although there would be variations amoi,g the components, the
main implementing agency or the Coordinator, assisted as the case may be by
a Committee, would generally be responsible for:
(a) Firming up and consolidating detailed work plans and budgets for
the particular component. The plans should specify the
quantified project inputs and outputs of the respective agency,
the target villages, and plans for in-house monitoring and
reporting.
(b) Coordinating timely delivery of inputs to the implementing
agencies.
(c) Monitoring project performance by maintaining contact with the
project implementers, attending workshops and seminars,
undertaking field visits, reviewing and consolidating progress
reports, and liaising with the M&E Unit in the Women's Bureau
for evaluating the impact of the component activities.
(d) Providing mechanisms to promote dialogue among the implementing
agencies at the national level (through the Component Advisory
or Coordinating Comr.ittee), as well as at the regional and local
levels (in coordination with Divisional staff and VDCs).
(e) Preparing semiannual progress reports on the component's overall
progress and, in the light of experience, suggesting any
necessary changes in the component activities. The progress
reports should describe the component's current status, major
activities undertaken during the reporting period, any problems
encountered, and plans for the upcoming quarter; they should
include estimates of the number of beneficiaries assisted, cost
effectiveness, and any social changes (in terms of behavior or
attitudes).



- 36 -
(f) Coordinating implementation of the component with the other
project components.
4.11      There are multiple collaboration requirements among and/or
within Ministries and the various agencies for implementing the project
components and sub-components. Whenever such collaboration requirements
affect entities or units within the same Ministry, as for example in the
case of the agricultural component, it would be the task of the component
Coordinator to ensure that those units work together in a timely and
orderly manner. Whenever the collaboration involves different ministries,
the implementing agencies should enter into cooperative agreements; these
should specify quantified outputs and objectives of the cooperation (to be
used in monitoring and evaluation), personnel and material inputs by each
participating agency, each agency's work plan and timetable of operations,
and respective implementation responsibilities. The signing of cooperative
agreements for the collaboration required under each of the following
components would be a condition of disbursement for expenditures made by
the respective agencies:
-    For the safe motherhood and family planning component: between
the Ministry of Health, Environment, Labor and Social Welfare
(MHELSW) and the Gambia Family Planning Association (GFPA); and
-    For the IEC component: between the Women's Bureau on the one
hand, and Radio Gambia, the Book Production and Material
Resources Unit (BPMRU), the Film Unit, the Agricultural
Communication Unit (ACU), the Health Education Unit (HEU) and
the Nonformal Education (NFE) Department on the other hand.
During negotiations, an understanding was reached that, in all the cases
where a formal cooperative agreement is required between a project agency
and a Ministry or government department for the execution of a part or
component of the project as a condition of disbursements for that project
agency, the PMU will not submit any withdrawal application for expenditures
for the whole component unless such an agreement has been signed.
4.12      Monitoring and evaluation would be a continuous activity,
beginning before project start-up and carried out during the entire life of
the project and even beyond. This very important sub-component is
described in detail in Annex 3-5. The system calls for the monitoring of
activities by each implementing agency, which would submit semiannual
progress reports to the component's manager or coordinator, who would in
turn report to the PMU. The M&E Unit of the Women's Bureau would conduct a
baseline survey, a KAP survey, and project services monitoring surveys to
evaluate the actual effects and impact of the WID project on women and
assess its success in enhancing of women's issues and changing attitudes
with regard to the role of Gambian women and the extent to which changes in
traditional practices have taken place. Plans for the progress reporting
system and the impact evaluation program (including indicators for inputs,
outputs, effects and impact) need to be firmed up through a collaborative
effort by the PMU, each component's managing or coordinating unit, the M&E
officer and the M&E Coordinating Committee, once they are fully staffed and
operational.



- 37 -
4.13      For certain activities, specific evaluations and actions would
be undertaken during the project period. In the case of the agricultural
component, the M&E Unit, in collaboration with the implementing agencies,
would undertake at the end of the second project year an assessment of the
impact of the crop extension services and the extent to which women farmers
are being reached by the input retailer network, as well as an evaluation
of the program for improving management of post-harvest equipment (prior to
a decision on contract renewal). External evaluations are also scheduled
after two full years of operations to examine the implementation and
effectiveness of the savings mobilization campaign of the Gambia Women's
Finance Company and to assess the potential for further expansion of the
women's retailer network. Monitoring and evaluation would be of critical
importance in the skills development component. The system would be
refined during project start-up; it would form part of the curriculum for
staff training and include representatives of the various women's groups
involved. In the case of the IEC component, to determine whether the IEC
activities are on course and to ensure program flexibillty, qualitative
surveys are built into most of them; they comprise simple focus group
discussions, supplemented by close contacts with the field, the production
agencies, and the concerned ministries. The surveys are expected to
provide information on, for example, the effectiveness of the management
structure, the timeliness of project interventions, the appropriateness of
the methods used, and the quality of production. Under the Fund component,
any proposal should have a monitoring and evaluation plan that reflects
compatibility with the M&E programs of the project's sectoral components to
which the proposed NGO activity would relate. In the case of the safe
motherhood and PP component, the M&E Unit, in collaboration with the MCH
Unit, would undertake at the end of the project third year an assessment of
the animal-drawn cart scheme before deciding whether to expand it to the
remaining 84 villages. An evaluation of the impact of the new TBA training
program is also scheduled for the fifth year of the project.
4.14      In addition to the above-described monitoring and evaluation
built into the project, the Government, IDA and the cofinancing agencies
would undertake a mid-term evaluation of the progress of project
implementation, including an assessment of whether the strategies being
followed are the correct ones. During negotiations the Government gave
assurances that it will undertake a mid-term evaluation of the progress of
project implementation. Also, the Government and IDA would annually review
the achievements and performance of the previous year's work program and
agree on the following year's program and budget (para 4.06).
C.   Implementation of the Project Components
4.15      Many NGOs attach great importance to women's development issues
and are already active in a number of programs of particular interest to
women (para 2.29). During project preparation, the desirability and
modalities of NGO participation in the project have been extensively
studied and discussed. A number of NG0s (such as SCF, GWFC, and GFPA) that
would participate in implementing some of the project components have
already been identified, and the proposed Fund (para 3.47 and 3.48) would



- 38 -
be exclusively for NGOs. Also, the participation of NGOs in the sectoral
components would be considered in light of the extent to which they have a
comparative advantage for certain activities. A consultant's report
(August 1989) on NGO participation includes suggestions on possible
modalities of such participation. At the time of appraisal, although the
Government had not yet taken a decision on the consultant's
recommendations, work had started on preparing a statement of policy
regarding NGO participation and drafting a standard contract between the
Government and participating NGOs. The draft statement of policy indicates
that it is the Government's declared intention to carry out its development
policy in collaboration with NGOs that have expressed interest in providing
assistance in their particular field of competence and within their
technical and financial capabilities. The progress of that work was
reviewed during negotiations. A condition of credit effectiveness would be
the adoption of (aj a statement of government policy regarding NGO
participation satisfa;tory to IDA, and (b) a satisfactory form of a
standard contract between the Government and participating NGOs.
4.16      Most of the project components include allowances for training,
both on-the-job training inside The Gambia and training abroad. Experience
has shown that key staff of ministries and agencies have sometimes been
sent abroad for somewhat long periods of training without adequate
arrangements being made for their replacement. To avoid a situation where
facilities provided under the project could no, be properly used for lack
of essential staff, IDA should review in adva- :e and approve the project-
financed training programs for key staff. r ing negotiations the
Government gave assurances that IDA will have an opportunity to review in
advance and to approve the training program for key staff under the WID
project.
4.17      It is assumed that the IDA credit will be effective and the
project will start in October 1990. Timetables of activities for most of
the project components are shown in Annex 4-1 (para 4.03). A project
launch seminar would be held before project start-up to ensure that all
Gambian parties to the project are fully aware of their role in
implementation. In agriculture, the sub-components to be implemented by
government departments (DAS, DASIHEP, DASIFNU, and DLS) are ready for
implementation, but timely start of the activities would be dependent upon
availability of inputs, such as vehicles and office equipment, particularly
for those agencies presently not adequately equipped (e.g. HEP and FNU) and
the contractors. The NGO included in the agricultural component, GWFC, has
been asked to present detailed work plans and budgets, on the basis of
which the Government would negotiate a contract with it. Similar
procedures should be followed with the Gambia Cooperative Union (GCU) in
its request for institutional support. A search is underway for a
competent NGO or private sector organization to implement the post harvest
equipment sub-component. Results of the study on the women's retailer
network should be available soon to allow for timely implementation with
the project start-up; otherwise, transitional arrangements will have to be
made. For the food storage, preservation and nutrition extension sub-
component, as soon as possible FNU should enter into a cooperative
agreement with an established food and nutrition institute that would
provide technical assistance for FNU.



- 39 -
4.18      For the skills development component, financing has been
provided under the PPF for an initial period to provide technical
assistance for the development of income-generating activities,
establishment of a credit scheme, and training program design. Recruitment
of the technical assistants and appointment of their Gambian counterparts
should take place as soon as possible.
4.19      For the safe motherhood and PP component, a consultant for
designing the TBA/MCH evaluation is expected to be recruited soon, and
funds have been provided under the PPF to start the exercise. The results
of the evaluation should be available by the end of 1990, to be
incorporated into the TBA training program. Timely and successful
implementation of the PP sub-component is contingent upon the Ministry of
Health and GFPA agreeing on a program of activities to undertake jointly.
A technical committee has been set up, composed of staff of the Ministry of
Health and GFPA, to formulate a new memorandum of understanding between the
two agencies, which would include an action plan for activities that the
Ministry and GFPA would undertake either jointly or ir cooperation. During
negotiations, an understanding was reached that the PMU will not submit any
withdrawal applications to IDA for expenditures for the safe motherhood and
FP component unless such a memorandum of understanding has been signed. To
assist GFPA and the Ministry of Health in implementing the FP sub-
component, a launch workshop would be organized in Banjul.
4.20      For the IEC component, a coordinator and a Gambian counterpart
should be appointed as soon as possible to assist the concerned line
Ministries in preparing detailed work plans. Preparations should then be
made to begin the visual literacy survey, as it is essential to the
development of much of the print materials scheduled for production. The
survey should be extensive and take into account such variables as language
groups, distance from Banjul, and access to other media. Besides gathering
data on visual literacy, the survey would undertake a micro-communication
assessment. The study tour to Niger (para 3.40) headed by the IEC
Coordinator should also take place as soon as possible, as the tour would
enable the visiting team to observe any problems encountered in that
country with solar-video equipment or with program production, as well as
success factors. The IEC Coordinator should also review the electronic
equipment needs of the project, in coordination with Radio Gambia and the
Film Unit, so that orders may be placed as soon as possible.
4.21      Regarding the component for strengthening of the Women's Bureau,
the most urgent actions to be taken prior to project start-up are the
employment of an internationally recruited consultant to firm up the
Bureau's research program and the carrying out of the baseline and KAP
surveys, for which financing is available under the PPF. The M&E Unit and
the M&E Committee should be established and staffed as soon as possible to
finalize the monitoring system. Following completion of the baseline and
KAP surveys, a one-week workshop should be held to present their findings,
as well as clear up any outstanding issues in the design of the monitoring
system and start planning the first project services monitoring surveys.



- 40 -
4.22      With regard to the Fund component, financing has been made
available under the PPF to finalize details on the procedures and operation
of the proposed Fund.
D. Environmental Impact
4.23      The WID project is expected to have a beneficial impact on the
environment. Women are in the forefront of the struggle with the harsh
environment in The Gambia: they most often are the ones who have to find
and carry fuelvood and potable water, ensure the family's food supply, and
care for the children.
4.24      The project would contribute to improving environmental
management through four of its components. The agricultural component
would provide support for improved cultivation of field crops, livestock
management, horticulture and other agriculture-related activities, which
are of critical importance in improving land management. The family
planning sub-component addresses another major environmental issue -- the
country's rapidly increasing population, whose food supply has to be
obtained from a constant land base. The FP programs would have an impact
by helping to bring about a reduction of the population growth rate over
the long term. The need to improve the skills of rural women, along with
their understanding of environmental issues and constraints, would be
addressed through the skills development component, in whose training
programs water conservation and use and household energy concerns can be
included. The IEC component would also make a significant contribution to
enhancing awareness and improving understanding of environmental issues.
Through all the IEC activities, environment-related topics and information
on techniques for managing the environment can be disseminated. Women have
a major role in managing the Gambian environment, and the WID project would
reinforce and support that role.
E.   Specialist Services
4.25      Consultants for the WID project would be selected in accordance
with the Bank's Guidelines for the Use of Consultants, with qualifications
and experience acceptable to IDA, to provide various forms of technical
assistance in a number of areas. The specialist services required would
total about 337 person-months, of which about 180 would be for locally
recruited specialists. This would include individual consultants and NGOs
active in the country.
4.26      The NGOs would either act as executing agencies or assist line
Ministries and agencies in fulfilling their implementation
responsibilities. International specialist services would include: the
services of an IEC Coordinator for the Women's Bureau (36 person-months); a
maintenance/repair engineer for overseeing the IEC equipment (24 person-
moaths); a number of short-term specialists to work with the Food and
Nutrition Unit (FNU) of the Ministry of Agriculture in developing a
strategy and work plan, preparing training materials, and training FNVT
staff in food storage and processing techniques (total of about 9 person-



- 41 -
months); a monitoring and evaluation specialist and a researcher for the
Women's Bureau (total of 6 person-months); credit, training, and small
business specialists for the skills development component (57 person-
months); audit and procurement specialists for the Project Management Unit
(total of about 13 person-months); and a number of short-term consultants
to assist the Gambian staff in organizing workshops and conducting
quantitative and qualitative surveys.

4.28      Contracts for goods, including equipment -- educational
(teaching materials, booklets, video-tapes), electrical (cold chain, radio
antennas, household equipment), transportation (4-WD vehicles and
motorcycles), and miscellaneous (tools, household appliances) -- totalling
US$1.2 million equivalent, representing about 90? of the total value of
contracts for goods financed by IDA, would be procured through
international competitive bidding (ICB) in accordance with the Bank's
Guidelines for Procurement Under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits (May 1985) and
would be grouped, to the extent practicable, into bid packages of at least
US$100,000 each. Where ICB procedures are used, a preferential margin of
15?, or the applicable customs duty, whichever is less, over the c.i.f.
prices of competing goods would be given to domestic manufacturers in
accordance with IDA's guidelines.
4.29      For the remaining 102 of the total value of goods and for all
civil works contracts financed by IDA, totalling US$0.4 million, about
three packages would be suitable for procurement other than ICB. The
exceptions to ICB procurement would be for (a) contracts for goods (limited
to cffice supplies and furniture) estimated to cost less than US$100,000
equivalent each, which in the aggregate would not exceed US$0.1 million;
and (b) a contract for construction of the headquarters for the Women's
Bureau, which would not exceed US$0.3 million. These items, which are not
expected to attract foreign bidders because of their small size, would be
awarded on the basis of competitive bidding advertized locally (LCB) in
accordance with procedures acceptable to IDA, provided that (a) the
aggregate amount of such procurement does not exceed US$0.5 million
equivalent; (b) pre-qualification is required for the civil works contract;
(c) the conditions under which pre-qualification takes place, as well as
the methods and criteria used, are acceptable to IDA; (d) the tenders are
advertised locally and bidders are allowed a minimum of 45 days to submit
bids; (e) all bids are opened in public; (f) evaluation criteria are
specified with sufficient clarity -- price, capability to perform, adequacy
of equipment and personnel, and financial capacity; (g) eligible foreign
firms not incorporated in The Gambia are allowed to participate in the
bidding; and (h) draft standard bidding documents have been reviewed and
approved by IDA prior to approval and signing of the first contract.
4.30      Incrimental operating costb (including spare parts, office
supplies, repairs and consumables) which cannot be grouped into bid
packages of at least US$20,000 equivalent would be procured through prudent
international and/or local shopping on the basis of price quotations
obtained from at least three reliable suppliers to ensure competitive
prices, provided that the aggregate amount of such procurement does not
exceed US$0.6 million equivalent and the procedures applied are acceptable
to IDA. Limited international bidding (LIB) by direct invitation without
open advertisement, which could be an appropriate method of procurement in
cases where the amounts are small and there are a limited number of
suppliers of the particular item, would be subject to prior IDA approval on
a case-by-case basis. Procurement from UNICEF, or through an NGO would be
in accordance with procedures acceptable to IDA including procedures
requiring goods to be purchased at a reasonable price.



- 44 -
4.31      For contracts for goods estimated at more than US$100,000
equivalent each, the bidding documents and proposed contract awards would
be reviewed by IDA prior to decision on the final awards. It is estimated
that this review would cover about 85Z of the total value of the amount
contracted for goods and works financed by IDA. For goods and civil works
contracts estimated at less than US$100,000 each, representing about 152 of
the value of these contracts, all procurement decisions would be subject to
IDA's selective post-award review.
4.32      Consultants (para 4.25) and training financed by IDA, totalling
US$2.2 million eciuivalent, would be contracted in accordance with the
Bank's Guidelines for the Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by
the World Bank as Executing Agency (August 1981).
4.33      The Government has decided to seek the assistance of a
procurement specialist to advise on procurement and prepare bidding
documents. During negotiations the Government gave assurances that it will
apply the procurement procedures and arrangements outlined above.
G.   Disbursements
4.34      Because the WID project is the first of its kind, there is no
sector disbursement profile that could be used as a reference. The IDA
credit is expected to be disbursed over a period of six years. The
estimated quarterly disbursement schedule is shown in Annex 4-2.
4.35      The IDA credit would be disbursed on the basis of categories
shown below.

4.36      Special Account.  To expedite project implementation, a Special
Account denominited in US dollars would be opened at the Central Bank of
The Gambia in Banjul to cover IDA's share of eligible expenditures. The
authorized allocation of the Special Account would be US$400,000,
representing anticipated eligible emenditures for four months. IDA would
make an initial deposit of that amount from the proposed credit immediately
upon credit effectiveness. Replenishment of the Special Account would be
made on the basis of full documentation except for expenditures valued at
less than US$20,000 equivalent, which would be against Statements of
Expenditure (SOEs). Replenishment requests should be submitted monthly
provided that the amount is for a minimum of US$50,000. All IDA
expenditures of less than US$20,000 would be financed from the Special
Account; the same limit would apply to requests for direct payment. The
documentation for withdrawals under SOEs would be retained at the PMU for
review by IDA staff during supervision missions and for regular semiannual
audits.
H.   Accounting, Auditing and Reporting
4.38      The PMU would maintain the project accounts, which should
aggregate expenditures incurred for each project component. With financing
provided under the PPF, the PHU has already employed an accountant, whose
qualifications and experience are satisfactory to IDA. The PMU would
submit to IDA: (a) semiannual progress reports on project implementation,
(b) annual financial statements on project expenditures, (c) annual work
programs and budgets (as approved by the PCC) for the following year; and
(d) a project completion report within nine months of the credit closing
date. Project accounts, including the Special Account, would be audited
annually by independent auditors acceptable to IDA; all disbursements under
SOEs would be audited semiannually. The audit reports would be submitted
to IDA within six months of the end of the fiscal year. During
negotiations an understanding was reached that the Government will appoint
independent auditors as soon as possible to assist the PMU in setting up a
system of accounts, including for the proposed Fund.
V. BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. Benefits
5.01      Women all over The Gambia, particularly those living in rural
areas, would benefit from the institutional strengthening of organizations
dealing directly with women's issues (i.e., the Women's Bureau, the
Ministries of Agriculture, Education, Health, and Local Government). The
project would help improve these institutions' responsiveness to women's
needs and create a greater awareness about women's development issues among
policy-makers; this would contribute to better sectoral project design over
the long term. The project coordination efforts would improve inter-
agency cooperation and harmonization of existing women's programs. A
number of project activities would be targeted to women in 200 selected
villages, with a population of direct beneficiaries estimated at 50,000.



- 46 -
Rural women nationwide are expected to benefit from the improved extension
services and the private farm input distribution network. Equally, the
family planning program would benefit both rural and urban women. The
entire community, especially children, would benefit from women's improved
health and education. Over the long term, women's enhanced role in the
country's development effort would contribute to better overall economic
performance and to poverty reduction.
B. Risks
5.02      The risks involve primarily (a) the difficulties in changing
society's perception of the role of women and the attitudes of civil
servants dealing with women's issues; (b) IDA's and the Government's lack
of experience in preparing and implementing a project of this nature; and
(c) the difficulty of implementing a project with a multi-sectoral
approach. Nevertheless, the Government's strong commitment to implementing
a VID project, the large-scale involvement of local professionals in
project preparation, and the planned full-scale IEC campaign included in
the project design are expected to provide a favorable climate for
successful implementation. By involving experienced NGOs, together with
technical assistance, the risk of unsatisfactory implementation would be
reduced.
VI. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
6.01      During negotiations, the Government and IDA reviewed and agreed
on:
(a) The list of the 200 project villages (para 3.05); and
(b)  The final terms of reference for the Project Coordinating
Committee (PCC), the Project Manager and the Deputy Project
Manager (para 4.07).
6.02      During negotiations, the Government gave the following
assurances:
(a) The Government will increase the percentage of contact farmers
who are women to 102, 15?, 20?, 25S and 302 by June 30, 1991,
1992, 1993, 1994 and 1995, respectively, and will increase the
percentage of block demonstration participants who are women to
15?, 202, 25?, 30S and 352, respectively, by the same dates
(para 3.07);
(b) The Government will prepare and transmit to IDA by December 31,
1990, for discussion a national strategy paper, including
strategy at the divisional level, aimed at increasing the impact
of its agricultural extension program on women and ensuring
achievement of the above-described percentage increases in the



- 47 -
number of women contact farmers and women block demonstration
participants (para 3.07);
(c) The Government will make available to the participating NGOs
portions of the amount allocated to the Fund, under agreements
to be entered into between the Government and the NGOs on terms
and conditions satisfactory to IDA (para 3.48);
(d) IDA will have the right to review and approve (i) the first
three proposals of NGOs for any particular activity (credit,
well-digging, etc.) regardless of the amount involved; and (ii)
any proposal in excess of US$40,000 equivalent (para 3.48);
(e) The joint annual review of project implementation will be held
not later than April 30 of each year, on the basis of
appropriate documentation to be prepared by the Project
Management Unit (para 4.06);
(f) PMU positions (including that of a full-time Project Manager)
and those of the Coordinators for the agricultural, skills
development, safe motherhood and FP, and IEC components and of
the M&E officer wi'.l be filled at all. times with personnel whose
qualifications and experience are satisfactory to IDA (para
4.07);
(g) The Government will undertake a mid-term evaluation of the
progress of project implementation (para 4.14);
(h) IDA will have an opportunity to review in advance and approve
training programs for key staff under the WID project (para
4.16); and
(i) The procurement procedures and arrangements agreed during
negotiations will be applied during project implementation (para
4.33).
6.03      The following would be conditions of credit effectiveness:
(a) Submission to IDA of details, satisfactory to IDA, on the
management and administration of the Fund for small projects and
programs, including the eligibility criteria (para 3.48);
(b) Appointment of the Deputy Project Manager, the four component
Coordinators, and the M&E officer (para 4.07); and
(c) Adoption of (i) a statement of government policy regarding NGO
participation, satisfactory to IDA; and (ii) a satisfactory form
of a standard contract between the Government and participating
NGOs (paza 4.15).
6.04      The signing of cooperative agreements for the collaboration
required under each of the following components would be a condition of



- 48 -
disbursement for expenditures made by the respective project agencies (para
4.11):
(a)  For the safe motherhood and family planning component: between
the Ministry of Health, Environment, Labor and Social Welfare
(M4ELSW) and Gambia Family Planning Association (GFPA); and
(b)  For the IEC component: between the Women's Bureau on the one
hand, and Radio Gambia, the Book Production and Material
Resources Unit (BPMRU), the Film Unit, and the Agricultural
Communication Unit (ACU), the Health Education Unit (HEU) and
the Nonformal Education (NFE) Department on the other hand.
6.05      Recommendation.  Subject to the above terms and conditions, the
proposed project would be suitable for an IDA credit of US$7.0 million
equivalent to the Republic of The Gambia on standard IDA terms, with 40
years' maturity.
