REPUBLIC OF THE GAMBIA
WOMEN IN DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
THE ROLE OF WOMEN IN GAMBIAN SOCIETY
A. Current Status
1.        Women comprise 44Z of the total labor force in The Gambia and
30-50S of the agricultural labor force, and are responsible for about 4OZ
of total agricultural production. They contribute substantially to the
production of both food and cash crops. In addition, they spend long hours
every day processing and preparing food and supplying most of the basic
needs of their familiest child rearing, health care, clothing, shelter,
water, and fuel. All these activities keep women busy up to 16 hours a day.
This substantial contribution to the country's economy has thus far gone
largely unnoticed because it is unpaid. Data on health, nutrition,
education, and economic performance indicate that the status of Gambian
women remains low and inferior to that of men.
2.        The high maternal mortality rate of 10 per 1,000 live births,
among the highest in the world, is not only a tragedy for the women
concerned, but also for their families because of women's role as food
producers and overall providers for their families. Studies conducted in
The Gambia have shown that young children whose mothers die often do not
survive. Because of too frequent pregnancies, insufficient caloric intake,
and their heavy workload, women in rural areas also suffer from under-
nutrition, as evidenced in the high proportion of low birthweight babies
(as high as 23-24Z in some rural areas). In a developed country the death
of an infant is usually an accident; In the developing world, it is a
process begun with the mother before its birth.
3.        Nationwide, the female literacy rate is much lower than the
literacy rate for men. As in most of the other African countries, boys
outnumber girls in the primary schools by a factor of 3 to 2; in the more
remote rural areas, enrollment of girls is even lower (about 30Z in the
Lower River Division). The dropout rate for girls is likewise much higher
than for boys. Women who have more education work in Government, health
care, and teaching, but their number remains very small. Few women hold
leadership positions in The Gambia. For instance, there are no women
agriculturalists at the senior level in the Ministry of Agriculture,
although some trained women nutritionists have posts in the Ministry's Food
and Nutrition Unit.
4.        In sum, poor health, limited education, and inadequate access to
productive resources and basic social services lock Gambian women into a
high fertility/low productivity pattern which in the end damages the well-
being of their families and that of the entire community.



-54-                             ANNEX 2-1
Page 2 of 8
B. Airiculture
5.        The Gambian farming system is rixaracterized by a fairly complex
and diverse gender division of responsibilities and resources within the
extended, polygamous family households, which have an average size of 15
persons. Both men and women are expected to produce food for household
consumption (maruo crop). This is usually done collectively under the
responsibility of the male household head in case of upland cereals or a
senior woman in case of swamp-rice production. In addition, both men and
women have the right to grow crops individually for their own use
(kamanvanto crops), a large share of which is sold. The income from these
sales is maintained separately. Gambians greatly value their personal
crops, from which they meet a range of expenditures. In addition to
personal outlays, men are expected to use their income for purchasing food
to cover household shortages, for payments of compound taxes, and for
construction and housing maintenance. Men often invest their surplus
income in cattle, draft animals, and agricultural inputs. Women are
expected to contribute food, particularly condiments, for the family and to
meet their personal needs and those of their children, including health
care, school fees, and clothing. Women prefer to use their savings for
investments in small ruminants and personal ornaments.
6.        Women play a prominent role in the Gambian farming system.
Depending on village location, they provide between 302 and 50 of
agricultural labor, and more than half of women of working age cultivate
their own kamanyanao fields. For instance, women are practically the sole
producers of swamp and tidal rice, horticultural crops, and lately, sesame.
But gender-specific responsibilities for certain crops and agricultural
activities vary widely in the Gambian farming system, depending among other
factors on location, ethnicity, and farm mechanization level. In villages
with access to lowland swamps along the river (lowland villages'),
particularly Mfandinka villages in the western and central areas of the
country, women traditionally cultivate swamp rice as a subsistence and cash
crop, although recently women in those areas also embarked on groundnut and
horticultural production. Men in these villages focus on cereal production
(millet, maize, sorghum) for household consumption and groundnuts for cash
income. The introduction of rice irrigation schemes in some parts of the
country during the 1970s and early 1980s induced men to shift to rice
production in the villages concerned, but this trend has been reversed due
to the continuous deterioration of these systems. In contrast, in the
upland villages, which rely exclusively on rain-fed upland crops (upland
cereals, groundnuts, cotton), situated along the border to Senegal and in
the eastern part of the country, both men and women grow groundnuts, cotton
and some sesame as individual (kamanvango) crops. Household food
production (maruo) in these villages is largely the responsibility of the
household head and other males. Women engage mainly in the tedious
domestic threshing and processing of these food crops.



- 55 -                           ANNEX 2-1
Page 3 of 8
7.        In general, female and male agricultural activities are more
separated in the lowland villages, where mutual assistance between men and
women is less common and where women usually spend more time on farming
than in the upland villages. Women in the upland villages, on the other
hand, tend to be more integrated into men's farming activities. There is a
considerable amount of labor exchange in upland crops: men often assist
women with the heavier tasks of land clearing and lifting of groundnuts,
and wherever animal traction is used (ploughing, planting, weeding).
Women, in turn, provide labor for harvesting and sometimes for weeding. In
general, no payments are made for work on communal fields, but farmers
often pay each other in cash or kind for assistance on individual fields.
In addition to crop and horticultural production, women own and manage a
large proportion of the country's sheep, goat and poultry stock.
8.        To improve the productivity of their agricultural work, women
require the usual resources--inputs, technology and information--but they
are highly constrained in their access to them. The overall lack of timely
availability of inputs, such as fertilizer and improved seeds, is a major
obstacle to increasing women's productivity. This is a problem that
affects all farmers, but men usually have priority access through the
Cooperative system, which is presently the major input distributor and only
source of institutional agricultural credit in rural Gambia. Less than 1O0
of the 110,000 members of the Gambia Cooperative Union (GCU) are women;
the fact that GCU has been mainly involved in the marketing of groundnuts,
a male dominated crop, partly explains women's low membership in the Union.
Virtually excluded from the formal credit system, most rural women borrow
and save in the informal sector--i.e., from friends and relatives or
through local women's groups who pool their money and then distribute it
for consumption or investment purposes. This rotating fund, called osusu,
is normally used by one group member at a time and provides only limited
funds for its members. It also cannot accommodate all the members' needs
at the same time (e.g., for buying farm inputs at the beginning of the
planting season).
9.        GCU also provides loans for agricultural implements, a major
factor in improving agricultural productivity in The Gambia. Women's
limited access to these loans has thus far restricted their benefiting from
agricultural mechanization, although 702 of Gambi-an farm households have
access to animal traction and women's upland fields often are mechanically
cultivated by their husbands or sons. But men, as the owners, have
priority in the use of agricultural implements, and timely task performance
has a critical impact on yields and consequently on overall productivity.
Moreover, women in the lowland villages rarely can make use of these
implements on their swamp-rice fields, since machines either may not be
appropriate or constraints in timing do not permit their simultaneous use
on upland and lowland fields. Women often are constrained by traditional
land rights. In principle, women, as do men, acquire land by clearing it
nd their right to land is recognized; in practice, however, they often are
prevented from enjoying this right. Traditional law favors compound and
village ward rights to land over individual rights; therefore, the
assignment of land to individuals often lies in the hands of the male
compound head or the village elders. Women may end up with land of
inferior quality or may even be driven off their traditionally held land.
This was, for instance, the case in irrigated rice projects during the



- 56 -                          ANNEX 2-A
Page 4 of 8
1970s, when technical assistance was directed mainly at men and women
frequently lost control over fields they had previously cultivated.
10.       Women's access to information on agricultural innovations or
techniques is also more limited than men's, because extension services and
agricultural research have been mainly oriented towards the predomiuantly
male cash and export crops. The IDA-supported Second Agricultural
Development Project (Cr. 1476-GM) is presently adapting the Training and
Visit (T&V) system of agricultural extension to the Gambian situation;
however, women constitute only 4-5 of the 4,100 'contact farmers' because
the contact farmer selection criteria include literacy and very few rural
women are literate. Given women's disadvantages in access to inputs and
technology, it does not come as a surprise that studies undertaken in The
Gambia show that women's productivity level is, on average, 351 inferior to
that of men. While part of this productivity gap certainly can be
explained by the heavy domestic workload and frequent pregnancies that
limit female labor input in agriculture, experience under the IFAD-assisted
Jahally-Pacharr Rice Project in recent years shows that when Gambian women
are given the necessary inputs their productivity matches that of men. But
this project has also shown that programs focused on women need to
carefully consider intra-household relationships. The Jahally-Pacharr
project was exclusively designed for women farmers. But despite
considerable efforts by the project's planners to improve women's access to
new rice technologies, most women ended up with control over the lower-
yielding technologies; men, on the other hand, took over responsibility for
the highest yielding, pump-irrigated rice technology. Although women still
benefited indirectly from the project through increased household food
security because most rice under male control was grown as 'maruo' crop,
the project clearly failed to improve women's disposable income. This
demonstrates the difficulty of attempts to singlehandedly target women,
even if the crop concerned is a traditional 'women's crop'.
C. Education and Training.
11.       Although The Gambia has considerably expanded its education
system since the 1960s and enrollments of boys and girls have improved over
the years, women still have limited access to education and training
opportunities. Against a total primary school enrollment of 682 in 1982,
female enrollment represented only 381. There are also considerable
regional variations in enrollment, with urban areas showing a greater
female participation in formal education than rural areas. The
representation of girls is even lower at the higher education levels. Boys
outnumber girls nearly 3 to 1 in secondary school and 5 to 1 in secondary
technical school. The Gambia has no university. Severe budgetary cuts under
the structural adjustment program in the past six years have limited the
number of spaces and teachers available to cater to an ever-increasing
number of school-age children. School enrollment declined from 68Z in 1982
to 622 in 1988; there is evidence that girls have been more affected than
boys and that the disparities between them have increased.



- 57 -                           ANNEX 2-1
Page 5 of 8
12.       Even when girls get into school, a number of factors operate
against successful completion and achievements higher dropout rates for
girls than for boys because of competing demands on girls' time, as well as
the high opportunity cost of keeping a girl in schooll early marriage and
pregnancy; negative parental and community attitudes towards the Western
education of girls; and the poor quality of education plus its lack of
relevance to the expected vocation of girls. Limited places in primary
school, the absence of role models, and teacher attitudes toward girls,
scholastic abilities and potential also affect the enrollment of girls,
their performance and desire to continue in school. An additional factor
that contributes to the low educational attainment of girls in The Gambia
is the late age of school entry (eight years), giving girls only a few
years of schooling before puberty and little chance to complete school.
13.       A study has been undertaken to determine the cause of low female
enrollment to help prepare realistic and concrete solutions for
implementation' over the long term. Some of the study's recommendations
would be supported under the proposed Gambia education sector project
appraised in June 1989. The project would support measures such as the
lowering of the entry age for primary school from eight years to seven
initially and later to six yearst the active participation of communities
in the construction of schools close to the village to encourage parents to
enroll girls; new curricula promoting a more positive image of women's
role in society; and priority given to female teachers in training
programs.
14.       Nonformal education could be an alternative means of reaching
the large majority of women in The Gambia who have been unable to attend or
complete formal schooling. Nonformal education programs could provide
basic skills in health, family planning, sanitation, and literacy--all of
crucial importance in improving women's lives.  They could also provide
basic skill training to improve women's productivity, such as in
agriculture and marketing. A number of governmental and non-governmental
agencies offer training in numeracylliteracy and other skills (i.e., soap-
making, vegetable gardening, tye and dye, seed multiplication, batik
making, accounting and record keeping). Although not specifically geared
to women, the programs have placed particular emphasis on upgrading women's
skills. Among the government agencies active in this field, the Department
of Community Development (DCD) has the largest number of skill training
programs. Action Aid is the most active NGO in the sector, accounting for
almost half of the training programs offered. The number of participants
is, however, rather small: 23.284, according to a recent survey,
representing about 102 of the adult rural population. Women far outnumber
men in these programs, showing their desire and need for training; however,
their participation is more irregular than that of men, due to a large
extent to competing household and child care responsibilities (most women
attending skill training programs are in the 20 to 30 age group).
Functional literacy/numeracy programs were introduced in 1982 by the
Department of Nonformal Education of the Ministry of Education. These



- 58 -                           ANNEX 2-1
Page 6 of 8
programs have been severely constrained by a lack of qualified and
experienced trainers and teaching materials. A national literacy campaign
is a stated objective of the Government's educational policy; however, no
implementing strategy has yet been devised.
D. Women's Entrepreneurshin
15.       Women are very active in the informal sector. In urban areas
this includes retail marketing of fresh food, small restaurant services,
textile dying and tailoring for the domestic and tourist markets,
manufacture of tiles, and road transport. In rural areas the activities
are limited to marketing of surplus vegetables and crafts production.
Informal surveys suggest there is considerable scope for increasing women's
income from these enterprises, for example, through improving the product
or service, exploiting economies of scale, and introducing better business
management. In addition, there appears to be a potential for women to
branch out into new areas such as fish processing, large-scale horticulture
production and marketing, and food processing and preservation industries.
16.       People engaged in small business encounter a myriad of problems,
the main ones being finances, markets and access to raw materials.
Commonplace constraints in small-scale enterprises (SSEs) become actual
barriers for women, because of society's negative attitude towards women's
ability, their lack of training and business experience, and their
difficulty in obtaining credit. The bulk of institutional lending has
financed mostly wholesale and retail trade business and groundnut
marketing, in which few women are involved. While some women have received
commercial loans and have a better repayment performance record than men,
very few women participate in the formal financial system, either as savers
or borrowers. Small credit schemes for urban women have been provided by
the Cooperative Thrift and Credit Society, a sub-system of the cooperatives
(a top-down organization with little membership participation), and by the
Indigenous Business Advisory Service (IBAS), established under the Ministry
of Economic Planning and Industrial Development. Both these institutions,
however, are saddled with financial problems due mainly to poor management,
therefore ruling them out (as in the case of GCU) as channels for lending
under the proposed WID project. As the urban sector continues to expand
and formal jobs become more limited, greater attention must be given to
improving productivity in the informal sector, where the majority of self-
employed workers are women.
E. Health and Nutrition Status
17.       Women's health status in The Gambia is among the poorest in the
world, as evidenced by a maternal mortality rate of 10 per 1,000 live
births--more than twice the 4.5 average rate for developing countries (in
some rural areas of the country maternal mortality is as high as 20 per



- 59 -                           ANNEX 21
Page 7 of 8
1,000 live births)--and a high incidence of malnutrition. Besides
affecting their overall productivity and prospects for development, women's
poor health has profound implications for the welfare of the country's
children. The Gambia's infant mortality rate of 125 per 1000 births and
child mortality rate of 292 per 1,000 births are among the highest in Sub-
Saharan Africa.
18.       Post-partum hemorrhage and infections are the leading causes of
maternal mortality, while infant and child mortality are caused mainly by
malaria, diarrhea, and respiratory tract infections. Malnutrition has a
compounding effect on mortality and morbidity, particularly among pregnant
women and children. Medical research conducted in The Gambia has identified
the following main factors influencing the high maternal mortality rate:
inadequate access to antenatal care, absence of an effective early warning
system for high-risk pregnancies, unavailability of transport in an
emergency, and a first referral level that is not adequately equipped in
terms of trained manpower and equipment to deal with high-risk cases.
19.       Considerable improvement in the health delivery system is
expected under the National Health Development Program launched in late
1987 with the support of the World Bank and other donors, including the
Italian, British and Dutch Governments. The program focuses on a) expanding
and strengthening the various referral levels from the health centers to
the hospitals; b) training community health nurses (CHNs) and state
enrolled nurses (SENs); c) improving communications; d) providing an
efficient logistical support system; and e) increasing the availability of
essential drugs. The program is also helping to extend primary health care
(PHC) to the Greater Banjul area, hitherto under-served, and is introducing
cost recovery for drugs and health services to promote more efficient use
of health facilities and shift some of the cost of improved health services
to the beneficiaries.
20.       Malnutrition is widespread in The Gambia; the most severely
affected groups are pregnant and lactating mothers and children between the
ages of 9 and 36 months. The type of malnutrition is predominantly energy
deficiency. There are both regional and seasonal variations in the
incidence of malnutrition, with rural areas more affected than urban areas.
A considerable shortfall in caloric intake in rural areas occurs during the
rainy season, when less food is available. Studies by the Ministry of
Health indicate that rural Gambians consume an average of 2,140 calories
per person per day during the post-harvest season, but only 1,660 calories
during the rainy season; this represents a shortfall in caloric intake of
about 500 calories per person per day. The effect of this "hungry seasona
is exacerbated by the heavy agricultural workload borne by all active
members of the community, including pregnant and lactating women.



ANNEX 2-1
-60 -                          Page 8 of 8
21.       A high fertility rate (6.5) also weakens maternal health and a
woman's ability to provide for her children. The Government has been
supportive of family planning (FP) programs and PP services have been
provided in the country for a number of years, mainly by the private Gambia
Family Planning Association (GFPA), an offshoot of the International
Planned Parenthood Association, through clinics, mobile services and
community-based distributors set up in rural areas. The Ministry of Health
also provides PP services at public health facilities and, at the village
level, through Village Health Workers (VHWs) and Traditional Birth
Attendants (TBAs). Although the program has had some success (the Gambian
contraceptive prevalence is currently estimated at 6.52), it has faced
considerable constraints and problems that have so far impeded wider
acceptance. Among these constraints are the extremely low level of
literacy of the population, particularly women; considerable misconceptions
still entertained about family planning; and the bias toward large
families. Lack of coordination between the Ministry of Health and GFPA can
also be counted among the factors contributing to the limited success of
the Gambian family planning program. The Government is becoming
increasingly concerned about the rapid growch of the population, and public
pronouncements about the need to reduce the current rate of growth have
become more frequent recently. This needs to be followed by an action
program with quantitative targets for FP activities.

REPUBLIC OF THE GAMBIA
WOMEN IN DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
4GRICULTURAL COMPONENT
A.  Component Obiectives and Sub-components
1.        The main objectives of this component are to (a) increase
women's agricultural productivity; (b) enable women in rural areas to
increase their disposable income; and (c) improve the nutritional status of
women and children. The strategies developed aim to invigorate government
services, animate private initiatives, and stimulate local NGO development.
2.        The agricultural component consists of the following sub-
components:
(a) Strengthening of extension services for women's field crops;
(b) Strengthening of extension services for women's horticulture;
(c)  Strengthening of extension zervices for women's livestock
production;
(d) Promotion of a private sector female input retailer network;
(e) Support for women's Cooperative groups;
(f) Strengthening of food storage, food preservation, and nutrition
extension;
(g) Improvement of the management of small-scale post-harvest
equipment;
(h) A savings mobilization campaign; and
(i) Establishment of an Agricultural Component Coordinator's Office.
B.   Target Villages
3.        Some activities of the agricultural component focus only on
women in the selected WID project villages, while others may expand well
beyond these villages. Some villages may lack the need for a particular
activity, may not be suited to WID project activities, or may already be
served by alternative projects. In particular, WID project activities in
the Upper River Division should be carefully monitored if they are
compatible with the ongoing integrated rural development project in that
area.



- 62 -                     ANNEX 3-1
Page 2 of 13
4.        The number of villages to be served with specific activities in
each project year would be determined before project start-up by the
implementing agencies in their five-year work plans and updated in the
annual work plan and budget proposals. It is suggested that those sub-
components that are innovative or experimental in character should start
off with a few villages should be closely coordinated with the other
participating agencies and with the Agricultural Component Coordinator.
Special aspects concerning the selection of target villages are discussed
by sub-component in Section E below.
C. Summary of Proiect Activities by Sub-component
(1) Extension Services for Women's Field Crops
5.        Prior to the start of the 1991 rainy season, a sensitization
campaign would be carried out in each Division. Its objective would be to
publicize and implement the new policy directive for ensuring that the
extension services give equal emphasis to women's and men's agricultural
staff and women's group leaders and the provision of guidelines for
adaptation of T&V schedules for women's productive activities.
6.        Program planning and evaluation workshops would be implemented
semiannually. These workshops would initially target one District in each
Division, chosen according to its proximity to a female input retailer, and
gradually expand to other Districts. The workshops would evaluate the
previous season's act4rities and plan support for women's horticultural and
rainy season crop production activities. T&V schedules would be developed
taking into account of women's domestic and sociallreligious obligations.
Following each round of semiannual workshops, DAS staff would prepare a
comprehensive report summing up the results of the evaluation and each
Division's proposed program, for circulation to all DACs and the
Agricultural Component Coordinator (as a primary tool for in-house M4E).
7.        An annual national extension workshop for women's agriculture
will be hosted and coordinated by the DAS. The workshop would serve mainly
to provide field extension supervisory staff from all concerned government
and non-government organizations with an opportunity to exchange
information on and coordinate their activities. Awards would be made to
field staff for outstanding achievement. DAS would publish the workshop
proceedings.
8.        A set of extension manuals (for rice production, upland crops,
and horticulture), would be prepared for use by all extension workers,
agricultural teachers and volunteers in The Gambia. The DAS would have
overall responsibility for editing, formatting and publishing the manuals
and for coordinating technical inputs from DAR, FNU, REP, and the FAO
Fertilizer Project Staff. The manuals should be designed so that they can
be easily updated. Each manual's technical crop production information
would be supplemented by sections that specifically address women's socio-
economic and cultural problems in agricultural production. 'Vven women's



- 63 -                     ANNEX 3-1
Page 3 of 13
major involvement in rice and horticultural production it is suggested to
start off with the preparation of manuals for horticulture and rice
production. A detailed work plan and calendar for completion of the
extension manuals -- including the allocation of adequate manpower and
other resources by the participating agencies to this task -- should be
developed as soon as possible.
9.        The Ministry of Agriculture would prepare a detailed strategy
paper on measures to increase the impact of agricultural extension
activities on women farmers. This would include both individual extension
contact, and the use of campaigns and media, at both the national and (at
least) the divisional level. Numerical targets would be specified for
participation of women in extension activities as contact farmers and as
block demonstration farmers. To improve the operational performance of
extension staff by speeding up the maintenance and repair of motorbikes and
vehicles, a revolvina fund for spare Rarts would be set up and operated by
DAS, which would work out the detailed arrangements for the fund in
cooperation with the Agricultural Component Coordinator and the PMU in
project year one.
10.       DAS would be responsible for organizing block demonstrations
specifically designed for women farmers, and orchestrated with the efforts
to improve input availability through the female input retailer network and
women's Cooperative groups.  Quantified targets for the number of block-
demonstrations and women to be reached should be set up in cooperation with
the collaborating institutions (PAO and GCU).
11.       At the end of project year two there would be a comprehensive
evaluation by the M&E Unit of the Women's Bureau in collaboration with DAS,
to assess the outreach of the extension services to women and the extent to
which women farmers are reached by the input retailer network.
12.       The crop extension services would pay special attention to women
in selected WID project villages by concentrating the block demonstrations
and sensitization campaign, as well as the semi-annual program planning and
evaluation workshops, on these villages. Beyond this, women throughout The
Gambia would benefit from the proposed countrywide increase of women
contact farmers under the T&V system.
(2) Extension Services for Women's Horticulture
13.       All VEWs in the Western Division and Subject Matter Specialists
and Training Officers from other Divisions would receive specialized
horticultural training during the first year of the project in two
Integrated Horticultural Development Workshops. In succeeding years this
training would be extended to other Divisions and reinforced through the
semi-annual workshops of DAS. Prior to the start of the project, HEP staff
would develop a training program and materials based on the T&V system. in
collaboration with DAR horticultural research staff. The training
materials developed during the first year would provide the raw material
for a comprehensive horticultural manual (para 16). The VSO training



- 64 -                           ANNEX 3-1
Page 4 of 13
coordinator would liaise with DAS in preparing the manual for final
publication; it would be made avail&ble to all extension workers,
agricultural teachers and volunteers in the country.
14.       Two of the HEP supervisors are to be trained abroad to diploma
level under the WID agricultural component, with only one supervisor to be
away at one time to maintain continuity.
15.       To strengthen horticultural extension services for both
categories of producers, an expanded program of horticultural
demonstrations and trials would be implemented in collaboration with the
Horticultural Extension Program (DAS), the Horticultural Research Program
(DAR) and the Food and Nutrition Unit (FNU). The marketing of
horticultural produce would be facilitated through the development of a
market information system, in collaboration with the FAOIUNDP funded
Horticultural Marketing Economist, to provide market price information
through radio broadcasts. A concerted effort would be made to reduce
seasonal gluts by promoting staggered production and improving the flow of
market informatien.
16.       The extension s.rvices for horticulture would initially be
active mainly in the Western Division, which presently has the highest
concentration of horticulture, and gradually expand to other Divisions.
Since priority should be given to serving existing gardens not all the WID
proiect villages may qualify for activities under this sub-component.
However, in promoting the establishment of new gardens the horticultural
extension services should give priority to the WID project villages,
provided the villagers express interest in receiving the services.
(3) Extension Services for Women's Livestock Production
17.       The Department of Livestock Services (DLS) would develop an
effective and systematic approach to wide-scale vaccination of small
ruminants and poultry and to the delivery of basic messages concerning
improved husbandry practices. Pilot research and demonstration programs
have been started and would be expanded in year one project. These
programs should identify social and economic constraints to adoption of
husbandry practices, with special attention given to gender roles in small
ruminant and poultry production. Technical assistance in evaluating the
pilot programs and developing a model health care and improved husbandry
packaae delivery system would be obtained from the ITC/UNDP regional
projects on trypanotolerant livestock. It is suggested that cooperative
agreements between DLS and these institutions be made before project start-
up.
18.       Once the delivery systems have been tested and established, they
would be gradually replicated on a Divisional basis throughout the country
(including villages in URD which are presently not being reached by the
local URDID project). DLS would provide an appropriate timetable for
extension of the program to both WID project and non-project villages.
19.       A drug fund should be established to procure the necessary
drugs, vaccines, and medications. During a brief initial period the drugs



- 65 -                           ANNEX 3-1
Page 5 of 13
would be administered free of charge to demonstrate to farmers the benefits
of such interventions. Subsequently, the drugs would be sold by extension
staff for cash and the revenues used to replenish the revolving fund at
DLS. Details of drug fund administration would be worked out by DLS in
cooperation with the Agricultural Component Coordinator and the PMU in
project year one. This should include a critical assessment of the
necessary amounts for the fund in light of the gradual extension of the
program and experience with adoption rates of improved health care and
husbandry packages.
20.       Information gained from the existing pilot programs and the
training materials developed during the first year of the project would
contribute to the preparation of a comDrehensive livestock extension manual
for use by field workers. The manual would be published by DLS before the
end of 1991 and made available to all extension workers, agricultural
teachers and volunteers in the country.
21.       The objective of the trainina of livestock assistants would be
to increase awareness and sensitivity to socio-economic practices and
constraints affecting husbandry practices. Emphasis would be placed on the
effects of intra-household distribution of resources and responsibilities.
As part of the training process, each participant would be required to
carry out a simple survey and prepare a report on animal health care and
husbandry practices in the villages for which he or she is responsible.
The reports would be printed by DLS and distributed to all the training
participants.
22.       Study tours for outstanding extension workers -- to be carried
out in project year one - would enable the staff implementing the project
to exchange information with their colleagues in neighboring countries
working under similar conditions.
23.       Improved laboratory equipment and furniture, field sampling
kits, and a cold chain for storage of heat sensitive vaccines, media, and
reagents would be provided under the project. It is also proposed to train
three female staff in laboratory technology to improve efficiency in the
Divisional laboratories and to enhance their prospects for advancement to
intermediate and senior level positions. Suitable candidates for this
training are currently at work in the laboratories.  DLS would start the
application procedures in project year 0, preferably at institutions in
West or East Africa, and the trainees would start their training in project
year one. Candidates would be identified by July 1990.
24.       Apart from the initial evaluation of the existing pilot programs
(para 25), delivery system models to be developed would include provisions
for base-line data collection to be used as a monitoring and evaluation
tool. The simple diagnostic survey (para 29) implemented by each Livestock
Assistant as part of the Divisional training programs, would form the basis
for an annual follow-up evaluation. Evaluation protocols would evolve over
the life of the project. The Agricultural Component Coordinator would be
kept informed on the evaluation results on a regular basis.



- 66 -                           ANNEX 3-1
Page 6 of 13
25.       The extension services for women's livestock production would
eventually provide improved access to small ruminants medication and
vaccination throughout The Gambia, but would start with work in the 200
proiect villa2es. In particularly, the proposed surveys to be carried out
as part of the extension workers training should cover a large share of
these 200 villages to provide the necessary baseline information for
project evaluation.
(4) Private Sector Input Distribution
26.       The WID project would provide technical assistance for setting
up a sustainable private sector retailer network of about 18 women input
traders across the country. In addition, a line of credit and/or a
guarantee fund would be established at a commercial bank to facilitate
access to credit for these women retailers. Interest would be charged at
commercial rates in accordance with SAL agreements and to prevent unfair
competition with the established input distributor, the Gambia Cooperative
Union (GCU). The government is expected to provide equal access to inputs
procured through public channels (i.e., fertilizer) for both the
Cooperatives and the private sector, particularly in regards to conditions
of payment.
27.       There is agreement on the need to make use of the PAO Fertilizer
Project's expertise in identifying private retailers, providing them with
business advice and technical support for an initial period, and creating a
link between input provision and the extension services. Given the
complexity of this issue and the number of unresolved questions, it is
suggested that a study be undertaken as soon as possible. It should
include an updated detailed work plan for FAO under the WID project, look
into the need for credit or other financial means to enhance the prospects
for women retailers, and recommend financial arrangements in line with
Government policy that are sustainable and conform with SAL agreements.
The study should build upon the suggestions of the agricultural
consultants' report (Novogratz and Boughton) and the aide-memoire of the
appraisal mission. Specifically, the study should examine the following
issues.
(a)  What kind and amount of financial support are needed for the
proposed network of private women retailers? What should be the
nature of the fund suggested under the WID project; should it
rather be used for a credit line or as a guarantee fund (e.g.,
in case of widespread crop failures as a result of drought, or
as a partial or total guarantee against default for any reason),
or both?
(b) How can lending for input distribution be channeled through the
commercial banking system? Are commercial banks ready to get
involved in loans for input distribution? What are their
conditions? How should the fund be handled upon project
completion?
(c) Who will have final responsibility for selecting loan
recipients? Who will take the risk of loan defaults? If a



- 67 -                           ANNEX 3-1
Page 7 of 13
guarantee fund is to be set up, what will be the procedures and
criteria for its use?
(d)  What are the minimum eligibility criteria for credit recipients
(gender, qualifications, collateral requirements, etc.) and what
are the lending conditions (interest, repayment procedures,
conditions for onlending, etc.)? Is there a (financial) need
for a wholesalerldistributor; if so, what would be the
requirements for lending WID project funds to a wholesaler?
(e)  For what types of inputs should the fund be used: should it be
made available for medium-term loans (e.g. farm implements) or
be confined to seasonal loans? What mechanisms can ensure that
credit provided under the fund will actually be used by traders
for the designed purpose of agricultural input provision?
(f)  Is the amount presently budgeted appropriate for a credit
linelguarantee fund for 18 retailers plus a wholesaler if it is
determined there is need for one? The consultant should
critically review the projected budget and cash flow of the two
elements of this sub-component. i.e., the operating cost of
technical advice and the fund itself. What assumptions have
been made about complementary financing from FAO or the Gambian
Government?
(g)  What exactly will be the responsibility of the FAQ Fertilizer
Project in the process of retailer selection, business advisory
service, and extension linkage in the context of the WID
project? Is there need for FAO to maintain a role in
wholesaling?
(h)  How do different assumptions about the future development of the
input sector in The Gambia influence the effective functioning
of this sub-component (particularly with reference to input
pricing policy and government involvement in fertilizer
procurement)? A preliminary plan of action for this sub-
component includes the following points set out in paras 37-42
below.
29.       Ten additional women retailer would be identified by the FAQ
Fertilizer Project in order to bring the national network retailers up to a
total of 18. Candidates would be selected on the basis of previous
business experience and their track record, availability of suitable
storage facilities, and geographical location. To meet the ERP objective
of establishing a viable private sector input distribution network over the
life of the project, a private sector wholesale distributor or distributors
to service the retail outlets would be identified. In the event
wholesaler participation is not immediately forthcoming, alternative
mechanisms for distribution should be considered.
30.       To provide an effective business advisory service, eighteen
technical sales agents (TSAs) would be employed on a two-year contract
basis to assist the women retailers in marketing agricultural inputs. The



- 68 -                           ANNEX 3-1
Page 8 of 13
role of the TSAr would be to (a) provide assistance to the retailers in
bookkeeping. stock control and demand forecasting, to (b) liaise with DAB
extension staff in order to identify cohesive and creditworthy women farmer
groups for promotional activities, and (c) ensure tl,-.t clients receive the
appropriate inputs for the proposed activity. Salaries of the TSAs would
be paid by the retailers and reimbursed under the project for the first two
years only. Each TSA would be provided with a motorbike, and the
operational and maintenance costs would be funded under the project for the
first two years. All TSAs would undergo an induction training course on
agricultural input marketing with FAO Project staff. This would be
reinforced through pre-season in-service training sessions and on-the-job
training by project superviscry staff.
31.       The FAO project staff would provide business advisory services
the retailers and the wholesaler(s) during the life of project. As an
integral part of the business advisory service, annual sales promotion
meetings would be held for all members of the Private Dealers Association
and their assistants. The purpose of these meetings would be to determine
the aggregate demand for different types of agricultural inputs, update
dealers in regard to new technologies and recommendations, discuss
marketing strategies (including promotional activities and credit), and
review costs, margins and profit analyses. FAO project staff would assist
the retailers in carrying out semiannual post-season stock control and in
preparing annual sales, profit and loss accounts. These activities would
provide the basis for an annual reassessment of credit line limits for each
retailer, and also facilitate demand forecasting.
32.       The present 'block demonstration' concept has proved to be
highly effective for promoting input use by farmers. In order to
substantially increase female participation cohesive women's groups would
be the focal point for promotional demonstrations. DAS would have primary
responsibility for ensuring adequate professional extension input to this
activity. Demand for group demonstrations is expected to rise from five
per woman retailer in project year one to 40 in year five, with an average
of 25 women farmers taking part in each demonstration.
33.       After two full years of operation there would be a comprehensive
external evaluation to assess the potential for expansion of the women's
retailer network (for which budgetary provision would be made) and any
necessary steps that should be taken to ensure that an independent,
vertically integrated private sector distribution network will be viable by
the end of the project life 1995). This would include an evaluation of the
extent to which local wholesalers have been able to successfully supply the
retailer network. This external evaluation should be conducted in close
cooperation with the M&E Unit of the Women's Bureau. The M&E Unit may also
provide field data from its study on the effective outreach of extension
services and the female private sector retailer network scheduled for
project year two.
34.       The women retailers would not necessarily be located in WID
proiect villages, and they would certainly serve a large clientele. But it
should be stressed by project management and FAO that the retailers and the



- 69 -
ANNEX 3-1
Page 9 of 13
technical sales assistants should make special efforts to promote input use
in these project villages.
(5) Women's Cooperative Groups
35.       The WID project would encourage the provision of credit to
women's Cooperative groups under the GCU village branch lending system by
providing institutional support for women credit officers. These officers
would identify and liaise with women farmer groups that are suitable for
loans under the program. Initially, three credit officers, preferably
female, would be financed under the project. Induction training would be
provided by GCU.
36.       GCU would specify in its work plans targets for the number of
women's Cooperative groups to be assisted. Quarterly status reports would
provide information on the actual number of groups and number of individual
women reached; they would include information on the kind and amount of
credit provided to these groups, as well as loan recovery rates. Given the
novelty of the approach, this sub-component requires close monitoring by
the M&E Unit of the Women's Bureau. Upon successful initiation in
successive years, the number of credit officers would gradually be expanded
to seven.
37.       The GCU women credit officers should emphasize liaising with
vomen's groups in the 200 WID proiect villages, although they would not be
restricted to those villages. Obviously, a major precondition for GCU
lending is that Cooperatives are active in the particular area and the
villages fulfill the criteria for credit provision under the GCU village
branch lending system.
(6) Food Storage, Preservation and Nutrition Extension
38.       FNU would build up linkages with an established technology
organization with expertise in food processing and storage technologies.
This partner organization would provide short-term technical assistance to
FNU during the initial phase of the project and owuld cover the following
activities:
- Training of FNU in focl processing and storage techniques;
- Development of training materials for use by FNU staff to
train extension agents from DAS and other agencies;
- Collaboration with FNU staff to identify appropriate
household-level technologies, given the existing economic and
cultural constraints in The Gambia; and
- Collaboration with FNU staff to formulate a prioritized
strategy and work plan, to be implemented during the next four
years of the project.
To improve staff skill levels on an ongoing basis, two FNU staff members
each year would receive the equivalent of one month of training in food



- 70 -                     ANNEX 3-1
Page 10 of 13
processing and storage in collaboration with the partner organization
durirng project years two through five.
39.       During the second year of the project, one FNU staff member
would receive university trainina toward completion of a Bachelor's degree
in food science and nutrition. Two additional staff members would commence
university training -- one in the second and the other in the third year of
the project. Staggering staff training in this way would ensure that the
operational staff will not be reduced by more than two members at any one
time. In the pre-project phase priority would be given to completing of
the pilot studies and to setting up appropriate office facilities. For
this purpose, FNU would prepare a procurement order for purchasing the
needed furniture and supplies.
40.       To improve the availability of educational resources and
references available, the FNU would establish a food and nutrition library
and prepare a literature review summary. The library resources would be
updated annually with recent literature and publications. FNU would also
develop training materials to provide guidelines for the transfer of
information on post-harvest handling, processing and storage to field-
based extension workers; these training materials would be edited and
included in the DAS extension manuals. Once the materials have been
prepared, two one-week awareness seminars would be administered by FNU,
with 30-35 extension agents from the Department of Agriculture and from
experienced NGOs participating in each seminar.
41.       Nutritious, low cost recipes would be developed, tested and
published in a simple recipe book in local languages. Du..ing the first
project year, FNU would establish a food production laboratory to
facilitate future activities concerning food preparation and the
demonstration of processing techniques. Starting in project year two, FNU
would conduct a promotional campaign to encourage household preparation of
alternative recipes using traditional foodstuff. The campaign would
include cooking demonstrations and training workshops.
42.       PNU would collaborate with the Nutrition Unit of the Department
of Health to identify 15 villages, on the basis of their cohesiveness and
interest level in areas suffering high seasonal malnutrition rates, that
would be provided training tailored to the nutritional nteds and crop bases
of the targeted areas. Through linking up with the Agricultural
Engineering Unit (AEU), nev household technologies and food processing
strategies would be developed and tested during the project. FNU would
focus their extension efforts on the 200 WID proiect villages, but start
with its field work in project year two only after a prioritized work plan
for FNU has been finalized.
(7) Management of Small-scale Post-Harvest Eauipment
43.       An NGO of private sector organization would be employed to
implement a program of institutional strengthening of village cereal
processing. This program would comprise the following:



- 71 -
ANNEX 3-1
Page 11 of 13
(a) Identification of villages with existing coarse grain milling
facilities that have the potential to become economically viable in
the long run;
(b) Implementation of a village management committee and operator
training program in selected villages (40 villages per annum);
(c) Identification and training of rural mechanics in the vicinity of the
selected villages, in the repair of processing units; and
(d) Assistance to villages that achieve an adequate cash flow in
preparing loan applications for replacing mills andlor purchasing
dehullers.
The implementing agency would also undertake research and filed tests to
identify a smaller dehuller for use with existing engines.
44.          The villages to be covered in the campaign to improve the
management of post-harvest equipment would not necessarily be WID project
villages, since availability of a machine and prospects for sustainable,
economical performance would be the main criteria for the selection of
suitable villages. The implementing agency is expected to work with at least
40 villages each year.
(8) Savings Mobilization Campaign
45.          Upon presentation of a detailed work plan, the Gambia Women's
Finance Company (GWFC) would be employed on a two-year renewable technical
assistance contract to organize savings mobilization among women farmers.
Savings would be deposited at commercial banks or other financial institutions
with a proven track record. GWFC would cooperate closely with existing
Gambian institutions, particularly other NGOs, in this effort and make use of
the experience and resources of the IEC component of the WID project.
46.          In collaboration with existing financial institutions, GWFC
would try to develop systems for improvinw access of women to savings
facilities in these institutions. This would include a critical evaluation of
the feasibility of a greater number of branches with deposit opportunities
available in rural areas. On the village level, GWFC would undertake to
increase women's awareness about such opportunities and assist in developing
systems for facilitating their access to savings facilities.
47.          In collaboration with the Management Development Institute
(MDI), GWFC would develop and conduct training seminars for extension agents
of existing organizations to increase their awareness a.bout savings
opportunities and to strengthen their credit analysis skills. This training
would be modified on a yearly basis in response to the articulated needs of
extension workers.
48.          The savings mobilization campaign would be directed mainly at
women in the 200 WID Droject villages. After project year two, an external
consultant would evaluate the effectiveness of the savings mobilization
campaign and recommend ways to improve it.

Page 12 of 13
(9) Establishment of an Agricultural Coordinator's Office
49.       The primary responsibilities of the Agricultural Component
Coordinator would be to support planning activities, expedite procurement
procedures, ensure timely implementation of the agricultural component
activities, and monitor progress in the component's implementing agencies.
The specific tasks of the Coordinator would be to:
(a) Coordinate timely delivery of inputs and other resources to
the implementing agencies;
(b) Monitor proiect performance by attending (organizing)
Divisional meetings, reviewing status reports (para 61
below), and maintaining contact with the implementing
agencies;
(c) Provide mechanisms to promote dialogue among the
implementing agencies at the national level (e.g., through
the Agricultural Component Advisory Committee) and the
Divisional level (in coordination with the Commissioners).
(d) Prepare quarterly progress reports on the component's
overall progress in reaching the targeted quantifiable and
social objectives and on the short-term needs of the
implementing agencies; and
(e) On the basis of experience gained during the project,
propose modifications to the programs to accelerate
progress in implementing them.
50.       A quarterly progress report would be prepared by the
Agricultural Component Coordinator for submission to the PMU to summarize
the information contained in the individual status reports and to recommend
any necessary changes in the component activities.  A financial statement
will be submitted by the Coordinator on an annual basis to describe the
financial situation of each implementing agency. Actual expenditures would
be compared with the estimated budgets.
51.       The Coordinator would attend one short-term project management
course each year at the Management Development Institute (MDI) to develop
his or her management and project evaluation skills. It is recommended
that the Coordinator also be enrolled in MDI's Advaneed Project Management
Course for Participants from the Civil Service. Prior to project start-
up, the Coordinator would receive one-on-one training by an MDI staff
member two days per week for a six-week period. This training would be
specifically oriented to management of the agricultural component. During
the training sessions, management policies, operational systems, and
reporting procedures and forms would be designed to facilitate daily
management of the Agricultural Component Coordinator's Office.
52.       Under the sapervision of the Divisional Agricultural Coordinator
and chaired by each Divisional Commissioner, Divisional meetinas would be



- 73-                      ANNEX 3-1
Page 13 of 13
held on a quarterly basis for monitoring purposes. Participants of the
Divisional meetings should include representatives from the implementing
agencies, as well as VEWs, representatives of active NGOs, private sector
women retailers, and leaders of women's Cooperative groups. The meetings
would serve as forums for the implementing agencies to transmit information
to farmers (through the women retailers and village leaders), and would
enable the women retailers and farmers to articulate their needs and those
of other women farmers to higher authorities. Villagers are expected to
meet regularly to aiscuss the local proRress of the overall WID project.
By providing direct access to the implementing agencies, the Divisional
meetings would enhance the effectiveness of the village level committees.
53.       The implementing agencies would be responsible and accountable
for the preparation of annual work plans and budgets. The plans should
specify the quantified inputs and outputs of the respective agency, the
target villages to be reached in the following year, and plans for in-
house monitoring and reporting. Corresponding procurement orders would
also be prepared. Quarterly status reports would be prepared by the
implementing agencies to describe the current status of the agricultural
component, major activities undertaken during the reporting period, any
problems encountered, and plans for the upcoming quarter. The status
reports should include estimates of the number of beneficiaries assisted,
cost effectiveness, and any social changes observed (in terms of actions or
attitudes). All reports and procurement orders should be submitted to the
Agricultural Component Coordinator.
54.       The present task force for preparation of the agricultural
component would be replaced by an Agricultural Component Advisory Committee
gACAC), composed of the Agricultural Component Coordinator and of
representatives of the implementing agencies for this component including
the relevant operating units of the Ministry of Agriculture. ACAC would
meet at least twice a year to review past performance, recommend remedial
acticns for any major problems that may arise, and coordinate the work
plans and field work of the implementing agencies.
55.       It should be emphasized that the responsibilities anu many tasks
of the Agricultural Component Coordinator require that this position be
filled by a full-time senior official, who should be provided with
appropriate admiinistrative and secretarial support by the Ministry of
Agriculture.
D.   Project Preparation Facility (PPF)
56.       Funds have been provided under tne PPF for the following:
establishment of the Agricultural Component Coordinator's Office (vehicle,
furniture, equipment end operating costs); study on the private sector
retailer network; component preparation by the Gambian Women's Finance
Company (GWFC); and completion of the pilot studies.

REPUBLIC OF THE GAMBIA
WOMEN IN DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SKILLS DEVELOPMENT COMPONENT
A.   Goals and Objectives
1.        In view of the low level of eco'nomic productivity and high
illiteracy rate among rural women in The f*Zmbia, this component aims to
develop their productive skills in order to promote women's fuller
participation in the economy and enhance their social position and improve
living standards within their communities. The specific objectives of the
skills development component are tot
(a)  Improve rural-based skills for women and promote the development
of other viable and marketable skills;
(b)  Organize and strengthen women's groups in rural areas for small
business and income-generating activities; and
(c)  Promote basic literacy and numeracy skills through  training
activities relevant to women's social and economic needs.
2.        To achieve these objectives, the skills development component
would provide a comprehensive program to support and promote women's
economic productivity. The key elements of the component are:
-    Direct involvement of women beneficiaries at the village
level, using traditional women's kafo groups as entry
points;
-    Specification by locality of viable income-generating
opportunities through market surveys and community needs
assessment;
-    Strengthening of the organization of women's groups to
facilitate skills training for improved production and
business management combined with functional literacy and
numeracy;
-    Training in the use of and improved access to credit for
women's small business and income-generating activities;
and
-    Technical assistance with marketing aspects, as well as
close follow-up assistance and advice on starting and
maintaining sizsll businesses or income-generating
activities.
.                                                            *~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~4



- 75 -
ANNEX 3-2
Page 2 of 7
3.        The skills development component would be implemented over a
period of six years. The Department of Community Development (DCD) in the
Ministry of Local Government and Lands would be the main implementing
agency. DCD, with its network of extension workers at the village level,
reaches the rural population effectively and promotes the acquisition of
numerous skills, including literacy and numeracy. The main participating
agency would be the Department of Nonformal Education (NFE) in the Ministry
of Education, Youth, Sports and Culture. NFE, with its experience and
expertise in skill training and the development of nonformal educational
materials (particularly literacy/numeracy), would be a key supporting
agency. To ensure smooth cooperation between DCD and NFE, a protocol of
understanding between the respective Ministries will specify the human and
physical resources to be allocated by each, as well as the role of the
various staff to be assigned. This protocol is to be drawn up by the
Ministry of Finance.
B.   Proposed Sub-components
4.        Three sub-components are proposed for this intervention- (a)
strengthening of Government Departments and participating agencies; (b) a
skills development program; and (c) improved access to credit.
C.   Sub-Component I: Strengthening of Government Departments and
Participatina Agencies
5.        This sub-component would strengthen the capacity of DCD and NFE
professional staff to effectively plan, implement and manage the skills
development program and associated credit scheme. In addition to
strengthening the management skills of government departments and
participating agencies and providing the appropriate vehicles and
equipment, the main activity of this sub-component would be to train the
trainers. This would comprise:
(a) Training of the national staff of DCD and NFE (Skill Component
Coordinator, CDOs and NFE Divisional staff) in implementation
and management techniques;
(b) Study tours for national staff (selected personnel of DCD, NFE
and Divisional CDOs) in neighboring countries, followed by
debriefing workshops in Banjul, to include the field-level CDAs;
(c) Division-level orientation workshops for District Commissioners
and Village Development Committees (VDCs); and
(d) In-service and refresher training for 50 Community Development
Agents (CDAs) and the retraining of Home Craft Assistants
(HCAs).



- 76 -
ANNEX 3-2
Page 3,of 7
6.        A technical assistance (TA) team composed of three specialists
(total of 57 person-months) would assist DCD and N2E in implementing the
skills development component, as described in paras ', 8 and 9 below. Each
specialist would have a Gambian counterpart, whom the specialist would
train to take over when the TA assignment is completed.
7.        TA for income-generating activities - Soecialist in small
business develooment (36 person-months). This specialist would be the TA
team leader and also serve as the Component Manager, heading the Project
Unit for the skills development component. During project start-up, the
specialist and Gambian counterpart would survey the existing programs and
the field experience of organizations providing small business advisory
services in The Gambia, such as IBAS, GCU and UNDP. With the help of
trained CDAs, they would organize and carry out village-level needs
assessments and local marketing surveys in order to specify which market
opportunities and productive activities would be appropriate for a given
village. On the basis of this field work, they would participate in the
selection of 125 villages for skills development from amons the 200 pre-
selected during project preparation. On the basis of these activities and
in collaboration with the Skills Development Component Coordinator and with
the TA team as a whole ,And senior-level staff from DCD and NFE, the small
business specialist would finalize a detailed implementation plan,
including staff training at the national and regional levels as well as a
precise timetable for the skills development component. The work plan
should give clear performance indicators for monitoring progress in
implementation. The small business specialist would play an important role
in the planning and execution of the various training activities
(workshops, training courses, orientation sessions, study tours, etc.)
which are described below.
8.        TA for skill trainins development - Specialist in nonformal
curriculum and design (12 oerson-months).    In close collaboration with
NFE and DCD, this specialist and the Gambian counterpart nominated by NFE
would survey, catalog and assess the appropriateness of the considerable
body of potentially useful teaching materials already in existence in the
country. Working closely with the small business and credit specialists,
they would undertake a training needs analysis and prepare a plan for the
development of appropriate materials and methods for implementing the
skills development component. The specialist and the Gambian counterpart
would need to advise on and plan for two quite distinct types of training;
the first type is related to the skills component staff (national staff of
DCD and NFE including the Skills Component Coordinator, and the CDAs in the
field), whereas the second is related to the direct beneficiaries of the
skill training for income generation and basic literacy/numeracy. The
training would include women's groups as well as selected women tutors.
The specialist and the Gambian counterpart would develop curricula and
methodologies, in cooperation with NFE, to cover the skili areas identified
in the market studies, as well as literacy/numeracy materials. The various
materials related to both types of training would be prepared in the
context of writers' workshops and reproduced at the BPMRU in the Ministry
of Education, Youth, Sports and Culture.



- 77 -
ANNEX 3-2
Page 4 of 7
9.        TA for establishment of a credit scheme - Specialist in small-
scale credit mechanisms (9 person-months). This specialist and the Gambian
counterpart, working closely with the Grassroots Development Fund (GRD) in
DCD, would finalize arrangements for establishing a revolving credit fund
to support small business and income-generating activities for women
trainees of the skills development component. In coordination with the
other two specialists, training curricula and orientation workshops would
be designed to strengthen the capacity of field staff of CDAs in credit
management and administration, as well as the ability of women to obtain
and manage credit. The operational policies and practices of the credit
scheme would be adapted from the current operations of the GRD, but would
alse take into account mechanisms in use by other organizations offering
credit in rural areas, such as Action Aid, Save the Children Fund (USA),
CUSO, WISDOM and IBAS. The credit specialist would be responsible for
elaborating detailed procedures for operating the credit scheme to be
funded under the skills development component; in this connection, a
monitoring system would be devised to keep track of credit applications,
disbursements, recovery records, and the financial status of the credit
scheme.
D.        Sub-Component II:  The Skills Development Program
10.       As the main operational activity of the entire component, the
Skills Development Program would seek to increase the economic productivity
of rural women and their capacity to raise their incomes and social status.
This program would target approximately 10,000 women in 125 villages
nationwide that would be selected from a larger group of 200 villages to be
agreed upon before the implementation phase begins. The Skills Development
Program would have nationwide impact and be implemented in phases, to
include The Gambia's five administrative Divisions, starting with two
Divisions in the second year, two in the third and one in the fourth year.
11.       The mobilization and strengthening of women's kafo groups and
the orientation of VDCs to support the skills development component would
take place during the initial period of implementation. Existing women's
kafo groups, would serve as entry points for the skills development
component. Trained CDAs, together with the kafo groups, would facilitate
the selection of women's groups already engaged in income activities, as
well as facilitate the formation of other groups interested in initiating
new business activities based on identified market opportunities. Women's
groups are involved in a number of productive activities -- e.g., food
processing and drying, oil pressing, poultry production, small
manufacturing of consumable .tems, trading, and tailoring.
12.       The skills development component would improve and upgrade
existing skills and introduce new ones. It would provide training in
technical skills, business management, improved production methods and
technologies, and the ability to use credit. The expertise of various
governmental and non-governmental agencies and local artisans would be



- 78 -
ANNEX 3-2
Page 5 of 7
utilized to impart the skill training. Relevant materials would be
prepared by NFE specifically for these activities (para 3 above).
13.       The CDAs would also use kafo groups as entry points to identify
and select women tutors or other appropriate persons from the villages to
promote basic numeracy and literacy; 125 tutors would be trained for three
months, spread over a period of six to seven months. Training methods that
emphasize the concept of learning by doing would be used, thereby building
confidence among the women tutors and providing them with follow-up
support.
14.       Skill training for income generation and for functional literacy
would commence simultaneously, so that both activities can mutually
reinforce each other and have a greater impact. The training activities
described above would be carried out on site (at the village level) and in
selected key villages during the seven to eight months of the dry season.
DCD and NFE staff and field workers, supported by technical assistance
provided under the skills development component, would closely follow
activities that are breaking new ground in The Gambian context.
Sustainability would be assured through the use of existing facilities and
local personnel in implementing all aspects vis-a-vis the identification of
training needs, training, management, etc., so as to continue the program
uninterrupted in subsequent years.
E.   Sub-Component III:  Improved Access to Credit
15.       An essential addition to the skills development component is
improved access to credit for rural women. In light of evidence that skill
training by itself cannot lead to the realization of income-generating
activities, a flexible revolving fund for rural women would be established
as an integral part of the component. Credit would be extended to women
participants in the skills development program who have acquired specific
skills; women's groups as well as individuals would be considered as
eligible.
16.       This small-scale credit scheme to be funded under the skills
development component would not exceed a total of US$600,000. Management
costs would be minimized by using the expertise of ongoing mechanisms for
extending credit in rural areas (para 9). Important design features of the
credit scheme would includes (a) bringing credit to women in villages
distant from banking institutions; (b) linking credit closely with income-
generating skills and small business development in a mutually reinforcing
package; (c) using a system of group guarantees to encourage high levels of
community involvement in the repayment of loans; and (d) charging an
interest rate that covers the cost of administering the credit scheme and
protects the revolving fund's real value against inflation over time (with
inflation now at 8? and an estimated administrative cost of 8t of annual
lending, the interest rate would be 162).



- 79 -
ANNEX 3-2
Page 6 of 7
17.       With agreed policy and procedural guidelines for the operation
of the credit scheme, it would become an institutionalized and sustainable
activity enhancing the income-generating potential of rural women who are
not served by other sources of credit.
F.   Monitoring and Evaluation
18.       Monitoring and evaluation would be a continuous activity carried
out during the entire life of the skills development component and its
interrelated credit scheme. Periodic monitoring to track progress towards
project objectives and to identify problems and ways to solve them is to be
accomplished by designing a simple, manageable system.
19.       Three technical assistants, together with their three Gambian
counterparts, would develop performance indicators to provide both a
quantitative and qualitative evaluation of the results/impact and
appropriateness of all activities of the skills development component.
Data generated from monitoring activities at the village level would
provide DCD and NFE with information to evaluate and improve overall
performance.
20.       To ensure that the monitoring and evaluation system will be
understood and properly implemented by all concerned, it would form part of
the curriculum of the training provided in workshops for national staff,
Division-level personnel, and field workers during the start-up phase. It
is envisaged that at the close of the short-term technical assistance the
monitoring and evaluation activities can be sustained throughout the
project cycle and beyond.
21.       Emphasis in the monitoring system would be on a participatory
approach. Particularly at the village level, the monitoring process would
include representatives of all the women's groups involved. This would
provide an opportunity for the women to articulate their concerns and,
together with the implementing agencies, determine ways to resolve any
problems that arise. The monitoring and evaluation activities would be
conducted at both the central and field levels by the Skills Component
Coordinator and the field staff, as follows:
(a) At the central level:
- Close follow-up of the skills development component work plan
and its implementation, as well as resolution of problems as
they arise.
- A detailed work plan with objectively verifiable performance
indicators would be developed for the training, credit, and
small business schemes.
- Regular field v'sits and back-up support, as required.

participating ministries and agencies.
(b) At the field levels
- Regular visits to women's groups and their business and other
