REPUBLIC OF THE GAMBIA
WOMEN IN DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SAFE MOTHERHOOD AND FAMILY PLANNING COMPONENT
A. Background
1.       Data on The Gambia's health status indicate that maternal, infant
and child mortality rates are among the highest in Africa. Maternal
mortality is reported at 10 per 1,000 live births, but recent studies by
the British Medical Research Council (MRC) show that in some rural areas it
is as high as 20-22 per 1000 live births. The infant and under five
mortality rates are 143 and 242, respectively. per 1,000. Post-partum
hemorrhage and infections are the leading causes of maternal mortality,
while infant and child mortality are caused mainly by malaria, diarrhea,
and respiratory tract infections. Malnutrition has a compounding effect on
mortality and morbidity, particularly among children and pregnant women.
Maternal death is one of the main causes of neonatal and infant mortality.
2.       To address these health problems, the Government embarked on an
ambitious primary health care (PHC) program in 1980, the cornerstone of
which is the establishment of a network of village-level health posts
(VHPs' operated and supported by village communities. So far, 316 VHPs
have been established in villages with a population of 400 and more; this
provides a PHC coverage of 60? for the country. Each VHP is staffed with a
village health worker (VHW) and a traditional birth attendant (TBA), both
of which are chosen by the villagers and trained by the Ministry of Health
in a range of curative and preventive health services. PHC villages are
grouped into circuits, each with an average of 4-5 villages, one of which
is designed as the "key' village; this village is a base for the community
hr.alth nurse (CHN), who participates in the initial training of the VHWs
and TBAs of his (or her) circuit and is also the first-line technical
supervisor of the two health workers.
3.        Another important element of the Government's PHC strategy is the
development of mobile outreach services (trekking teams) that are provided
by health centers and dispensaries, and that visit wayside stations
(trekking stations) at monthly intervals. These trekking teams serve as
the link between the villages health services and the Government's referral
system (health centers, dispensaries and hospitals). Because of the high
toll of sickness and premature deaths among mothers and young children,
priority has been given to MCH services in the outreach clinics. To date,
mobile clinic services are offered at more than 100 locations throughout
the country, and they have played a major role in The Gambia being able to
attain a high level of infant vaccination coverage.
4.       The Government's impressive PHC effort; however, has not produced
the expected improvement in the level of maternal mortality.  An evaluarion
of the performance of the TBAs will be undertaken before the start of the
project (paras 13 and 14), to determine what has caused this lack of
improvement. But in all likelihood it is due to inadequate training and



- 82 -                             ANNEX 3-3
Page 2 of 11
supervision, the absence of a good referral system to back up the village
health structure, and the severe budgetary constraints the Government has
faced during the 1980s. which have led to a steady decline in operating
expenditures for the health sector, particularly for essential drugs,
thereby progressively undermining the effectiveness of the health care
system. The National Health Development Program (NHDP). launched in early
1986 with the support of the World Bank and of other donors, is addressing
these problems. It focuses on (a) expanding and strengthening the various
referral levels, from health centers to hospitals; (b) training community
health nurses (CHNs) and state enrolled nurses (SENs): and (c) increasing
the availability of essential drugs. An important feature of the program
is the introduction of cost recovery for drugs and health services to
promote more efficient use of health facilities and shift some of the cost
of improved health services to the beneficiaries.
5.       Most of the information on maternal mortality is related to
hospital-based surveys, although in rural areas only a minority of women
deliver in a hospital or medical center, even when there have been
complications during the pregnancy. In these circumstances, many maternal
and neonatal deaths occur at home and therefore information on them often
is not available.  Two studies undertaken in The Gambia -- a year-long
prospective study conducted by MRC in 1982-83, covering 41 villages in the
North Bank Division, and a more recent study undertaken by the Ministry of
Health in the Western Region --point to obstetrical factors as a major
cause of maternal mortality. These could largely be prevented through early
detection of high-risk pregnancies, appropriate prenatal care, and prompt
handling of complications. However, health workers usually see a pregnant
women for the first time when she has reached an advanced stage of
pregnancy and often only when complications arise. Transport in many
villages is difficult to obtain in an emergency: road conditions in many
areas are poor, with horse-and-donkey carts the only means, other than by
walking, by which residents of the more isolated villages can reach town.
In some areas that have no health facilities, women travel up to 20 km to
reach a maternal and child health (MCH) outreach clinic, which are
periodically organized by the nearest health center.
6.        Few health centers have the facilities to handle emergency cases,
which have to be evacuated to the two hospitals in Banjul or Bansang. For
residents on the north bank of the Gambia River, until recently this meant
a journey of several hours. The situation has begun to improve with the
recent upgrading of two major health centers -- one in Banjul and the other
in Kuntaur, on the. river's north bank -- which are equipped to provide
advanced obstetrical treatment and to handle emergencies, including blood
transfusions. These are the first two health centers (out of 7 throughout
the country) to be upgraded to provide advanced maternal care under the
strategy being implemented in the context of the NHDP (para 4). In
addition, at all the country's 16 health centers (including the 7 to be
upgraded), maternity waiting homes would be constructed (6 under the NHDP
and 10 under a United Nations Fund for Population Activities (UNFPA)
project) to allow mothers at risk to stay near a health facility prior to
delivery.        0
7.       The high fertility rate (6.5 live births per woman) in The Gambia
also weakens maternal health and a woman's ability to provide for her
children. The Government has supported family planning (FP) for many
years, and FP activities have been carried out by the Ministry of Health
.                     X
l



- 83 -                           ANNEX 3-3
Page 3 of 11
(through hospitals, health centers and the village-based system) and by an
indigenous NGO, the Gambia Family Planning Association (GFPA), mainly
through community-based distributors (CBDs). In spite of these activities,
the contraceptive prevalence rate tCPR) has remained disappointingly low
(about 6X, according to the most recent estimates). To shed light on the
current situation and determine its causes, a contraceptive prevalence
survey will be undertaken in 1990 with UNFPA and IDA financing.
8.        Situated within the Sahelian zone, The Gambia is subject to the
harsh climatic conditions typical of this region, with a long dry season
and a short rainy season, during which food crop production is undertaken.
Rainfall is extremely variable and unevenly distributed, and the soils are
poor and fragile. These factors make the country's food and nutrition
situation rather precarious--a situation compounded by widespread
infectious, parasitic and gastrointestinal diseases, poor environmental and
personal sanitation, poor intra-household food distribution, and harmful
food and health practices and beliefs.
9.        Research conducted thus far on malnutrition in The Gambia by
such organizations as the Dunn Nutrition Unit of MRC, the Ministry of
Health, and several NGOs, indicates that malnutrition is widespread and
varies by season and region, with rural areas more severely affected than
urban. Urban Gambians consume an average of 2,200 calories per person per
day throughout the year (the FAO-established norm is 2,230 calories),
compared with the rural population's consumption of an average of 2,140
calories per day during the post-harvest season but only 1,660 during the
rainy season, representing a daily shortfall of about 500 calories per
person per day in rural areas. The effects of the "hungry' season are
exacerbated by a heavy agricultural workload borne by all active members of
the community, including pregnant and lactating women.
10.       Pregnant and lactating mothers and children, particularly those
between six months and two or three years of age, are the most vulnerable
groups. Research undertaken thus far indicates that about 30-402 of
Gambian children are affected by chronic malnutrition (mild in appearance,
this malnutrition takes the form of low stature and weight for the
particular age), while severe malnutrition (less than 602 of the Harvard
standard for weight per age) affects only between 22 and 4X of Gambian
children. The nutritional situation of women is especially critical. Most
Gambian women have their first child soon after menarche and continue with
regular reproductive cycles until menopause. Pregnancy often follows
immediately after lactation (which lasts from 18 to 22 months), without an
intervening period during which nutritional stores could be replenished.
Women therefore frequently start pregnancy in a nutritionally debilitated
state. Maternal malnutrition severely limits fetal growth and causes low
birth weight (below the WHO cut-off of 2,500 g. '-slected as the point below
which neo-natal mortality rates increase sharply). Studies undertaken by
MRC and Save the Children Fund (SCF) in some rural areas of the country
show an incidence of low birth weight as high as 25X.  Still births and
early termination of pregnancies are also high in those areas.



- gIL -
ANNEX 3-3
Page 4 of 11
B. ObJectives of the Safe Motherhood and Family Planning Component
11.      The objectives of the safe motherhood and FP component of the WID
project are to: (a) reduce the current high maternal mortality rate; (b)
reduce the incidence of maternal malnutrition; and (c) raise the
contraceptive prevalence rate from the current low level of 6Z. The
strategy proposed under the WID project to achieve these objectives
includes: training of TBAs to improve identification and management of at-
risk mothers well before delivery; establishment of an "alarm- evacuation
system to transfer high-risk pregnancy and emergency cases from isolated
villages to facilities where more sophisticated care can be provided; a
village-based nutrition education program; and expansion of FP services.
By concentrating on strengthening and expanding the village-based health
system, the component would complement the activities undertaken under the
NHDP (para 4) and improve the linkage between village communities and the
public health services, with respect to both the patient referral system
and the supervision and support of personnel. As some of the proposed
measures are innovative, they would be introduced on a pilot basis. This
component comprises four sub-components: (a) risk identification and
management; (b) local evacuation and telecommunications; (c) women's
nutrition; and (d) family planning. These are described below. While sub-
components (a) to (c) would be implemented in the 200 villages targeted for
project intervention, sub-component (d) would cover both rural and peri-
urban areas.
(1) Risk Identification and Management
12.      The objectives of this sub-component are to: (a) routinely provide
a full range of ante-natal and post-natal care for pregnant women by the
end of the five-year project period, through the combined services of
trained TBAs at the village level and of trekking teams at trekking
stations; (b) ensure that 50 of pregnant women in the intervention
villages who give birth will have had at least two ante-natal consultations a
during their pregnancies and at least one post-partum consultation,
including family planning education; and (c) ensure that 50? of the
estimated high-risk pregnancies will be identified and referred by the TBA
to the community health nurse (CHN) for follow-up and monitoring.
13.      To achieve these objectives, the strategy would be to improve
ante-natal, delivery and post-natal care and to strengthen the system for
identifying, managing and referring high-risk pregnancies. Under the sub-
component, in-service training would be provided for 200 TBAs (the trained
TBA in each of the 200 project villages), 40 to 50 CHNs (depending on the
number of key villages in the project area), and 15 trekking teams covering
35 trekking stations. Special attention would be given to the interaction
among these three MCH services at the PHC level. The in-service training
would address gaps in the health worker performance identified by the
evaluation of th TEA program and the MCH services provided by trekking
teams (para 3). The objectives of the evaluation are to determine whether
the majority of rpral women use the trained TBAs, how effective these TBAs
are, whether they have integrated the new procedures into their routine
practice, and whether risk factors and potential complications are being
noted and referred to higher level.



- 85 -                              ANNEX 3-3
Page 5 of 11
14.      The evaluation not only would provide answers on how TBA training
may be improved, but also help review the Government's policy and establish
whether the role envisioned for the TEAs as key actors in the formal health
care system is realistic. The results of the TRA evaluation, along with
the results of the contraceptive prevalence survey, which will also collect
data on maternal mortality, are expected to stimulate consideration of new
community-level interventions.
15.      Many untrained TBAs still operate at the village level, and it is
suspected that many women continue to use the TBAs that have traditionally
served their groups even when trained TBAs are available in the area. The
TMA survey should reveal to what extent this is happening. The project
proposes to integrate into the PHC network 400 untrained TBAs (two in each
of the 200 project villages) by providing basic training in a limited range
of skills such as sterile delivery techniques, identification and referral
of high-risk pregnancies, and family planning.
16.      The identification of mothers with high-risk pregnancies is a
crucial element of the high-risk referral strategy. Approaches currently
used by the NGO, Save the Children Fund (SCF), to identify and refer high-
risk pregnancies -- i.e., use of traditional women's groups (kabilos) based
on family ties, and introduction of simple pictoral cards for screening
risk cases -- would be tried out in the context of the government health
services and, if proven successful, integrated by the Government into the
PHC program. The Government would contract SCF to help it adapt both
approaches to the PHC system and also test them.
17.      Dilapidated facilities, as well as a lack of drugs and basic
equipment, have made the outreach service practically unable to perform its
functions. Hence, under this sub-component, the project would refurbish
and/or reconstruct 35 sub-dispensaries used as trekking stations by the MCH
outreach clinics periodically organized by the health centers in their
areas. Fifteen trekking teams would be provided basic equipment, and
health posts would be constructed in 10 isolated villages of the Upper
River Division that have VHWs and trained TBAs but lack a permanent lealth
facility where patients and particularly pregnant women can be treated
privately. This is seen as a strong deterrent in keeping pregnant women
from using preventive health services.
(2) Local Evacuation and Telecommunications
18.      The objectives of this sub-component are tot (a) establish an
appropriate and easily maintained means for evacuating emergency cases in
105 isolated villages; and (b) provide solar-powered radio communication
links between key villages and health centers in one of the most isolated
and unserviced part of the country.
19.      The health centers and hospitals are currently equipped with
ambulances in various states of operation. Furthermore, a
telecommunicationg system is being introduced under the NHDP to link the
health centers to regional headquarters of the Ministry of Health, the two
hospitals, and the national telecommunications network. Below the level of
the health centers, however. communication and access to timely transport
are very poor. Hence, the project would improve the situation by (a)
providing animal drawn (horse/donkey) ambulances for 105 isolated villages



- 86 -                              ANNEX 3-3
Page 6 of 11
for emergency evacuation of pregnant women to a place where motorized
transport is available: and (b) providing solar-powered radio equipment for
five base and 10 out-stations in the isolated Upper River Division,
enabling the health centers to maintain emergency and routine
communications with key villages in that area. This would be part of a
phased approach to gradually providing communication links between all
health centers and key villages in the country. The village ambulances
would be improved carts designed to provide comfort and safety (i.e., built
with padding and side barriers). The carts would belong to the Village
Development Committees (VDCs), which would appoint a caretaker for each
animal and cart and closely monitor their use and care. Some use of the
animal (i.e., for farming activities) would be granted to the caretaker as
an incentive to look after it. This system would be first tried out in 21
villages, and only if proven successful would be extended to the remaining
villages.
20.      The project would finance: carts and animals, radio equipment and
materials, equipment maintenance, and an evaluation of both the ambulance
and the telecommunications program.
(3) Women's Nutrition
21.      The Government's priority in the area of nutrition is to improve
the nutritional status of the most vulnerable groups. Its recently
developed strategy to do this emphasizes (a) integration of nutrition into
the PHCIMCH system of the Ministry of Health; (b) a food supplementation
program targeted to pregnant and lactating women but including their
children up to age 2; and (c) an intensive nutrition educational effort to
increase awareness and understanding of the nutritional needs of pregnant
and lactating women anC small children, so as to promote behavioral change.
The NGO, Catholic Relief Services (CRS), would provide the food
supplementation, which would consist of take-home food packages. CRS has
been administering a food supplementation program in The Gambia since 1974
and recently agreed to link this program more closely to the Government's
nutrition objectives and strategy. As a result, in addition to merging the
program into the PHC system managed by the Ministry of Health, the target
group has changed from children under age 5 to the Government target group,
pregnant and lactating women and their children up to age 2. Title II
imported commodities, thus far used for the feeding program, would be
gradually phased out and replaced by locally grown food (sesame,
groundnuts, sugar, cowpeas, fruits and vegetables), which ths communities
themselves would provide. Recipes for weaning food and maternal
supplements would be developed with the assistance of the Food and
Nutrition Unit (FNU) of the Ministry of Agriculture, and would be prepared
by women's groups and tested in selected villages before being introduced
on a larger scale (funds for these activities are included under the
agricultural component).
22.      The food supplementation program would be implemented by community
management commitjees whose members would be chosen by the local kafos
(women's groups). Growth monitoring of children would be managed at PHC
posts, and analysis and data reporting of this nutrition surveillance data
would be done by the Nutrition Unit of the Ministry of Health. Women would
be encouraged to bring their children regularly to the health services for
growth monitoring. The results of these activities would be compiled and



- 87 -                              ANNEX 3-3
Page 7 of 11
discussed by the Village Councils to increase the villagers' awareness and
understanding of malnutrition and encourage them to take remedial action.
While it is acknowledged that in a take-home food distribution program
there is considerable sharing with other household members, thus reducing
the effectiveness of specific targeting for vulnerable individuals, an on-
site food distribution program is considered too cumbersome and expensive
to administer. The introduction of foods suitable mainly for the target
groups and the nutrition educational program described below should
minimize food 'leakages'.
23.      The project would complement CRS activities by financing a
nutrition educational program and micro-nutrient supplementation, both to
be implemented in the 200 project villages, while the CRS supplementation
program (para 21) would be implemented in all 316 PHC villages. In
addition, funds would be provided to undertake (a) market food and
household shelf surveys by season to find out what kinds of food and what
quantities are available in the markets and village compounds in the wet
and dry seasons; (b) a series of qualitative studies that should help to
shed light on such issues as the dynamics of intra-household food
distribution, food practices and beliefs, and the influence of religion on
nutrition; and (c) a study on Vitamin A deficiency. Research results will
guide the design and development of the nutrition educational program.
24.      The nutrition educational program supported by the project would
address attitudinal constraints that militate against a women's adequate
nutrition during pregnancy and breastfeeding. As highlighte; by an
informal survey undertaken by FNU in 1987, these attitudinal constraints
range from taboos that preclude women and children from eating highly
nutritional food (e.g., eggs, fish and baobab fruits), to women "eating
downw during pregnancy in order to have small babies and thus reduce risk
during delivery, and to the tradition of women and children eating apart
from men and only after the latter have been given the most nutritious
portions and largest share of the meal.  T.e FNU survey also revealed that
variety in meals is very limited, with many families eating only two types
of food per week, and that there is limited consumption of cereals other
than rice, or of legumes, root crops and vegetables. More than 802 of the
families interviewed prepared low-nutrient dense and very watery weaning
food, with a high chance of contamination because of the practice of
cooking food only once a day. A similar practice is reported by the Dunn
Nutrition Unit of MRC and is associated with children's frequent bouts of
diarrhea, which often result in malnutrition and death. The nutrition
educational program supported under the project would address the above-
described constraints, as well as those to be identified by the additional
qualitative studies financed under the project.
25.      The project's nutrition educational program was prepared by the
Nutrition Unit of the Ministry of Health in collaboration with the
nutritionists of CRS, MRC and SCF. The program would use both formal and
informal methods of communication, with special emphasis on participatory
communication techniques (e.g., local musicians, problem dramas, and role
playing). Such techniques encourage communities to discuss their problems
and to seek solutions acceptable to them. Some of these techniques have
proved to be quite successful in helping to expand the coverage of
immunization in The Gambia. The project would finance the services of an
international consultant (4 weeks) to train the educational teams of the
Health Education Unit in problem-solving methods of health education.



- 88                                ANNEX 3-3
Page 8 of 11
Financing would also be provided for training 160 extension workers at the
PCH level (CHNs, agricultural extension workers, and CDAs) in the delivery
of nutritional messages.
26.      Training would be provided by a central training team, managed by
the Nutrition Unit of tne Ministry of Health and composed of staff from the
Ministry's Health Education and PHC Training Units, FNU, NGOs involved in
nutrition activities (GAFNA, CRS and SCF), and the Dunn Nutrition Unit of
MRC. In addition to organizing the training workshops and seminars, this
team would assess intervention areas for nutrition messages, formulate and
test the messages, and design an interactive educational program for the
villages. The team would coordinate the activities of three health
education teams which would be formed at the regional level to monitor the
work of the extension agents and evaluate the impact of the nutrition
educational program. These regional teams would be composed of the most
senior staff of the Ministry of Health and the NGOs at that level (public
health nurses, public health superintendents, regional nutritional
assistants, SCF health coordinators, and GAFNA's and GFPA's staff). Their
tasks would be to sensitize the region, identify extension agents for
training, identify and train regional musician troupes, and supervise the
village-to-village campaigns. At the village or PHC circuit level (a key
village and its 4-5 village satellites), teams would be formed composed of
VDC members, women kafo leaders, religious leaders, community health
nurses, the VHW, the TBA and other extension agents. Their role would be
to identify suitable messages, identify local musicians/griots to be
trained by the regional groups, and organize the village interactive
performances. While in interpersonal and participatory communication the
educational messages would be very specific (e.g., growth monitoring,
promotion of vitamin/mineral supplementation, promotion of weaning food for
children and special supplements for mothers), the messages developed
through mass media would be multi-purpose and emphasize the
interrelationship of health, hygiene, and family planning with nutrition.
27.      Pregnant and lactating women would receive, in addition to food
supplementation, iron and foliate supplementation and iodine to overcome
severe iron and iodine deficiencies, which are quite c.,mmon in The Gambia
among women and children. Iron deficiency (anemia) is caused by low
dietary iron ir.take and frequent attacks of malaria and worm infestations,
with the malaria season coinciding utth tha *hungry" season (para 9); in
pregnant women it decreases their ability to survive hemorrhage and other
complications of labor and delivery. Iodine-deficiency disorders (goiter)
are particularly widespread in the McCarthy Island Division (MID) ard the
Upper River Division (URD). Another important micro-nutrient deficiency in
The Gambia is riboflavin deficiency (stomatitis), also common among women
and children and caused mainly by inadequate intake of milk and milk
products, particularly during the rainy season when these products become
less available. The project would finance micro-nutrient supplements (iron
and odine) for about 10,0(,0 women per year; This represents about 201 of
the estimated number of women of reproductive age (WRA) in the project
villages. The importance of B-vitamins and their availability from local
foods would be emphasized in the nutrition educational program. The
Gambian health services are currently not giving priority to Vitamin A
deficiency (night blindness), but more research needs tc be done to define
its magnitude nationwide. A study on Vitamin A deficiency would be
financed under the project.



- 89 -                               ANNEX 3-3
Page 9 of 11
28.      Items to be financed under the sub-component include: the micro-
nutrient supplementation program; vc,%icles and their operation and
maintenance costs; preparation of a nutrition educational program and
supporting materials; workshops, meetings and seminars at various
administrative levels to strengthen the educational process; technical
assistance (4 weeks) to train the health educational teams in problem-
solving educational methods; local entertainers' interventions, and
appropriate studies. Adequate operating funds would be provided to ensure
that the officials involved in the nutrition educational program have the
mobility needed to carry out regular supervision.
(4) Family Planning (FP)
29.      The objective of this sub-component is to increase access to FP
services and public awareness about modern FP methods through: (a)
establishment of community-based distribution sites in areas not covered by
the PHC program and in the Greater Banjul area; (b) community health
education activities; and (c) enlistment of the private sector in the
provision of safe and effective distribution of contraceptives.
30.      The FP sub-component is designed to complement a UNFPA family
planning project the implementation of which recently started.
Specifically, the UNFPA project, whose total cost is about US$1.0 million,
aims to (a) strengthen the capacity of the Ministry of Health to plan and
manage FP programs; (b) upgrade and expand FP services in all Gambian PHC
villages; and (c) improve FP and MCH data collection, analysis and
utilization at all levels. FP activities under this project would be
supported by an intensive IEC campaign, which is the object of a separate
UNFPA-financed project.  The executing agency for the UNFPA project is the
Ministry of Health, in collaboration with GFPA and SCF. The FP sub-
component would complement the above activities by further expanding the
Ministry's and GFPA's contraceptive distribution (CBD) network to ensure
the largest possible coverage. Emphasis would be placed on involving
private pharmacists and drug dispensers in the promotion of FP services.
GFPA currently operates a fairly successful community-based distribution
(CBD) system at 140 sites throughout the country. The WID project would
assist GFPA in expanding its network of CBDs in the Greater Banjul area.
The population of this area has grown at an estimated 10 per annum during
the past 15 years and now accounts for almost 30Z of the country's
population, while public services, particularly in the social sectors, have
lagged behind. It is proposed to establish 20 new CBD sites in the urban
area as part of the FP sub-component. In addition, 40 CBDs would be
established in rural non-PHC villages within the catchment area of health
centers, to be run by the Ministry of Health, using the TBAs as CBD agents.
The project would support the training and supervision of GFPA's CBD agents
and of Government TBAs. The training would be oriented specifically to the
tasks of motivating potential clients, screening, referring, and
identifying important side effects. As an incentive for the TBAs, it is
proposed to follow GFPA's practice of allowing the CBD agent to charge a
small fixed fee fpr contraceptives.
31.      During preparation of the WID project, close collaboration has
been established between UNFPA and IDA. The Government is being strongly
encouraged to merge the two donors' interventions into a national FP
program and to begin establishing program targets defined in terms of



ANNEX 3-3
- 90 -                       Page 10 of 11
additional health facilities to provide FP services, the number of agents
to be trained in FP, and new acceptors and users of FP services. An FP
adviser to the Ministry of Health, expected to be appointed in the course
of 1990, will assist in this task. Furthermore, the Ministry and GFPA have
been urged to jointly undertake FP activities. Under the WID project, GFPA
and the Ministry of Health would be asked to sign a protocol specifying in
detail those activities that they agree to undertake jointly. As mentioned
in para 7, UNFPA and IDA will finance a contraceptive prevalence survey
that should provide important information to feed into the FP program, in
particular the data needed for a realistic target-setting exercise.
32.       To assist GFPA and the Ministry of Health in implementing the FP
program, a workshop would be organized in Banjul, possibly in the summer of
1990, to prepare an action plan for the FP program, discuss implementation
issues, identify training needs, define the activities that GFPA and the
Ministry of Health would undertake jointly, and set program targets.
Similar workshops would be organized periodically during the life of the
project, possibly with the presence of the two donors, to monitor program
progress and resolve any implementation problems that may arise.
33.       UNFPA has supported FP educational activities for several years
in The Gambia.  In collaboration with UJNESCO, it recently prepared a
substantial multi-media IEC program, which is expected to begin to be
implemented at the end of 1990 and to continue for three years. The WID
project would complement UNFPA's IEC effort by financing participatory FP
educational activities, using the same approach (role playing, problem
dramas, etc.) as in the nutrition educational prograa (para 26). the
educational activities would. in particular, address the issues of male
attitudes towards FP and the impact of religious beliefs on FP practices.
Research undertaken as part of the preparation of the PP sub-component
identified those two issues as having a considerably negative impact olu the
acceptance and adoption of FP. The project would also support family life
educational activities, particularly among rural youth to sensitize them to
the country's population issues and help them understand the importance of
having only as many children as can be provided with adequate care,
nutrition, clothing and education.
34.       An important feature of the FP sub-component would be the role
assigned to the private sector -- pharmacists and drug dispensers working
in pharmacies -- in motivating the public to adopt family planning and in
ensuring the safe and effective use of contraceptives. Such a strategy has
proved effective in other African countries, provided it includes
sufficient incentives such as enabling pharmacists to have easy access to
contraceptive supplies, providing subsidies and packaging difrerent from
that envisaged for the public sector, and the Government publicizing the
availability of contraceptives at pharmacies. A nationwide survey by GFPA
would be conducted under the project to determine private-sector
contraceptive distribution channels, types of contraceptives available
through these channels, and other information needed to improve and expand
the private sector's involvement. Results of the survey would be used by
GFPA to establish guidelines for the private sector's involvement. the



- 91 -                           ANNEX 3-3
Page 11 of 11
project proposes to finance (a) the training of 100 drug dispensers in
motivating potential clients, referring, and identifying dangerous side
effects; and (b) the organization of one-day annual meeting with
pharmacists to discuss their involvement in the FP program.
35.       The Ministry of Health has requested that IDA finance under the
WID project a study on infertility in The Gambia. Although not a matter of
priority, infertility is an important women's health concern and as such
deserves to be included in the project. Furthermore, there is some
evidence that infertility is a problem in The Gambia. The study would be
conducted by local specialists, assisted by an expatriate, and would aim to
determine the prevalence of infertility among Gambian women and its causes.
The findings of the study would be discussed in a three-day national
conference with public officials (including a WHO expert) and professionals
from a variety of backgrounds. An action plan for implementing the study's
recommendations would be drawn up during the conference.
36.       The FP sub-component would finance: the purchase of vehicles for
supervision activities, fuel and materials; the training and supervision of
TBAs and GFPA's CBD agents; the training of drug dispensers and
pharmacists; FP and family life educational activities; the national survey
on PP delivery by the private sector; and the infertility study. UNFPA
would provide the contraceptives to be distributed under the project.
37.       Funds have been provided under the Project Preparation Facility
(PPF) for the following Safe Motherhood activities: (a) the design of the
TBA evaluation study and the carrying out of the study: and (b) the
contraceptive prevalence survey.

REPUBLIC OF ThE GAMBIA
WOMEN IN DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
INFORM&TION. EDUCATION AND COMMUNICATION (IEC) COMPONENT
A.   Background
1.        The general goals of this component are to create awareness
about the concerns and needs of Gambian women and their role in socio-
economic development, and to identify, mobilize and utilize the
communication resources accessible to women in the promotion of project
goals. Specifically, the IEC component aims to:
(a) Create awareness and initiate dialogue among policy-makers and
*gatekeepersZ as to the needs and status of women;
(b) Provide communication support for the various field activities
of the line Ministries;
(c) Provide infrastructure support for the production and broadcast
agencies to make them more responsive to the country's
development needs;
(d) Establish a communication model in 30 villages to enhance
learning as well as accelerate improvement in the status of
women in general;
(e) Provide research support for all communication activities to
ensure quality production and efficient dissemination and to
enhance learning;
(f) Provide a feedback mechanism to enable popular participation in
the formulation, design and implementation of communication
activities through the use of traditional as well as modern
communication media, and to serve as a source of information for
policy-makers; and
(g) Train a cadre of professionals in all the concerned
Ministries so that they become familiar with the processes of
communication and can effectively plan, implement and evaluate
communication activities.
2.        The IEC component of the WID project would provide overall
communication support to each of the other components -- agriculture, safe
motherhood and family planning, and skills development -- as well as
generally promote the status of Gambiar women. The support would include:



- 93 -
Page 2 of 7
the training of front-line workers in communication techniquest the
production and dissemination of materials: qualitativs as well as
quantitative research in communication issues related to project
implementation; and infrastructure development necessary for ensuring
sustainability.
3.        The IEC cor-onent would rely on four public sector institutions
for implementation, and therefore consists of four sub-components. The
lead agency would be the Women's Bureau, located in The Office of the
President. The Bureau would be responsible for coordinating all IEC
activities, both centrally and in the field. Much of the shared resources
of the project would be managed by the Bureau in collaboration with the
concerned line Ministries. The units responsible for IEC within each
sector would be the Agricultural Communication Unit (ACU) of the Ministry
of Agriculture for the agricultural component; the Health Education Unit
(HEU) of the Ministry of Health for the safe motherhood and FP component;
and the Nonformal Education Department of the Ministry of Education for the
skills development component.
4.        This component requires a great deal of coordination, as its
management will be complex. At all three levels of ac:'.on -- the central
level in Banjul, the Divisional level and the village level, -- decisions
on priority programs will have to be taken as to what is logical and most
effective and what best meets the needs of the target population, rural
women.
B.   The Women's Bureau and Central Coordination
5.        Central coordination responsibility for this component would
rest with the IEC Unit of the Women's Bureau. It would have technical and
technological coordination duties, and would supervise the overall
technical progress of the IEC component, coordinate the scheduling of
campaigns and media production for the various sub-components, and assist
in the formulation of messages and delivery of media content. The IEC Unit
would also assist the Project Management Unit (PHU) in ordering and
installing goods purchased for this component.
6.        As part of the infrastructure development related to IEC, the
Women's Bureau would engage the services of two expatriate experts, one of
whom would serve as the Coordinator of this component for a period of three
years. He would be assisted by a national counterpart, whom he would train
and who would receive further training opportunities under the project. The
coordinator would be recruited prior to the start of the project to assist
with preparing a detailed IEC plan and also assist in procurement. Another
technical specialist would be recruited for a two-year period during the
second year of the project as a maintenance speciElist to ensure adequate
maintenance and repair of the electronic equipment to be purchased for this
component. He would also provide training for local counterparts.

7.        The Women's Bureau would initiate an innovative IEC activity by
providing solar-powered VCR and monitors to 30 project villages. These
video halls would be placed in the larger towns and villages and would
serve as a focus for educational activities. Educational video programs
prepared by the Film Unit for each project component would be screened at
the video halls on a regular weekly basis. Field personnel from the
concerned Ministries would schedule their visits to coincide with showings
of their material so that they may respond to questions or initiate
discussion.
8.        The video halls would be placed in the care of organized women's
groups in each village. Commercial videos could also be aired, for a
charge, by the women's associations, with the income used for maintenance,
rental of commercial videotapes, or other minimal recurrent expenditures.
Repair of the equipment would be assured through the Film Unit and the
maintenance specialist recruited under the project. It is anticipated that
10 video halls will be established in each of project yzars two, three and
four, thereby allowing time for production of an adequate number of
videotapes, preparation of baseline data for a longitudinal survey (para
10), selection of the project villages, pricing surveys, and commodity
procurement. It is important that the Government carefully consider which
video system (PAL, SECAM, etc.) in which it wishes to invest, in light of
any existing plan for the establishment of a TV broadcast system in the
future.
9.        The Women's Bureau would coordinate 200 radio-listening clubs,
which would serve all four sub-components (para 3). These clubs could be
supplemented with field workers on regular visits, who would provide
systematic feedback on the learning conditions. Radio programs would be
duplicated and made available to the villages, on their request, for
further individualized listening.
10.       The Women's Bureau would also coordinate print production
(posters, pamphlets, etc.) for all four sub-components, as well as all IEC-
related research. It would be responsible for a visual literacy survey
prior to the start of the project and other qualitative surveys at a later
date. One major research activity would be the regular qualitative surveys,
which would serve as a baseline for attitudes and behavior concerning
women; this,in turn, would aid in message development and in monitoring the
progress of improving women's status in general. The Bureau would also
assist the production agencies in pre-testing their products prior to mass
production and distribution, thereby ensuring their comprehensibility and
quality. The Bureau would also undertake a longitudinal survey to compare
the 30 'video hall' villages with control groups to determine what impact,
if any, easy access to media may have on a variety of educational, social,
economic and anthropological variables.
11.       In its training activities the Women's Bureau would manage four
IEC workshops, drawing from all the concerned Ministries. In addition, it
would host seminars and conferences where information would be shared and
an exchange of views and dialogue about women's roles would take place. One



- 95 -
ANNEX 3-4
Page 4 of 7
workshop would be held each year during the first four years of the
project. Apart from long-term technical assistance, the Bureau would
manage short-term technical assistance for the IEC component. Two person-
months of technical assistance would be available each year for the first
five years, totaling 10 person months. This technical assistance would be
in support of training and research activities. The IEC Coordinator and
counterpart would be part of a four-person team that would make a field
visit to Niger to view rural solar-videos in operation; this would take
place as part of project start-up.
12.       Production of IEC materials, with the exception of those for the
Agricultural Communication Unit (ACU), would be delegated to the various
production agencies in The Gambia. Print materials would be contracted with
the Book Production and Materials Resources Unit, video production with the
Film Unit (to be provided a vehicle for the purpose of filming in the
field). The Women's Bureau would coordinate the scheduling of production
and the dissemination of the products. The Bureau would also develop
contractual arrangements with all the production agencies to ensure the
timely preparation of materials, pre-testing of all materials, and quality
production and dissemination, and would also assist them with evaluation
procedures.
13.       Radio Gambia would assure radio support for all key project
activities. This support would include: preparation of radio programs,
production of single-issue series (e.g., women's health), radio spots in
support of campaigns, duplication of tapes for use by radio listening
clubs, and other services as deemed necessary. Radio Gambia would be
provided with the necessary technical training to undertake audience
surveys to assist in improving the quality of its programs. The Bureau
would assist Radio Gambia and the Project Management Unit (PHU) in the
purchase and installation of a transmitter during the first year of the
project, in order to extend Radio Gambia's signal to the whole country. The
Bureau would also assist Radio Gambia with refurbishing two of its radio
studios. The Bureau would make a contractual agreement with Radio Gambia
for production and air time and assist in carrying out regular audience
surveys.
14.       The Women's Bureau would be provided with basic communication
equipment in Banjul and also three 4-WD vehicles to give its staff the
flexibility needed to make frequent monitoring and supervision visits.
Spare parts for these vehicles would also be furnished. Of the three
vehicles, one would be for the use of the Women's Bureau Coordinator and
the counterpart, the second for the Film Unit to assist its staff with
field production, and the third vehicle would be for Radio Gambia, to
ensure both field production anrd maintenance of its transmitter facilities.
15.       In support of its field activities, necessary office equipment
and audio-visual equipment for the Women's Bureau in Banjul would be
provided. Included would be word-processing programs, desk-top publishing
programs to assist in publishing a newsletter and brochures, and

computerized research programs (such as SPSS) to assist with the survey
data. The staff would be trained in the use of the computerized equipment.
16.       While the Women's Bureau and Central Coordination sub-component
appears to have high investment costs, they are justified by the fact that
the bulk of the costs would be utilized to make Radio Gambia more efficient
and to extend its reach and improve production quality. The other large
budgetary item is technical assistance, which would be discontinued after
the third year of the project. This technical assistance is justifiable
when one considers the relative inexperience of the Women's Bureau.
17.       Recurrent costs for the sub-component are minimal and mainly
consist of support for the Coordinator's counterpart, vehicle operation and
maintenance, and costs associated with the production of materials. Since
the latter are always highest at the outset, they can also be considered as
investment. There are also some recurrent costs associated with the
monitoring and supervision of the IEC activities.
C.   The Agricultural Communication Unit (ACU)
18.       ACU has an important role in pursuance of the IEC goals. It has
a functioning educational system already in operation which includes studio
and production facilities for videotapes and simple print materials. ACU
has also two video vans, which are in the field 10 days every month. These
video vans would be refurbished through the provision of two video
projectors -- one for each van in the first year of the project -- as well
as new motors for each van, thereby avoiding replacement costs of the vans
themselves.
19.       ACU would support ongoing field activities by agricultural
extension workers, train them in the communication process, and provide
them with the materials needed to conduct extension training. Two workshops
are scheduled to be held exclusively for ACU; one would develop prototype
materials for the field workers, which would then be tested in a workshop
prior to duplication. ACU expects to produce 200 flip-charts (one for each
project village), booklets and cloth posters on a variety of agriculture-
related topics.
20.       ACU would also produce video programs in support of its field
activities and coordinate them with the Women's Bureau for viewing in the
30 project villages to be provided with video halls.
21.       One activity of the agricultural component is the savings
mobilization scheme. This would be supported as part of an annual social
mobilization campaign to recruit new savers, as well as maintenance
activities in project years three, four and five to ensure that the savers
already in the system will remain in it.
22.       One member of ACU would accompany the team making the sLudy tour
to observe solar-video systems in operation in Niger (para 11).

D.   The Health Education Unit (HEU)
23.       IEC support for the safe motherhood and FP component would be
managed by the Health Education Unit (HEU) of the Ministry of Health. Many
of the issues involved in communicating safe motherhood concepts have been
addressed by a variety of agencies, such as UNFPA and USAID. Hence, the
WID project would focus on activities not yet covered by other projects.
24.       The primary activity would be nutrition education.  Allocations
have been made for staff training, the preparation of materials (both
print and electronic), and field interpersonal activities. One workshop has
been budgeted for year two of the project; this would be a materials
development workshop, as well serve as a trainer for training activities.

25.       It is hoped that HEU will make maximum use of the video halls,
as well as the radio-listening clubs, for health education activities.
26.       In addition to the production of equipment, the Food and
Nutrition Unit would be provided with audio-cassette machines to allow its

27.       In addition to nutrition activities, ZEC for the safe motherhood
and FP component would provide materials for the training of Traditional
Birth Attendants (TEAs). Five nutrition brochures and a nutrition flip-
chart would be produced. Ten videotapes related to nutrition would also be
developed; they would focus on a variety of nutritional problems and can be
either dramatic or instructional in their content. It is expected that the
topics would include nutritional deficiency diseases, weaning practices and
infant nutrition, prenatal nutrition of mothers, oral rehydration therapy
and diarrheal diseases, pregnancy complications and safe delivery for TBA
training, and other topics as determined by a communication needs
assessment.
E.   The Nonformal Education (NFE) DePartment
